Technical Note  4 
 Technical Note 
 
   In this text we have phoneticized the names of the Tibetan masters following the phonetic system developed as standard for all FPMT texts and translations by the FPMT Education Department. Whenever a Tibetan word is used, it is transliterated using the transcription system of Turrell Wylie. When a Sanskrit word is used, it is transliterated without diacritical marks. Those who are interested in knowing more about the FPMT system of phonetics can contact the FPMT Education Department directly.  
 The translation of the verses from chapter 4 of the root text, Ornament for Clear 
Realizations (Abhisamayalamkara ), is extracted from Geshe Jampa Gyatsos 
commentary, A Short Explanation of the Meaning of the Words of  A Treatise of 
Instruction on the Perfection of Wisdom: Ornament for Clear Realizations, 
translated by Ven. Joan Nicell. These verses appear throughout this study guide in indented italicized type, together with the relevant verse numbers.    
Introduction  5 
 Introduction 
 
   
This manual is a study guide for the fourth chapter of Ornament for Clear Realizations  
(Abhisamayalamkara ) by Maitreya. It is intended to be used as a support and a 
reference guide for the study of the fourth chapter of this text, which is part of the 
FPMTs Basic Program. As such, it has been designed to accompany, supplement, and support an oral commentary given by a geshe or other qualified teacher.  
 This guide is based on the teachings of Ornament for Clear Realizations  given by 
Geshe Jampa Gyatso for the Masters Program at Istituto Lama Tzong Khapa in 
Pomaia, Italy. With surpassing kindness, patience, and wisdom Geshe-la conferred 
more than 700 hours of teachings on Ornament  and its commentaries over the 
course of two years (199899). Geshe-la primarily based his teachings on two commentaries to Ornament : Acharya Haribhadras Commentary Clarifying the Meaning 
and Gy
ältsab Jes Essential Explanation,  an explanation of Haribhadras text. In 
addition, Ocean of Sport  b y  J e t s ü n  C h ö k y i  G y ä l t s ä n ,  a  t e x t b o o k  w r i t e r  o f  S e r a  J e  
monastery, was also used as a supplement. After giving comprehensive teachings 
based on these commentaries, Geshe-la tirelessly composed and expounded his own 
commentary on Ornament . All of these teachings were translated into English by 
Thubten Sherab and transcribed by Ven. Joan Nicell; these transcriptions were used 
as a basis for preparing the material included in this guide. Other reference materials consulted include transcripts of review discussions among students led by the Masters Program teaching assistant, Jampa Gendun, and publications on related 
topics such as Meditation on Emptiness, A Study of Svatantrika , and so forth. 
 
Geshe Jampa Gyatso was born in Dham in north-central Tibet in early 1932. At the age of seven he became a monk, and at thirteen he left home to live and study at Sera Je Monastery outside Lhasa. It was at Sera that Geshe-la met Lama Thubten Yeshe, who would become one of his dearest friends. In 1959 when the Chinese occupation forced him to leave Tibet, he and Lama Yeshe joined a party of thirty-five 
to escape toward Bhutan and eventually India. After recovering from serious illness, 
Geshe-la continued his studies in Buxa and in 1967 joined the newly instituted Buddhist studies program at Sanskrit University in Varanasi, where he received his Acharya degree in 1970. Subsequently, he continued his studies at the Lower Tantric College and received his Lharampa Geshe degree, the highest monastic 
Introduction  6 
 philosophical degree awarded, in 1972. Geshe-la worked on a number of projects 
until he received a request from Lama Yeshe to travel to the West to teach. Since 1980, Geshe Jampa Gyatso has been the principal resident teacher and precious spiritual guide at Istituto Lama Tzong Khapa, the largest FPMT center in Italy.  
Purpose of this study guide 
At the beginning of the seven-year Masters Program, we were confronted with a 
scarcity of reference materials available in English on Ornament . For various reasons, 
Ornament  has not attracted much attention of modern Buddhist scholars and 
translators, and commentaries on Ornament  written in Tibetan remain inaccessible 
to the majority of us. A similar difficulty will likely arise when people in the FPMTs Basic Program embark on the study of the fourth chapter of Ornament . Therefore, 
this study guide has been prepared with the aim of making some of this information 
available to those students who are interested in gaining a more comprehensive 
understanding of Ornament . It is intended to supplement an oral explanation given 
by a qualified teacher and not as a stand-alone self-study manual.  
 
What is included in this study guide 
This study guide contains the translation of Maitreyas root text of chapter four of 
Ornament for Clear Realizations  and a summarized explanation of each topic within 
this chapter, based upon Geshe Jampa Gyatsos written and oral commentaries and Gyältsab Jes Essential Explanation . As and when it is applicable, we have included the 
formal definitions of terms as presented in Ocean of Sport , the monastic textbook of 
Sera Je and the standard commentary to Ornament  indicated for the FPMT Basic 
Program. These definitions are set apart in gray shaded boxes throughout this study 
guide for easy consultation. The verses of Maitreyas root text are indented and italicized together with the relevant verse numbers. 
 The topics covered in Ornament  are extremely vast, covering a wide range of 
topics that presuppose some knowledge of Buddhist philosophy. Traditionally in 
Tibetan monasteries, a number of texts are studied prior to embarking on the study 
of Ornament . Therefore, we have also included supplementary material on a few 
basic topics of Buddhism, such as the four noble truths and grounds and paths. In addition, because Ornament  is taught in terms of the Yogachara-Madhyamaka-
Svatantrika school, one of the two sub-schools within the Madhyamaka-Svatantrika 
Introduction  7 
 school, an overview of Svatantrika tenets and some of the important differences 
between Svatantrika and Prasangika, the highest school of tenets, are also included.   Also included in this guide are sample quizzes for the Basic Program, with answer 
keys; discussion topics; suggestions for meditations and reflections; and an English-Tibetan glossary. 
 In conclusion, Maitreyas Ornament  is often likened to a map. It clearly shows 
where we want to go and how to get there. Like any map, it needs to be studied and 
followed well. But, unlike the use we make of most maps, the motivation behind our 
studying and practicing Ornament  is not only to arrive at our destination, but also to 
learn how to skillfully show others the way as well. May we achieve our goal. 
History of Ornament for Clear Realizations   8 
 History of Ornament for Clear Realizations  
 
 
  
Background on the Perfection of Wisdom 
According to the Tibetan tradition, our teacher, Buddha Shakyamuni, demonstrated 
enlightenment at the age of thirty-five under the bodhi tree near the town of Gaya in India. It is said that he remained there for seven weeks before setting out for Deer Park in Sarnath, where he first turned the wheel of Dharma. From that time until he passed away forty-five years later, he traveled through the Ganges plain of northern India giving teachings in accordance with the various capacities and dispositions of his many listeners. With his passing, disagreements arose within the Buddhist community as to the interpretation and authenticity of some teachings. Due to the dispositions of his disciples at that time, the Hinayana vehicle prevailed and many of Buddhas teachings associated with the Mahayana were forced into temporary obscurity, virtually disappearing from the mainstream Buddhism of the time.  Some 400 years later, as prophesied by Buddha Shakyamuni, Nagarjuna recovered these lost teachings and devoted his energies to restoring and consolidating the doctrine of the Mahayana. It is said that Nagarjuna was taken to 
the land of the nagas, where he retrieved a number of Mahayana sutras such as the 
Perfection of Wisdom in 100,000 Stanzas , and brought them back to the world of 
human beings. With the re-emergence of the once-lost doctrines of the Mahayana, a 
large corpus of literature known as the Perfection of Wisdom sutras was gradually introduced into the Buddhist canon.   There are a number of sutras that comprise the body of literature called the Perfection of Wisdom. The principal sutras are the three mothers, upon which 
Ornament  is based. They are called mothers because the perfection of wisdom is the 
mother of all the buddhas. The three mothers, the three main Perfection of Wisdom 
sutras, are the extensive, middling, and brief. The extensive has 100,000 stanzas, the middling has 25,000 stanzas, and the brief has 8,000 stanzas.   
In the Perfection of Wisdom sutras, Buddha taught both the profound view of 
emptiness and the vast stages of the path to enlightenment. The profound view of emptiness is presented in an explicit way, while the vast stages of the path are 
History of Ornament for Clear Realizations   9 
 presented in a hidden manner. Since the Perfection of Wisdom sutras are difficult 
to understand, great masters  who were predicted by Buddha himself  appeared to explain their meaning after the Buddhas death. While Nagarjuna appeared in the 
world and illuminated the profound meaning  of the perfection of wisdom, Maitreya 
illuminated the hidden vast meaning  of the perfection of wisdom approximately 600 
years later by composing Ornament for Clear Realizations  with the assistance of 
Asanga. 
Origin of Ornament  
It is said that the Indian master Arya Asanga, with the intention of achieving a 
vision of Maitreya, meditated on compassion for twelve long years. When, frustrated by failure, he abandoned his endeavor and set out from his cave toward the nearest town, he was stopped by the sight of an old female dog, sick and dying, her left leg rotting and covered with maggots eating her flesh. Appalled, Asanga felt great compassion and wished to help free the dog from this suffering. He decided to transfer the maggots to a piece of his own flesh, cutting a piece from his own thigh, in order to preserve their lives. Then, in order to avoid hurting them with his fingers, he put out his tongue to lick the maggots off. As he neared the foul-smelling dog, he felt overwhelmed by revulsion and, closing his eyes, bent down to lick up the maggots. After failing to encounter the dog, he opened his eyes and saw radiant Maitreya standing before him. Upon seeing Maitreya, Asanga cried out, You have no compassion! I have been trying to see you for a long time, but you did not appear. Maitreya explained to Asanga that he had been with him throughout his retreat, but Asanga had been unable to see him due to his own karmic obscurations.   Maitreya used his power to take Asanga with him to Tushita pure land, where he instructed him in the Mahayana vehicle doctrines. When he returned to earth, Asanga brought back with him the five important texts of Maitreya, one of which 
was Ornament for Clear Realizations . The others are Ornament of the Mahayana Sutras 
(Mahayanasutralamkara) , Sublime Continuum of the Great Vehicle (Uttaratantra or 
Ratnagotravibhanga),  Discrimination between the Center and the Extremes 
(Madhyantavibhaga) , and Discrimination between Phenomena and Reality 
(Dharmadharmatavibhanga) . These are widely known as the five teachings of 
Maitreya
 1 and form part of the core curriculum studied in the Tibetan Buddhist 
monasteries. 
 Maitreya, the buddha of loving kindness, is generally depicted on Buddha 
Shakyamunis right side in the refuge merit field, representing the method side of practice; Manjushri sits on Buddha Shakyamunis left and represents the wisdom 
History of Ornament for Clear Realizations   10 
 side. Although he attained enlightenment before Buddha Shakyamuni, Maitreya 
manifested as one of his disciples to demonstrate how the bodhisattva path should be followed. Maitreya is prophesied as the fifth founding buddha of this world age. He will appear in this world long after Shakyamunis teachings have degenerated and disappeared, during a time of widespread immorality, war, famine, and extremely short lifespans. Prior to manifesting as the fifth founding buddha, he will manifest as a tall, handsome person of regal bearing and will teach love and patience. As a result of following Maitreyas example, sentient beings fortunes will gradually improve until they become ripe to follow a spiritual path. At this time, Maitreya will descend from Tushita pure land, his current abode, and reintroduce the Dharma to the world as the fifth founding buddha.
2 
Commentaries to Ornament  
Because Ornament for Clear Realizations  is a very condensed versified summary of the 
Perfection of Wisdom teachings, it is extremely difficult to comprehend fully without relying upon its commentaries. A total of twenty-one commentaries composed by Indian masters have been translated into Tibetan. Among these, 
Commentary Clarifying the Meaning  by the Indian scholar Haribhadra forms the 
nucleus of the curriculum in the study of Ornament  in Gelugpa monastic 
universities. Even this commentary is terse and difficult to understand. Therefore, the Tibetans composed further commentaries  Je Tsongkhapas Golden Rosary , 
Gyältsab Jes Essential Explanation , and Jetsün Chökyi Gyältsäns Ocean of Sport . The 
primary commentary that will be used in FPMTs Basic Program will be Ocean of 
Sport, written by Jetsün Chökyi Gyältsän, the textbook writer of Sera Je Monastery. 
 
 
Overview of Ornament for Clear Realizations  11 
 Overview of Ornament for Clear Realizations  
 
   The Perfection of Wisdom sutras were taught by Buddha Shakyamuni during what is known as the second turning of the wheel of Dharma. They explicitly and extensively present the topic of emptiness of true existence of all phenomena. In addition, they also set forth in a hidden manner the different clear realizations developed by a spiritual aspirant over the path to buddhahood. In order to clarify 
and make explicit these clear realizations, Maitreya composed his Ornament for Clear 
Realizations . Among the different commentaries on Ornament , those by the Indian 
master Haribhadra are the basis for studies in the Gelug tradition. Acharya Haribhadra commented on Ornament  from the point of view of Yogachara-
Svatantrika-Madhyamaka tenets, and some of his assertions differ from those in 
Prasangika-Madhyamaka, which is generally regarded as the highest among the Buddhist tenets systems. 
 Ornament for Clear Realizations  is composed entirely in verse and is divided into 
eight chapters, as well as an introduction and a conclusion. Each chapter presents 
one clear realization, which is illustrated by different number of topics. The topics help to enhance the understanding of each clear realization by describing different features such as cause, entity, and result. 
 A total of eight clear realizations are presented. The first three − exalted knower 
of all aspects, knower of paths, and knower of bases − are objects to be practiced and 
generated; the next four − complete aspects, peak, serial, and momentary 
applications − are the four practices to be engaged in; and the final clear realization 
− resultant truth body − is the final object of attainment. In this way, the entire 
bodhisattva path to buddhahood is clarified. However, the overall presentation is 
not a simple step-by-step description of the path from the initial point of entry till the attainment of the final result. Instead, the entire path to enlightenment is revealed within the presentation of each clear realization. 
Brief explanation of the title 
The full name of the text in Sanskrit is Abhisamayalamkara-nama-prajñaparamita-
upadesha-shastra . This is translated into English as A Treatise of Instruction on the 
Perfection of Wisdom Called Ornament for Clear Realizations.  
Overview of Ornament for Clear Realizations  12 
  A Buddhist treatise is described as possessing two qualities: curing and 
protecting. It helps to cure the disease of the afflictive emotions, and it protects sentient beings from the sufferings of cyclic existence in general, and those of the lower realms in particular. This text is known as an instruction because it is an instruction or a means for easily realizing the meaning of the Perfection of Wisdom sutras.  
 With respect to the word ornament, Ornament  itself is described as a clarifying 
ornament that clarifies the hidden meaning of the perfection of wisdom. There are 
four types of ornaments described in the commentaries: natural, beautifying, and clarifying ornaments and the ornament of joy. For example, a persons beautiful body is a natural ornament, which is further adorned with the beautifying ornaments of bracelets and so forth. When this is reflected in a clarifying ornament, such as a mirror, it generates joy in the mind of the person, the ornament of joy. Similarly, in this case, the natural ornament is the beautiful body of the mother-like Perfection of Wisdom sutras, which is adorned by the beautifying ornament of the eight clear realizations and seventy topics. When this is reflected in this treatise, 
Ornament for Clear Realizations, the clarifying ornament, it generates joy in the minds 
of the trainees, the ornament of joy. 
 The term clear realization refers to a path. A path is defined as an exalted knower conjoined with an uncontrived mind of renunciation.
3 It is a consciousness 
to which the name path has been given because just as an external path leads a 
person to his destination, a consciousness path leads a being to a higher level of 
spiritual realization. Ornament  presents the different clear realizations developed by a 
bodhisattva during his path as well as those possessed by a fully enlightened buddha. 
Overview of the eight clear realizations and seventy topics 
The eight clear realizations4 make up the eight chapters in Ornament . These are also 
named the eight categories. Each clear realization is presented by means of different 
numbers of topics making a total of seventy topics. The following are these eight categories, in order:   1. Exalted knower of all aspects  (10 topics)  2. Knower of paths    (11 topics)  3. Knower of bases    (9 topics)  4. Application in complete aspects  (11 topics) 
 5. Peak application    (8 topics) 
 6. Serial application    (13 topics) 
Overview of Ornament for Clear Realizations  13 
  7. Momentary application   (4 topics) 
 8. Resultant truth body   (4 topics)  The eight clear realizations are grouped into:  
1. the three objects to be practiced,  2. the four means for attaining them, and  3. the final result.  
  The three exalted knowers are the objects to be practiced and cultivated by the four applications. To meditate on the three exalted knowers refers to meditating on the aspects, or features, of the three exalted knowers. One way of meditating on the aspects is to generate the mind into the nature of the aspects. This is akin to meditating on compassion in order to generate the mind into the nature of compassion. It is not similar to the case of an eye consciousness perceiving a form, wherein the object, form, remains separate from the mind perceiving it. The exalted knower of all aspects is presented as the means that enables the buddhas to turn the wheel of Dharma and set forth teachings suited to the dispositions of the trainees. With the knower of paths, a bodhisattva superior will be able to guide trainees on their respective paths to enlightenment based on his own realizations. The knower of bases enables the hearer and solitary realizer superiors to pacify all suffering and achieve the peace of nirvana.  The four applications are the means for attaining these three exalted knowers; 
hence, they are the causes of the three exalted knowers. They are yogas, or practices 
in the continuum of a bodhisattva, that are a union of calm abiding and special insight meditating on the aspects of the exalted knowers. Such a union refers to a special wisdom that sees its object clearly and that is maintained on the basis of a concentrated mind.
5 The application in complete aspects cultivates all 173 aspects of 
the three exalted knowers in order to master them. Once mastery is achieved, one enters peak application. Next, serial application cultivates these aspects serially in order to attain steadiness with respect to them, and it gives rise to momentary application. This last application occurs in the instant just prior to the attainment of buddhahood and is the direct cause for enlightenment.  The final result of the applications is the resultant truth body  the final object of attainment. Resultant truth body is synonymous with buddha; by learning about its excellent qualities, one is moved to generate a strong aspiration to achieve it for the sake of benefiting others.  Following are descriptions of each of the eight clear realizations, together with the topics included under each one. 
Overview of Ornament for Clear Realizations  14 
 1. Exalted knower of all aspects 
The first chapter of Ornament  describes the exalted knower of all aspects, which is 
the omniscient mind of a buddha. It is a fully developed exalted wisdom that is able 
to know all aspects of the objects of knowledge. The manner in which it is presented is by way of the ten topics, beginning with Mahayana conventional mind generation. These ten topics serve to illustrate such an exalted knower and to help us develop an understanding of the omniscient mind. The ten topics are:  
1. Mahayana conventional mind generation (Skt. bodhichitta ). This is presented 
first, since it is only with the development of such a mind aspiring to enlightenment for the sake of all sentient beings that one enters the Mahayana path. 
2. Mahayana precepts. These are precepts (oral instructions) on the two truths, 
the four noble truths, the Three Jewels, effort, and so forth. 
3 .  M a h a y a n a  p a t h  o f  p r e p a r a t i o n .  T h i s  i s  t h e  s e c o n d  o f  t h e  f i v e  p a t h s  o f  a  
bodhisattva. 
4. Naturally abiding lineage, the basis of Mahayana achieving. In general, 
naturally abiding lineage is also called buddha-nature. It is possessed by all sentient beings and shows that all beings have the potential to achieve buddhahood. 
5. Observed objects of Mahayana achieving. All phenomena are presented as the 
observed objects of a bodhisattvas practice. 
6. Objects of intent. The final results for which a bodhisattva exerts effort to 
achieve. 
7. Achieving through armor. The bodhisattvas practice of the six perfections 
within each of the six perfections. 
8. Achieving through engagement. The bodhisattva engages with joyous effort in 
practices such as developing meditative absorptions, the six perfections, the four immeasurables, and so forth. 
9. Achieving through collections. This is mainly through the two vast collections 
of merits and wisdom. 
10. Achieving through definite issuance. A practice that definitely without doubt 
issues forth the exalted knower of all aspects. 
2. Knower of paths 
Knowers of paths are clear realizations found only in the continuum of Mahayana superiors  i.e., bodhisattva superiors and buddha superiors. They are of three types: those that know hearers paths, those that know solitary realizers paths, and those 
Overview of Ornament for Clear Realizations  15 
 that know Mahayana paths. A bodhisattva superior develops these knowers of paths 
in the manner of actually generating in his continuum the different realizations shared with hearers and solitary realizers, as well as those unique to the Mahayana. It is not just a matter of knowing the different realizations and attainments in a theoretical fashion. By equipping himself with such knowledge, a bodhisattva is able to guide sentient beings of different dispositions, according to their different inclinations, to their desired goals of a hearers or solitary realizers nirvana or a buddhas enlightenment. The knower of paths is illustrated by eleven topics: 
1. Limbs of a knower of paths. These are the causes, entity, and result of a 
knower of paths. 
2. Knower of paths that knows hearers paths. This is for the purpose of guiding 
sentient beings with an inclination for the hearers path to a hearers nirvana. The principal realization is a realization of the selflessness of persons. 
3. Knower of paths that knows solitary realizers paths. This is for the purpose of 
guiding sentient beings with inclination for the solitary realizers path to the solitary realizers nirvana. The principal realization in this case is the realization of the emptiness of subject and object being different substantial entities. 
4. Mahayana path of seeing. This is the first occasion when a bodhisattva gains a 
direct realization of the emptiness of true existence. 
5. Function of the Mahayana path of meditation. These are the beneficial 
qualities that are attained through cultivating the path of meditation. 
6. Mahayana path of meditation of belief. This is to have the conviction that the 
three mother sutras are the source of attaining ones own aim, others aim, and the aims of both. 
7. Benefits of the path of meditation of belief. The benefits are to receive praises, 
venerations, and laudations from the buddhas. 
8. Path of meditation of dedication. Dedication transforms ones own or others 
roots of virtue into a cause for complete enlightenment. 
9. Path of meditation of rejoicing. A bodhisattva rejoices in either his own or 
others roots of virtue. 
10. Path of meditation of achieving. This path establishes the imprint for 
achieving the final realization of a buddha. 
11. Completely pure path of meditation. This path establishes the imprint for 
achieving the final abandonment of a buddha. 
Overview of Ornament for Clear Realizations  16 
 3. Knower of bases 
A knower of bases is an exalted wisdom developed by all superiors. Bases refer to 
the four noble truths and their sixteen attributes. The principal realization in this case will be the wisdom directly realizing the selflessness of persons. Through this knower of bases, hearers and solitary realizers who seek the peace of nirvana are able to achieve the total pacification of all sufferings. This category is illustrated by nine topics: 
1. Knower of paths that does not abide in cyclic existence through knowledge. 
This knowledge  the wisdom realizing the selflessness of person  liberates 
one from cyclic existence. 
2. Knower of paths that does not abide in (solitary) peace through compassion. 
Due to great compassion, a bodhisattva does not dwell in the solitary peace of 
nirvana and does not disregard the sufferings of other sentient beings. 
3. Knower of bases that is distant from the effect mother. This knower of bases 
is devoid of great compassion and bound by the conception of true existence. Therefore, it is far from achieving the effect mother  the complete enlightenment of a buddha. 
4. Knower of bases that is close to the effect mother. Due to possessing great 
compassion and the wisdom directly realizing emptiness, this knower of bases is described as close to the effect mother. 
5. Knower of bases that is of the discordant class. This knower of bases is devoid 
of special method and wisdom and, thus, should not be cultivated by a bodhisattva. Hence, it is labeled as the discordant class. 
6. Knower of bases that is of the antidote class. Being conjoined with special 
method and wisdom, this knower of bases is to be cultivated by a bodhisattva. 
7. A bodhisattvas applications indicated in the case of a knower of bases. These 
applications of a bodhisattva cultivate the antidote to the perverse conceptions adhering to true existence. 
8. Equality of a bodhisattvas applications indicated in the case of a knower of 
bases. These are applications that stop adherence to true existence with respect to the entities of phenomena, their divisions, definitions, definienda, and so forth. 
9. Mahayana path of seeing. This presents the sixteen moments of the Mahayana 
path of seeing, which directly realizes the freedom from elaboration of true existence. 
Overview of Ornament for Clear Realizations  17 
 4. Application in the complete aspects 
The application in the complete aspects is a bodhisattvas meditation on 173 
different aspects of the three exalted knowers by way of a union of calm abiding and special insight. This is for the purpose of attaining mastery over these different aspects. It is illustrated by eleven topics. This is the subject of this study guide and will be presented in detail in the subsequent chapters. 
5. Peak application 
Peak application is attained when the bodhisattva has attained mastery over the 173 aspects of the three exalted knowers. This occurs on the Mahayana path of preparation. To attain mastery means to achieve the ability to meditate on all 173 aspects in the correct order without mistake in a single session. This application is illustrated by the following eight topics: 
1. Peak application of heat. Peak application on the heat level of the Mahayana 
path of preparation is presented along with the twelve signs that indicate the attainment of peak application. 
2. Peak application of peak. Peak application on the peak level is shown to have 
attained increase in merits. 
3. Peak application of tolerance. Peak application on the tolerance level has 
attained a stable wisdom realizing a complete similitude of the three exalted knowers and a stable method that does not forsake the welfare of sentient beings.  
4. Peak application of supreme mundane qualities. Peak application on the 
supreme mundane qualities level, the fourth level of the path of preparation, has attained a thorough stability of mind with respect to limitless meditative stabilizations, which ripens the capacity of the bodhisattva to generate the Mahayana path of seeing. 
5. Peak application of the path of seeing. This peak application acts as the direct 
antidote to the acquired conceptions of true existence and their seeds. These conceptions are presented in four categories: (1) conception of true existence of objects to be engaged in, (2) conception of true existence of objects to be withdrawn from, (3) conception of true existence of subjects apprehending substantial existents, and (4) conception of true existence of subjects apprehending imputed existents. 
6. Peak application of the path of meditation. Similarly, peak application of the 
path of meditation is presented along with the four types of innate conception of true existence that are abandoned by it. 
Overview of Ornament for Clear Realizations  18 
 7. Uninterrupted peak application. This is peak application in the last moment 
of the path of meditation, just prior to the attainment of buddhahood. It 
functions to directly produce an exalted knower of all aspects, the omniscient mind of a buddha. 
8. Perverse achievings to be eliminated as indicated here. The sixteen types of 
wrong conceptions that are to be eliminated are identified. They are all related to conceiving the two truths, ultimate truth and conventional truth, as not suitable to be contained in a single base. 
6. Serial application 
Serial application is the bodhisattvas practice to attain steadiness in relation to his meditation on the 173 aspects of the three exalted knowers. In order to do so, these aspects are meditated on serially. The wisdom that meditates serially on these aspects is conjoined with the bodhisattvas practices, of which thirteen of them are described here: 
16. Serial application of the six perfections: generosity, ethics, patience, effort, 
concentration, and wisdom. 
712. Serial application of the six recollections. These are recollection of the 
Buddha Jewel, Dharma Jewel, Sangha Jewel, ethics, generosity, and gods.  
13. Serial application of non-entitiness. This is the wisdom realizing emptiness 
of true existence. 
7. Momentary application 
When serial application is perfected, its result is momentary application. This application has attained steadiness with respect to the 173 aspects of the three exalted knowers. It exists only on the last moment of the path of meditation, which is also known as the end of ones continuum as a sentient being. Beyond this point, a bodhisattva has achieved enlightenment and is no longer a sentient being. This application can be conceptually divided into four, although these four are, in reality, one entity: 
1. Non-fully matured momentary application 2. Fully matured momentary application 3. Characterless momentary application 4. Non-dual momentary application 
Overview of Ornament for Clear Realizations  19 
 8. Resultant truth body 
The final result that is attained by the power of cultivating the 173 aspects of the 
three exalted knowers is the resultant truth body, or the buddha. When divided, there are: 
1. Nature body (Skt. svabhavikakaya ). The emptiness of true existence of the 
mind of the buddha. 
2. Enjoyment body (Skt. sambhogakaya ). The form body of the buddha that is 
perceivable only by superior bodhisattvas in a buddhas pure land. 
3. Emanation body (Skt. nirmanakaya ). The form body of the buddha that is 
emanated for the sake of guiding ordinary beings. 
4. Wisdom truth body (Skt. jñanakaya ). The enlightened mind of the buddha. As 
an auxiliary topic, the twenty-seven types of enlightened activities of a buddha are also presented here. 
Conclusion 
To summarize, Ornament  presents the final result of the Buddhist path, the resultant 
truth body, along with its excellent qualities. When one comes to see the marvelous qualities of a buddha, one is moved and develops the aspiration wishing to achieve the same buddhahood. However, buddhahood, or complete enlightenment, is not attained by merely wishing for it, nor can it be bestowed on us by another. It is 
necessary to cultivate the causes for it. Therefore, Ornament  describes the four 
applications: application in the complete aspects, peak application, serial 
application, and momentary application. If asked, what are the objects to be cultivated by these applications? The answer is the three exalted knowers: the exalted knower of all aspects, knower of paths, and knower of bases. In this way, through 
presenting the eight categories and seventy topics, Ornament  sets out the complete 
path of a bodhisattva and reveals the hidden teachings of the Perfection of Wisdom 
sutras.

Introduction to Key Buddhist Concepts   22 
 Introduction to Key Buddhist Concepts 
 
   When the students in Tibetan monasteries, such as the three great Gelugpa 
monasteries  Drepung, Ganden, and Sera  encounter Ornament for Clear 
Realizations  and its commentaries in their curriculum, they have already built up a 
background of knowledge that facilitates their study of Ornament . They will already 
have studied Collected Topics, an introductory study of logic and epistemology; 
Paths and Grounds, a presentation of the different levels of spiritual development; and the Presentation of Tenets, an overview of the four Indian Buddhist philosophical schools and their assertions, and so forth. 
 In contrast, most people who will be studying the fourth chapter of Ornament  in 
Western countries will most likely not be familiar with the technical terminology and concepts contained in Ornament . This can be a frustrating experience since the 
commentaries on Ornament  are written for an informed audience and not for 
beginners. Thus, this introduction to key Buddhist concepts is intended to provide 
some helpful information drawn from these other various introductory texts. This introduction is by no means comprehensive since this material is only meant as a 
supplement to aid the study of the fourth chapter of Ornament for Clear Realizations.  
Consequently, this presentation focuses on those topics that are relevant to the 
present study.  The following subjects are covered in this introduction: 
1. Paths and grounds 2. An overview of Svatantrika tenets 
Introduction to Key Buddhist Concepts   23 
 Paths and Grounds  
This section briefly presents the Mahayana paths and grounds. What is a path? To 
put it simply, a path is a realization, a consciousness. It is a clear realizer that is conjoined with, or accompanied by, renunciation. In particular, a Mahayana path is a clear realizer that is conjoined with both renunciation and bodhichitta. Therefore, in this context, a path is not a process or a method; it is a realization, a consciousness, an exalted wisdom.  There are five Mahayana paths: the Mahayana path of accumulation, path of preparation, path of seeing, path of meditation, and path of no-more-learning. These five paths are developments of consciousness over time. Before entering the path (i.e., entering the first Mahayana path of accumulation), we need to meditate on the small and medium scopes of the lam-rim (the graduated path to enlightenment)  perfect human rebirth, impermanence and death, the sufferings of cyclic existence, refuge, and karma  in order to achieve renunciation desiring freedom from cyclic existence. In addition, we must develop bodhichitta by means of one of two methods: meditating on the sevenfold cause and effect, or exchanging oneself with others. When we develop an uncontrived, or effortless, bodhichitta, we enter the Mahayana path.   Among the five Mahayana paths, the two Mahayana paths of accumulation and preparation are paths of an ordinary bodhisattva. They are called Mahayana mundane paths in that they are paths of a mundane, or ordinary, being who has not yet realized emptiness directly.
6 The last three Mahayana paths are called 
supramundane paths  two are paths of bodhisattva superiors (the Mahayana paths of seeing and meditation) and one is the path of a buddha superior (the Mahayana path of no-more-learning). 
Mahayana path of accumulation  
The Mahayana path of accumulation is the initial door of entry into the Mahayana. Prior to entering the Mahayana path, one develops renunciation, great compassion, and contrived bodhichitta. This contrived bodhichitta, the aspiration to attain buddhahood for the welfare of all sentient beings, arises through concerted, repeated effort. When this bodhichitta becomes uncontrived, arising spontaneously without effort, one enters the Mahayana path for the first time and achieves the Mahayana path of accumulation. On the Mahayana path of accumulation, the bodhisattva begins to accumulate the three countless great eons of merit necessary to attain buddhahood. This is why it is called path of accumulation.  
Introduction to Key Buddhist Concepts   24 
  On the Mahayana path of accumulation, the bodhisattva listens to teachings, 
reflects on them, and meditates on their meaning. He gains the wisdoms of hearing and reflecting on emptiness. Prior to entering the path, a sharp-facultied bodhisattva has already gained an inferential realization
7 of emptiness. A dull-facultied 
bodhisattva does not necessarily gain this level of understanding of emptiness prior to entering the path, but definitely realizes emptiness inferentially prior to entering the path of preparation.   There are three levels of the path of accumulation: small, middling, and great. On the small level of the path of accumulation, bodhichitta can still degenerate and the bodhisattva can fall to the Hinayana vehicle by giving up his aspiration for perfect complete enlightenment in order to seek a mere liberation from cyclic existence for himself. However, on the medium level of the path of accumulation, the bodhisattva has attained the gold-like mind of enlightenment, or bodhichitta, and his bodhichitta has been strengthened to the extent that it can no longer degenerate. 
Mahayana path of preparation 
The Mahayana path of preparation arises subsequent to the completion of the Mahayana path of accumulation, its cause. When the bodhisattva initially attains the union of calm abiding and special insight with emptiness as the object, he attains the Mahayana path of preparation. At this point, the bodhisattva realizes emptiness through the medium of a mental image, or meaning generality
8  that is, he does not 
yet realize emptiness directly. At this time, the bodhisattva attains a wisdom arisen from meditating. Over the course of the four levels of the path of preparation  heat, peak, tolerance, and supreme mundane qualities levels  the realization of emptiness becomes stronger and stronger until the meaning generality completely disappears and it becomes a direct realization, whereupon he enters the path of seeing.  
! The heat level is so called because at this time the bodhisattva generates an 
exalted wisdom that begins to burn the fuel of the knowledge obstructions. 
! The peak level is so called because at this point it is not possible for the roots of 
virtue in the continuum of this bodhisattva to be destroyed by wrong views. 
! The tolerance  level is so called because at this point the bodhisattva has passed 
beyond the sorrow of bad migrations in that he or she will definitely not fall into the lower realms. Although the bodhisattva on the peak level is already definite not to fall into the lower realms, he attains stability with regard to this only on 
Introduction to Key Buddhist Concepts   25 
 the tolerance level. According to Grounds and Paths , he has forbearance [i.e., 
tolerance] with respect to the sufferings of the lower realms.9  
! The supreme mundane qualities  level is so called because the mundane virtue 
existing on this level is supreme compared to others. Grounds and Paths  says: It is 
the last quality ( dharma ) of the mundane path. After this, the bodhisattva goes 
beyond the mundane level.10 
Mahayana path of seeing 
On the supreme mundane qualities level of the path of preparation, the meaning 
generality of emptiness slowly fades until the bodhisattva sees emptiness directly without the medium of a meaning generality. At this time, the bodhisattva simultaneously enters the Mahayana path of seeing and the first bodhisattva ground. 
This direct realization of emptiness occurs in what is known as meditative equipoise . 
Meditative equipoise is one of the two main divisions of the path of seeing, the 
other being subsequent attainment.  
 During meditative equipoise , the bodhisattva is single-pointedly meditating on 
emptiness. Only emptiness appears to that bodhisatttva; all conceptual thought has 
temporarily ceased, and no conventionalities appear. Subsequently, when the bodhisattva arises from this realization of emptiness, when conceptual thought 
begins again, he enters subsequent attainment . Meditative equipoise refers to a 
meditation that is directly realizing emptiness. It has two main parts  the 
uninterrupted path and the path of release. The uninterrupted path of the path of seeing is the direct antidote to the abandonments of the path of seeing, the objects to be abandoned thereon. In the next moment, the path of release arises. On the path of release, the mind continues to abide in the direct realization of emptiness; however, at this point, the bodhisattva has been released from the obstructions that have been abandoned. In summary, the uninterrupted path is the antidote to the objects of abandonment, and the path of release abides in that state of abandonment. These two comprise the meditative equipoise of the path of seeing.  According to Svatantrika-Madhyamaka, the objects to be abandoned on the path of seeing are the acquired afflictive and knowledge obstructions.
11 Afflictive 
obstructions are those that mainly obstruct the attainment of liberation, while knowledge obstructions mainly obstruct the attainment of buddhahood. Acquired refers to those obstructions that have been obtained through learning incorrect tenets. According to Svatantrika-Madhyamaka, there are 112 acquired afflictive obstructions and 108 acquired knowledge obstructions, all of which are abandoned by the Mahayana path of seeing. 
Introduction to Key Buddhist Concepts   26 
  Another way to view the meditative equipoise of the path of seeing is in terms of 
the sixteen moments  the eight forbearances, which correspond to the uninterrupted path, and the eight knowledges, which correspond to the path of release. The eight forbearances  the four Dharma forbearances and the four subsequent forbearances  are the uninterrupted path related to objects and subjects. The four Dharma forbearances realize the emptiness of the four noble truths, the objects, while the four subsequent forbearances realize the emptiness of the Dharma forbearances themselves, i.e., the subjects, or realizing consciousnesses. Thus, the uninterrupted path of seeing realizes the emptiness of the four noble truths as well as the emptiness of these four realizations. The eight knowledges are the path of release that follows from the eight forbearances. According to the Madhyamaka school, all eight forbearances occur simultaneously on the uninterrupted path, and all eight knowledges occur simultaneously on the path of release.
12

Introduction to Key Buddhist Concepts   27 
  Upon arising from the meditative equipoise of the path of seeing, the bodhisattva 
abides in the subsequent attainment of the path of seeing. During this time, he engages in various activities such as the practice of the six perfections, thereby accumulating merit and wisdom. Then the bodhisattva enters meditative equipoise on emptiness once again. When this bodhisattva develops the uninterrupted path that is the antidote to the coarsest level of innate obstructions, he enters the Mahayana path of meditation.  
Mahayana path of meditation 
The bodhisattva attains the path of meditation when he begins to abandon the 
innate obstructions. At this time, while remaining on the uninterrupted path, the 
bodhisattva has entered the path of meditation but is still on the first bodhisattva ground. When he reaches the path of release, this bodhisattva enters the second bodhisattva ground. Thus, the first bodhisattva ground begins on the uninterrupted path of the Mahayana path of seeing and continues through the initial uninterrupted path of the Mahayana path of meditation.   Over the course of the Mahayana path of meditation, the innate afflictive and knowledge obstructions are gradually removed. Innate obstructions are obstructions that arise from latencies carried in the mental continuum since beginningless time and are not due to the effects of learning wrong tenets. The coarsest obstructions are abandoned first, followed by the subtler obstructions. This is likened to washing clothes  the coarsest stains are removed with the first wash; then, with continued scrubbing one removes the subtler stains. Throughout the Mahayana path of meditation, the bodhisattva progressively removes the innate afflictive and knowledge obstructions by familiarizing himself with the direct realization of emptiness and practicing the ten perfections; thus he creates the causes to achieve buddhahood.  According to Svatantrika-Madhyamaka, the innate obstructions are divided into great, middling, and small, each of which in turn is divided into great, middling, and small. In addition, the great of the great obstructions are divided into two  coarse and subtle. Thus, there are ten levels of innate obstructions that are abandoned on the ten grounds. 
 
Introduction to Key Buddhist Concepts   28 
 Chart 0.3  Innate obstructions abandoned by the grounds over the path of meditation13 
Obstructions Abandoned by  
 Great  Coarse 
Subtle First ground  Second ground

 Great  Eighth ground 
Small Medium  Ninth ground 
 Small  Tenth ground 
    
 
  The bodhisattva always advances from ground to ground during meditative equipoise on emptiness. The bodhisattva generates the antidote to a particular level of obstructions; this antidote is an uninterrupted path. When the bodhisattva has been released from those obstructions, i.e., attains a path of release, the bodhisattva graduates to the next ground. For example, a bodhisattva generates an uninterrupted path that is the antidote to the great of the great coarse obstructions; at this time, he is on the first ground. When this bodhisattva attains the path of release, having abandoned the great of the great coarse obstructions, he attains the second ground. With the exception of the first ground, all of the other grounds begin with a path of release. The first ground begins with an uninterrupted path  the uninterrupted path of the path of seeing which is the initial direct realization of emptiness.  On each of the ten grounds the bodhisattva attains the surpassing practice of one of the ten perfections, as shown in Chart 0.4 below. For example, the first ground bodhisattva attains the surpassing practice of the perfection of generosity. Although 
it is said that a bodhisattva attains the surpassing practice  of the ten perfections on the 
ten bodhisattva grounds, the actual  ten perfections are not achieved until 
buddhahood. In other words, they are not actually perfected until buddhahood. 
Introduction to Key Buddhist Concepts   29 
  
Chart 0.4  The ten grounds and the ten perfections 
 Ground Perfection 
1 Very Joyous Generosity 
2 Stainless Ethics 
3 Luminous Patience 
4 Radiant Joyous effort 
5 Difficult to Overcome Concentration 
6 Manifesting Wisdom 
7 Gone Afar Skillful means 
8 Immovable Prayer 
9 Gone Beyond Power 
10 Cloud of Doctrine Exalted wisdom 
 
 
 When the bodhisattva generates the uninterrupted path that is the antidote to 
the subtlest obstructions, he has reached the end of the continuum as a sentient being. In the next moment, a path of release and the Mahayana path of no-more-learning are attained simultaneously. 
Mahayana path of no-more-learning 
The Mahayana path of no-more-learning is the final result, the omniscient mind of a buddha. When the bodhisattva reaches the Mahayana path of no-more-learning, he becomes a buddha superior, a perfectly and completely enlightened being. At this time, he simultaneously and unmistakenly realizes all phenomena of the past, present, and future. While he constantly abides in meditative equipoise on emptiness, he simultaneously perceives the two truths, conventional truth and ultimate truth. A buddha has eliminated all obstructions forever in that he has completely abandoned all types of ignorance and afflictive emotions such that they will never arise again. He has finally attained the state in which he can be of maximum benefit to sentient beings, his purpose for becoming a buddha. 
 According to Jeffrey Hopkins in Meditation on Emptiness : 
While the yogi is in deep contemplation on only emptiness (at the end of the 
tenth ground), he passes into a condition wherein, simultaneous with continuous direct cognition of emptiness, all the phenomena that are 
Introduction to Key Buddhist Concepts   30 
 qualified by emptiness appear to him. He passes to the final path of release, 
and while in a meditative equipoise in which there is not the slightest interference of duality, this same consciousness directly cognizes all the many varieties of objects of knowledge as clearly as one sees an olive in the palm of ones hand. Since the potencies of mistaken dualistic appearance have been eradicated, the yogi attains the divine eye that has no impediments with respect to objects of knowledge and is not impeded from seeing one object by seeing another. He has exhaustively eliminated the knowledge obstructions, has actualized the limit of reality, has actualized the Truth Body, and has become thoroughly and completely enlightened with respect to all phenomena.
14  
 At this time, the yogi also attains the four bodies of a buddha  the wisdom truth 
body ( jñanakaya ) and the nature body ( svabhavikakaya) , which are the two truth 
bodies, and the complete enjoyment body ( sambhogakaya) and the emanation body 
(nirmanakaya) , which are the two form bodies. By means of the four bodies, a 
buddha attains both his own and others purposes. From the wisdom truth body, or 
a buddhas omniscient mind, the complete enjoyment body arises in order to give teachings to bodhisattva superiors in a pure buddha realm. In addition, a buddha manifests countless emanation bodies in all conceivable forms in countless worlds according to the needs of sentient beings.
15  A buddha spontaneously and effortlessly 
performs the welfare of sentient beings without straying from the wisdom realizing the final nature of phenomena.  As stated by Jeffrey Hopkins, Responding to sentient beings needs throughout time and space, emanation bodies appear, perform their task without effort, and are withdrawn. A sentient beings noticing or not noticing them as such depends on his fortune, which is formed through the potencies established on the mind by his virtuous and non-virtuous deeds. As long as space exists, the various activities of a buddha, arising from great compassion, come into existence spontaneously and continuously.
16

Introduction to Key Buddhist Concepts   32 
 Tenets of Yogachara-Svatantrika-Madhyamaka 
Introduction 
The Svatantrika-Madhyamaka is one of the two subdivisions of the Madhyamaka 
school founded by Arya Nagarjuna. A Svatantrika-Madhyamika is defined as a 
Madhyamika who, by means of positing an autonomous sign,17 does not accept true existence, 
even conventionally .18 Furthermore, there are two subdivisions of the Svatantrika, 
namely the Sautrantika-Svatantrika and the Yogachara-Svatantrika. In this 
presentation, only the Yogachara-Svatantrika tenet is emphasized because the study 
of Ornament  is mainly based on the commentaries of the Indian master, Haribhadra, 
who is considered to be a follower of Yogachara-Svatantrika. 
 A Yogachara-Svatantrika-Madhyamika is defined as a Madhyamika who propounds a 
presentation of conventionalities for the most part in accordance with Chittamatra tenets19 
(which is also called Yogachara, hence the name Yogachara-Svatantrika). One of the 
main assertions of the Chittamatra is the non-existence of external objects. Object and subject are not different substantial entities because they arise simultaneously from the activation of a single latency stored in the consciousness. This assertion is also accepted by the Yogachara-Svatantrika but not by the Sautrantika-Svatantrika. Therefore, the term Yogachara in the name serves to indicate an agreement with at least some of the assertions of Chittamatra tenets. Indian Buddhist masters who propound Yogachara-Svatantrika tenets include Shantarakshita, Kamalashila, and Haribhadra.  
 This presentation of the tenets of Yogachara-Svatantrika Madhyamaka includes:  
1. The three vehicles 
2. The afflictive and knowledge obstructions 3. The mode of asserting selflessness 4. A brief explanation of true existence
 
1. The Three Vehicles  
The three vehicles presented in the sutras are the hearer vehicle, the solitary realizer vehicle, and the bodhisattva vehicle (or the Mahayana). These are three different pathways for sentient beings of different inclinations to proceed to enlightenment. 
Hearers, literally hearer-listeners (Skt. shravaka ), are so called because they listen to 
teachings from others and, after attaining the fruit of their meditation, the 
Introduction to Key Buddhist Concepts   33 
 enlightenment of a hearer, they cause others to hear about that attainment. They 
proclaim, I have done what was to be done; I will not know another birth.  
 Solitary realizers (Skt. pratyekabuddha ), like hearers, belong to the Hinayana, but 
they surpass hearers in terms of their cultivation of wisdom and merit. They are able 
to generate the wisdom of a foe destroyer during their final lifetime in cyclic existence without depending on another masters teaching. They arise during the time when there are no buddhas or hearers in the world and actualize the enlightenment of solitary realizers. Due to their former prayers, they are able to teach sentient beings by means of physical gestures through demonstrations of miraculous powers and so forth without using speech, because they do not wish to disturb their peace of mind with verbal communication.  Bodhisattvas are followers of the Mahayana. Prior to entering the path, they generate intense compassion for all suffering sentient beings and the altruistic mind 
of enlightenment − the desire to attain perfect, complete enlightenment for the sake 
of all beings. When this mind of enlightenment arises spontaneously and 
effortlessly, they become bodhisattvas, also known as conquerors children. Bodhisattvas engage in the practice of six perfections and the four means of assembling trainees, progress through the ten bodhisattva grounds, and achieve buddhahood in order to be able to free all beings from their sufferings.   Although three vehicles are posited, a Svatantrika-Madhyamika asserts the view of one final vehicle  that is, that all sentient beings will eventually enter the Mahayana, travel the bodhisattvas path, and achieve the state of a perfect, complete buddha. The hearer and solitary realizer vehicles are viewed as skillful means to guide some sentient beings who are temporarily unable to practice the Mahayana path. Even hearer and solitary realizer foe destroyers, who have attained the final path of their vehicles, will eventually be urged by the buddhas to enter the 
Mahayana path to enlightenment. 
 According to Yogachara-Svatantrika Madhyamikas, the practitioners of these three vehicles differ in terms of their main objects to be abandoned and their main objects of meditation.  Holders of the hearer lineage, i.e., those who are predisposed to practice the hearer vehicle, take as their main object of abandonment the conception of a self-sufficient, substantially existent person, and as the antidote to this, take as their main object of meditation the view realizing the emptiness of a self-sufficient, substantially existent person. By these means, they eventually attain a hearers enlightenment.  Holders of the solitary realizer lineage take as their main object of abandonment the conception of subject and object being different substantial entities, and take as 
Introduction to Key Buddhist Concepts   34 
 their main object of meditation the view realizing the emptiness of subject and 
object being different substantial entities. By these means, they attain a solitary realizers enlightenment.  Bodhisattvas take as their main object of abandonment the conception of true existence, and take as their main object of meditation the view realizing the non-true existence of all phenomena. By these means, they attain great enlightenment, the state of a perfect, complete buddha. 
2. The Afflictive and Knowledge Obstructions 
Two types of obstructions are posited by the Yogachara-Svatantrika, namely, afflictive 
obstructions and knowledge obstructions. Afflictive obstructions are obstructions 
that mainly hinder the attainment of liberation, while knowledge obstructions are those that mainly hinder the attainment of omniscience, the exalted knower of all aspects.   Afflictive obstructions are the afflictions such as anger and attachment along with their seeds. Chief among these afflictions is the ignorance conceiving a self of persons, i.e., holding the person to be a self-sufficient, substantially existent self. This ignorance is identified as the root of cyclic existence and is the first of the twelve links of dependent arising, which describe the process whereby a being is compelled to take repeated rebirths in samsara due to the force of contaminated actions and afflictions. Hence, it is through eliminating such ignorance that one is liberated from cyclic existence. The antidote to be cultivated is the wisdom directly realizing the selflessness of persons, which realizes the non-existence of a self-sufficient, substantially existent self. In Yogachara-Svatantrika, a hearer foe destroyer has directly realized this selflessness of persons, through which all the afflictions and their seeds have been abandoned; thus, he is liberated from cyclic existence.  Knowledge obstructions obstruct the attainment of the exalted wisdom of all aspects of a buddha. It is not an obstruction to liberation and, hence, is not abandoned when a being attains the nirvana, or liberation, of a hearer foe destroyer. Yogachara-Svatantrika asserts both coarse and subtle knowledge obstructions. A coarse knowledge obstruction is the conception of subject and object as being different substantial entities, and its seeds; this is the main object of abandonment for the attainment of solitary realizer foe destroyer. A subtle knowledge obstruction is identified as the conception of true existence, and its seeds; this type of obstruction prevents the attainment of the different marvelous abilities and qualities of a buddha, and is abandoned by the bodhisattva over the Mahayana paths of seeing and meditation. The term seeds mentioned refers to the potency of a former 
Introduction to Key Buddhist Concepts   35 
 affliction (or one of the two conceptions) that is able to give rise to a later affliction 
of similar type.  
Chart 0.6  Obstructions asserted by Yogachara-Svatantrika

3. The Mode of Asserting Selflessness 
Selflessness is a central theme of the Buddhas teaching. It is due to our 
misconception of a self that we experience suffering, and it is through our correctly realizing selflessness that we are liberated from suffering. Hence, the different Buddhist tenet systems have gone to great length in explaining the meaning of selflessness.  In the Mahayana, two types of selflessness are described: the selflessness of person and the selflessness of phenomena. Person refers to all sentient beings, while phenomena refers to all existents. Svatantrika-Madhyamikas hold that the Hinayana schools only present the selflessness of person, whereas the Mahayana schools also set forth the more profound and subtler selflessness of phenomena. A bodhisattva is superior to a Hinayana practitioner because he realizes both types of selflessness.  Yogachara-Svatantrika Madhyamikas assert a coarse selflessness of person that is a persons emptiness of being permanent, unitary, and independent, and a subtle selflessness of person that is a persons emptiness of being self-sufficient and substantially existent. They assert a coarse selflessness of phenomena that is the emptiness of subject and object being different substantial entities,
20 and a subtle 
selflessness of phenomena that is the emptiness of true existence of all phenomena.  The subtle selflessnesses of persons and phenomena are differentiated according to their objects of negation and not according to their bases of emptiness. This is because the refutation of the object of negation, true existence, upon any base is the subtle selflessness of phenomena, while the refutation of self-sufficient, substantial existence upon any base is the subtle selflessness of person. For example, the realization of oneself, a person, as empty of being a self-sufficient, substantially existent person is a realization of the selflessness of person, while the realization of 
Introduction to Key Buddhist Concepts   36 
 oneself, a person, as empty of true existence is a realization of the selflessness of 
phenomena. 
 
Chart 0.7  Self as asserted by Yogachara-Svatantrika
