4. A brief explanation of true existence  
The conception of true existence and its seeds are accepted by Yogachara-Svatantrika 
to be the knowledge obstructions, the main obstacle to the attainment of omniscience and buddhahood. This conception conceives all phenomena to be truly existent, which means that they exist without being posited through the force of the mind. Here, true existence, independent existence, and ultimate existence are equivalent hypothetical ways in which phenomena are wrongly conceived to exist. Though no phenomenon, in fact, exists in such a way, still, the conception of true existence holds strongly that phenomena do thus exist. Thus, the conception of true existence is a wrong consciousness. Being a wrong consciousness it can be removed from the mind through the power of its antidote, a consciousness with the opposite 
mode of apprehension  namely, the wisdom realizing phenomena as empty of true 
existence.   In order to develop such wisdom, it is essential to identify precisely the true existence that phenomena are empty of. Remembering that true existence is a hypothetical way of existing that is superimposed upon objects, the measure of true existence is as follows: existence of an object by way of its own uncommon objective mode of subsistence without being posited by the force of appearing to a non-defective awareness. A non-defective awareness is either a conceptual or non-conceptual consciousness that is non-mistaken. To ordinary beings, all phenomena appear to exist with their own objective mode of subsistence, seeming to exist exclusively from their own side without being dependent on a mind. This mistaken appearance is identified as the true existence of all phenomena.  Svatantrika asserts that the conventional mode of existence of an object is posited in dependence on two factors: (1) the objective mode of subsistence of the object, and (2) the appearance of this to a non-defective awareness. Both factors are necessary for positing the existence of the object. If one were to posit an object 
Introduction to Key Buddhist Concepts   37 
 merely by the force of awareness without depending on the objects own character, 
then that object would be unreal, a mere mental construction. Someone who propounds such a view has fallen into the extreme of annihilation. On the other hand, if the object were to be posited as having its own uncommon objective mode of subsistence even without appearing to a non-defective awareness, then such an object would ultimately exist. Anyone who propounds such a view has fallen into the extreme of permanence. 
 In order to assist in gaining a sense of its intended meaning a traditional analogy 
is presented. In ancient India it is said that a magician could take a pebble and a twig, apply a special salve, cast a mantra, and thereby cause the pebble and twig to appear as a horse and an elephant. In this scenario, we can imagine three different types of persons who attend this performance:
  
1. the audience present at the time the mantra was cast,  2. the magician, and  3. a latecomer whose eyes are unaffected by the mantra.  
Each of these persons will view the trick from a different perspective.  
The audience, whose eyes have been affected by the mantra, have both the appearance 
of a horse and an elephant as well as the conception believing them to be a real horse and 
elephant . That is, the audience, unaware that what appears to them is due to their 
minds having been affected by the mantra, has the impression that an actual horse 
and elephant have been led onto the stage. The horse and elephant appear to exist 
independent of their minds, and so the audience grasps this appearance as true, thinking, What a wonderful horse! I would love to ride it! or, I had better be careful. That elephant could easily kill me! thereby generating attachment and aversion. By the force of the appearance of the horse and the elephant and the belief in that appearance to be true, the audience wrongly posits the existence of a real horse and elephant.  The audience in the analogy represents ordinary people who have not realized emptiness. To them, all phenomena naturally appear to exist by way of their own mode of subsistence without being posited by the force of mind. Just as the audience believes that the mere appearance of a horse and an elephant are a real horse and elephant, similarly, ordinary beings, due to the influence of the conception of true existence, believe that the mere appearance of phenomena as independently existent to be the way that phenomena do, in fact, exist. The conception of true existence holds that phenomena exist in accordance with the way they appear. Thus, ordinary people have both the appearance of true existence and a belief in that appearance to be true. 
Introduction to Key Buddhist Concepts   38 
  The magician, whose eyes, like those of the audience, have also been affected by 
the mantra, has the appearance of a horse and an elephant but not the conception believing 
them to be a real horse and elephant . The magician is aware that the appearance of the 
horse and the elephant is due to his mind having been affected by the mantra. 
Though the horse and elephant appear to exist independently of his mind, he does not believe the appearance to be true. Therefore, unlike the audience, he does not go on to wrongly posit the existence of a real horse and elephant and, from that, does not generate attachment or aversion.  The magician of the analogy represents the person who has realized emptiness conceptually by way of a meaning generality, that is, through an idea or concept of what emptiness is as opposed to having realized emptiness directly. For such a person, phenomena still appear as they do for ordinary people, that is, as existing by way of their own objective mode of subsistence without being posited by the force of mind. But unlike ordinary people, due to the force of his realization of emptiness, such a person no longer believes this mistaken appearance to be true. He is aware that phenomena are posited by the force of their mode of subsistence appearing to his mind and, therefore, do not exist independently, though they appear so. Thus, such a person has the appearance of true existence but not the belief in that appearance as true.   The latecomer, whose eyes, unlike those of both the audience and the magician, 
have not been affected by the mantra, has neither the appearance of a horse and an 
elephant nor the conception believing them to be a real horse and elephant . This person 
represents a superior abiding in meditative equipoise who directly realizes emptiness. 
For such a person, all appearances of true existence are completely absent as well as any possibility of the conception believing in the truth of such deceptive appearances. This exalted wisdom directly realizes that phenomena do not exist exclusively from their own side, that is, that phenomena lack a mode of being not posited by the non-defective awareness to which they appear. Such a wisdom realizing emptiness is the direct antidote to the conception of true existence. 
Introduction to Chapter 4   39 
 Introduction to Chapter 4  
 
   
Application in the Complete Aspects 
Application in the complete aspects, the fourth of the eight clear realizations 
explained in Ornament , is the subject of chapter 4. Application in the complete 
aspects is a means by which one cultivates the three exalted knowers  the knower of 
bases, the knower of paths, and the exalted knower of all aspects  in order to attain buddhahood. Thus, application in the complete aspects is a means for attaining buddhahood.   In order to attain buddhahood, a bodhisattva must cultivate each of the three exalted knowers in his continuum. He must cultivate the knower of bases  an exalted wisdom that realizes all bases, i.e., the sixteen attributes of the four noble truths, as without a self of persons. He does so by meditating on the aspects, or features, of the knower of bases. He must also cultivate the knower of paths, an exalted wisdom realizing that all paths are empty of true existence. He does so by meditating on the aspects of the knower of paths. Finally, he must cultivate the exalted knower of all aspects  the omniscient mind of a buddha. He does so by meditating on the aspects of the exalted knower of all aspects. Thus, the bodhisattva generates the three exalted knowers in his continuum by meditating on the 27 aspects of the knower of bases, the 36 aspects of the knower of paths, and the 110 aspects of the exalted knower of all aspects. There are 173 aspects in all.  The 173 aspects are cultivated by means of the four applications application in the complete aspects, peak application, serial application, and momentary application. Application in the complete aspects, the subject of this study guide, is 
the means by which one familiarizes oneself with the 173 aspects in order to gain 
mastery over them. This is done by meditating on a compendium of the aspects with a union of calm abiding and special insight. The other three applications further develop and refine this wisdom until the bodhisattva is able to correctly meditate on all 173 aspects serially in the shortest moment of a complete action.
21  
 
Introduction to Chapter 4   40 
 The definition of an application in the complete aspects  is a bodhisattvas yoga conjoined 
with the wisdom cultivating a compendium of the aspects of the three exalted 
knowers. 22  
 
 What is an application? Its Tibetan name, sbyor ba  (pronounced jor wa), has a 
multitude of meanings such as connect, join, or prepare. In this case, application 
in the complete aspects is a method for joining to buddhahood or preparing for buddhahood.   Application in the complete aspects is (1) a training in which a bodhisattva cultivates a compendium of the 173 aspects, or (2) an exalted wisdom that is 
conjoined with, or accompanied by , this training. An example of the first is an exalted 
wisdom of a bodhisattva meditating on the sixteen attributes of the four noble 
truths. An example of the second is the great compassion in the continuum of a bodhisattva.  Application in the complete aspects initially arises on the Mahayana path of accumulation and continues through to the end of ones continuum as a sentient being. All bodhisattvas paths, clear realizations, and exalted wisdoms  these terms are all synonymous  are applications in the complete aspects.  
The Eleven Topics of Application in the Complete Aspects 
There are eleven topics that illustrate application in the complete aspects, and thus 
are the topics explained in chapter 4 of Ornament : 
1. Subject-aspects of the antidote class   These are the 173 aspects of the three 
exalted knowers. 
2. Principal applications   The principal applications engaged in by bodhisattvas 
are presented. 
3. Excellent qualities of Mahayana applications   Presents the benefits that are 
attained by cultivating the Mahayana applications that were presented in the 
second topic explained above. 
4. Hindrances to the applications   These are the pitfalls that can interfere with the 
production, abidance, or completion of an application. 
5. Characteristics of the applications   The characteristics assist one in 
understanding the principal applications. 
6. Mahayana partial concordance with liberation    T h i s  i s  t h e  M a h a y a n a  p a t h  o f  
accumulation, the initial path in the Mahayana vehicle, during which one practices an application in the complete aspects. The characteristics of the 
Introduction to Chapter 4   41 
 applications can only be generated in those trainees who have entered the 
Mahayana path of accumulation. 
7. Mahayana partial concordance with definite discrimination   This is the Mahayana 
path of preparation, the second Mahayana path. This topic specifically addresses the subsequent attainment period of the Mahayana path of preparation, which is subsequent to a meditative equipoise focusing on emptiness. 
8. Bodhisattva sangha who has attained a sign of irreversibility   This topic presents 
the signs that indicate to trainees that a bodhisattva is irreversible from complete enlightenment. 
9. Application in the equality of cyclic existence and peace   This presents the 
applications that give rise to the truth body of a buddha.  
10. Application in a pure land   This presents the applications that give rise to the 
complete enjoyment body of a buddha.  
11. Application in skillful means   This presents the applications that give rise to 
the emanation body of a buddha. These three applications are practiced on the three pure grounds (bodhisattva grounds eight, nine, and ten). 
  In short, chapter 4 first presents the 173 aspects that are to be cultivated in order to attain the three exalted knowers and ultimately buddhahood (topic 1). Following 
that, the principal applications of a bodhisattva are presented (topic 2). Then, the 
excellent qualities of the applications (topic 3) and the hindrances to practicing them (topic 4) are presented in order to inspire trainees to practice them and to help them avoid potential pitfalls. The characteristics of the applications (topic 5) are presented in order to help trainees understand the applications individual characteristics, enabling them to practice the applications appropriately. Following that, the Mahayana partial concordance with liberation (topic 6) and partial concordance with definite discrimination (topic 7), the initial paths on which application in the complete aspects is practiced, are presented. The signs of irreversibility (topic 8) first arise for sharp-facultied bodhisattvas on the Mahayana partial concordance with definite discrimination, so this topic is presented here. By the time the bodhisattva reaches the eighth bodhisattva ground, he has definitely attained the signs of irreversibility. At this time, the bodhisattva has entered the three pure grounds and is creating the causes for attaining the three bodies of a buddha through engaging in the application in the equality of cyclic existence and peace (topic 9), application in a pure land (topic 10), and application in skillful means (topic 11).  
Topic 1: Subject-Aspects of the Antidote Class 42 
 Topic 1: Subject-Aspects of the Antidote Class 
 
   
The first topic of the fourth chapter of Ornament for Clear Realizations  is the aspects 
to be meditated on or cultivated by a bodhisattva in order to attain enlightenment 
for the sake of all sentient beings. A total of 173 aspects will be presented.  An aspect is an attribute or a feature. In this context, aspects refer to the aspects of the three exalted knowers that are objects of meditation by an application in the complete aspects in order to gain mastery over them.  How are these aspects cultivated? In general, there are two ways to meditate on them:
23 
! Meditation in which an aspect is taken as the object of the mode of apprehension, that is, as the object of the meditative mind. For example, meditating on impermanence or emptiness. 
! Meditation in which the mind is generated in the actual entity of that aspect. For example, meditating on compassion, love, and so forth. 
  In regard to the 173 aspects presented in the fourth chapter, the bodhisattva can meditate on them in four ways.  
! Meditation by taking a belief to mind. Here, one is not able to generate the mind 
in the entity of the object meditated exactly as it is, but generates merely a similitude of it. This is the case with meditating on a similitude of the five powers on the path of accumulation, while the five powers are attained only on the path of preparation. 
! Meditation by generating the mind in the entity of the object meditated exactly as it is.  
The aspect that is the object cultivated is generated in the mental continuum just as it is. For example, meditating on the actual close placements of mindfulness on the path of accumulation. The close placements of mindfulness are actually attained on the path of accumulation. 
! Meditation by analyzing the character of an aspect.  The characters of higher 
qualities, which are objects of later attainments, are taken to mind, individually identifying them as this and that, and are analyzed with wisdom. For example, analyzing the uncommon aspects of an exalted knower 
of all aspects. 
Topic 1: Subject-Aspects of the Antidote Class 43 
 ! Meditation on an aspect of a wish.  This refers to meditating on an aspect by way 
of generating a wish or aspiration, thinking, May such and such states which 
are objects of attainment be attained! 
  Therefore, while on the path of learning, i.e., before attaining buddhahood, one can meditate on the exalted knower of all aspects. In cases where one is unable to generate the actual entity of the aspects or similitudes of them, one is still able to analyze their characters and qualities, as well as to generate the aspiration to attain such aspects in the future.   In general there are two types of aspects: (1) aspects that exist as the objects of realization, such as the four noble truths, and (2) aspects that exist as the realizers, i.e., the three exalted knowers. The former are known as object-aspects, and the latter as subject-aspects. For example, in the case of the knower of bases that realizes the impermanence of true sufferings, the impermanence of true sufferings is the object-aspect while the knower of bases itself is the subject-aspect. In other words, the object-aspect refers to the object being realized while the subject-aspect refers to the realizing awareness. In this chapter, aspects mainly refers to subject-aspects.  If someone were to hold the object-aspect and subject-aspect as two separate objects to be cultivated, that would be a mistake. This is because when one is familiarizing with the object-aspect, impermanence, simultaneously one is also familiarizing or cultivating the subject-aspect, the exalted wisdom realizing impermanence. And when one is cultivating the subject-aspect of the exalted wisdom 
realizing impermanence, one is simultaneously familiarizing with the aspect of 
impermanence. 
 
The definition of  subject-aspect of the antidote class indicated in this case  is an exalted 
knower that is able to quell its discordant class .24  
 
Boundaries : The subject-aspects of a knower of bases exist in the continua of all 
superiors. The subject-aspects of a knower of paths exist in the continua of 
Mahayana superiors. The subject-aspects of an exalted knower of all aspects exist 
only in the continua of buddha superiors. 
 
4.1 The instances of a knower of bases 
Known as aspects are the characteristics.  
Because there are three types of exalted knowers of all 
Topic 1: Subject-Aspects of the Antidote Class 44 
 They are asserted to be of three types. 
 
 Instances of the exalted wisdom knowing bases, bases being the sixteen attributes of the four noble truths, are known as the exalted wisdoms that quell the discordant class. The aspect that appears to this exalted wisdom is the very opposite of its discordant class. Such an exalted wisdom cultivated by a bodhisattva, with an aspect of any of the three exalted knowers appearing to it, is referred to as an antidote in this case.  Take as an example the exalted wisdom of a bodhisattva realizing the impermanence of the aggregates. It is an antidote to the wrong conception grasping the aggregates as permanent because such an exalted wisdom directly harms the wrong conception by means of the appearance of the aspect of impermanence, 
which is the opposite of the referent object (Tib. zhen yul ) of that wrong conception. 
The referent object of this conception is the permanent aggregates. In other words, 
the wrong conception of permanence is abandoned through realizing that the aggregates are, in reality, impermanent. This explanation should be applied to all the aspects cultivated.  When these aspects that are antidotes are condensed, they are of three types. This is because they are definitely aspects of the three types of exalted knowers of all  knower of bases, knower of paths and exalted knower of aspects  which are objects of cultivation. These 173 aspects are listed in chart 1.1. 
 
Having explained aspects in general, the detailed explanation of the aspects cultivated by a bodhisattva is presented in terms of: 
1. Aspects of a knower of bases 
2. Aspects of a knower of paths 3. Aspects of an exalted knower of all aspects

Topic 1: Subject-Aspects of the Antidote Class 46 
 1. Aspects of a knower of bases 
 
173 aspects of an exalted knower of all aspects 
1. 27 aspects of a knower of bases  
2. 36 aspects of a knower of paths 
3. 110 aspects of an exalted knower of all aspects 
• 37 aspects of an exalted knower of all aspects harmonious with hearers 
• 34 aspects of an exalted knower of all aspects harmonious with bodhisattvas 
• 39 uncommon aspects of an exalted knower of all aspects 
 
The definition of subject-aspect of a knower of bases  is an exalted knower in the 
continuum of a superior which is an aspect that (1) is an object of cultivation of a 
knower of bases and (2) abides in a type of Hinayana realization .25  
 
Boundaries:  It exists in the continua of all superiors. 
 
4.2 From the aspect of non-existence through 
 The aspect of unshakability,  They are explained as four with respect to each truth  And as fifteen with respect to paths.  
 
 There are twenty-seven aspects of a knower of bases in relation to the four noble truths. The first three truths  true sufferings, true origins, and true cessations  have four aspects each while the fourth, true paths, has fifteen aspects. These aspects are set out in the Perfection of Wisdom sutras saying, Bhagavan, this perfection of wisdom is the perfection of non-existence through this perfection of wisdom is the perfection of unshakability.   The meditation on these aspects is an ancillary practice belonging to the training 
of a being of middling scope, as presented in the Stages of the Path  (lam-rim  
literature). Through meditating on these aspects, one will see the faults of possessing 
the contaminated aggregates which are of the nature of impermanence, suffering and so forth, as well as the disadvantages of being afflicted by the three kinds of suffering. 
Topic 1: Subject-Aspects of the Antidote Class 47 
 Four aspects of true sufferings 
1. Impermanent . This is indicated by non-existence in the Perfection of Wisdom 
Sutra , since sufferings do not exist permanently. Sufferings arise and disintegrate 
momentarily, and therefore, they are impermanent. 
2. Miserable . This is indicated by not produced since sufferings are not produced 
ultimately. In particular, having taken on the contaminated aggregates of a 
human being by the force of actions and afflictions, one has to experience the sufferings of birth, aging, sickness, death, encountering suffering, separating from 
the happiness one has, not finding the happiness one seeks, and so forth. 
3. Empty . This is indicated by devoid of a self that is a different entity from the 
aggregates. This refers to the conception of a permanent, unitary, and 
independent self and that the aggregates are objects of use of such a self. 
4. Selfless . This is indicated by not under the control of a self of persons that is the 
same entity as the aggregates. This refers to the conception of a self-sufficient, substantially existent self and the conception that the aggregates are the objects of use of such a self. 
Four aspects of true origins 
5. Cause . The cause of suffering, craving, is non-abiding since it does not exist 
ultimately. Craving refers to the eighth link of the twelve links of dependent arising. 
6. Source .  C r a v i n g  i s  u l t i m a t e l y  l i k e   s p a c e .   J u s t  a s  s p a c e  i s  e m p t y ,  s o u r c e  i s  a l s o  
empty of ultimate existence.  
7. Strong producer . Craving is inexpressible as it is a cause that cannot be ultimately 
expressed by investigation and analysis. Investigation is a mental factor that engages its object roughly while analysis is a mental factor that engages its object in detail.  
8. Causal condition . Craving and feeling, which are conditions for repeated existence, 
are ultimately nameless. Craving and feeling are posited as conditions for repeated existence because due to feeling, one generates craving, which activates the karmic imprint stored on the consciousness that brings about repeated existence. 
Topic 1: Subject-Aspects of the Antidote Class 48 
 Four aspects of true cessations 
9. Cessation . Cessation is ultimately without progress. A true cessation is a 
freedom from stains that does not progress and does not disintegrate. 
10. Pacification . The pacification of sufferings is not appropriated by conditions. It 
is a pacification that cannot be taken away by anyone. 
11. Highly auspicious . The liberation that is highly auspicious is inexhaustible. Once 
this liberation is attained, it remains without exhaustion. 
12. Definitely liberating . The cessation that is a freedom from stains is unproduced. 
It is called unproduced because the state of cessation is permanent and is not 
produced by causes. 
Fifteen aspects of true paths  
These are divided into three categories: 
! Four aspects of true paths that are uncontaminated paths of seeing, which are antidotes to the afflictive obstructions. 
! Five aspects of true paths that are contaminated paths of meditation, which are antidotes to the knowledge obstructions. 
! Six aspects of true paths that are uncontaminated paths of seeing, which are antidotes to the knowledge obstructions. 
  The first four of these fifteen aspects are antidotes to afflictive obstructions while the remaining eleven are antidotes to knowledge obstructions.
26 In particular, these 
knowledge obstructions are coarse knowledge obstructions, such as the conception of forms and so forth as external objects  in other words, the conception of subject and object being different substantial entities. The subtle knowledge obstructions are the conceptions of true existence. 
Four aspects of true paths that are uncontaminated paths of seeing 
The four aspects of true paths that are uncontaminated paths of seeing, which are antidotes to the afflictive obstructions, directly realize selflessness: 
13. Path. This is indicated by non-agent in the sutras and presents the selflessness 
of persons who are agents. In general, there are persons who are agents that 
meditate, practice, and so forth, persons who are knowers that know many things, persons who are transferers from lower states to higher states, and persons 
Topic 1: Subject-Aspects of the Antidote Class 49 
 who are tamers of the afflictions. However, there are no such self-sufficient, 
substantially existent persons. 
14. Suitable . This is indicated by non-knower and presents the selflessness of 
persons who are knowers of objects. 
15. Achieving . This is indicated by non-transferer and presents the selflessness of 
persons who are transferers from inferior states. 
16. Deliverer . This is indicated by non-tamer and presents the selflessness of persons 
who are tamers of afflictions. 
  These first sixteen aspects are commonly described as the sixteen attributes of the four noble truths. A more detailed explanation of these can be found in appendix 2, pp. 162166. 
Five aspects of true paths that are contaminated paths of meditation 
The five aspects of true paths that are contaminated paths of meditation are the exalted wisdoms of subsequent attainment of a bodhisattvas path of meditation. They realize the non-existence of external objects, or in other words, that forms and so forth are 
17. lacking the entity of external objects, since they are mere appearances that arise from 
latencies and so forth, like dreams.  Forms and so forth appear to exist externally but 
do not, just as the mansion, elephant, horse, and so forth in a dream appear to 
exist but do not. 
18. not being produced as external objects, like echoes. When one shouts in an empty 
house, an echo is produced. Although it appears that the voice is produced by the house, it does not exist in this way. 
19. not ceasing as external objects, like optical illusions.  For example, due to an 
impairment of the eyes, a single moon can appear as double although it does not exist in this way. 
20. being pacified from the beginning as external objects, like mirages.  A mirage is the 
appearance of water that comes about due to the condition of hot sun shining on a flat surface, while in reality there is no water there from the beginning. 
21. by nature passed beyond sorrow as external objects, like illusions.  Illusions, such as 
women, horses, elephants, and so forth, are creations made by a magician through the power of substance and mantra that trick the eyes of the spectators. Although these creations appear to be real, they are not. 
Topic 1: Subject-Aspects of the Antidote Class 50 
  
Moreover, the bodhisattva on the path of meditation realizes that forms and so 
forth, while not existing as external objects, are mere mistaken appearances that appear to be external objects. 
Six aspects of true paths that are uncontaminated paths of seeing 
The six aspects of true paths that are uncontaminated paths of seeing are the exalted wisdoms in the continuum of a bodhisattva on the path of seeing that directly realize forms and so forth are not external objects. They newly and directly realize that 
22. thoroughly afflicted phenomena such as desire are empty of being external 
objects, as indicated by non-existence of thoroughly afflicted phenomena. 
23. completely pure phenomena such as faith are empty of being external objects, 
as indicated by non-existence of completely pure phenomena. 
24. the nature of mind is unsullied by stains due to externally existent latencies. 
25. forms and so forth are without the elaboration of externally existent 
divisions. 
26. there is no fancying for taste regarding a realization that adheres to external 
objects. 
2 7 .   t h e  o b j e c t  o f  a p p r e h e n s i o n  t h a t  i s   u n s h a k a b l e   ( r e f e r r i n g  t o  t h e  n o n -
degeneration of realizations already attained) does not exist as an external object. 
 
Topic 1: Subject-Aspects of the Antidote Class 51 
 2. Aspects of a knower of paths 
 
173 aspects of an exalted knower of all aspects  
1. 27 aspects of a knower of bases

The definition of a subject-aspect of a knower of paths is  a subject-aspect of the antidote 
class in the continuum of a Mahayana superior that (1) is attained by the force of 
cultivating an application that is a knower of paths, the method for attaining it, and 
(2) is able to fulfill the aims of the wishes of bodhisattva superiors, its children.27 
 
Boundaries : It exists in the continua of Mahayana superiors. 
 
4.3 Regarding cause, path, suffering, and cessation, respectively, 
 Eight, seven, five and sixteen are proclaimed.  
 
 There are thirty-six aspects of a knower of paths, which are also related to the four noble truths. The causes, true origins and true paths, and the results, true sufferings and true cessations, have respectively eight, seven, five and sixteen aspects. The four noble truths in this context are set out in the order of causes and results. This is for the special purpose of making known the forward order of entering into 
cyclic existence in dependence on the thoroughly afflicted − true origins being the 
cause and true sufferings being the result, and the reverse order of exiting from cyclic existence in dependence on the completely pure − true paths being the cause and 
true cessations being the nominal result. 
Eight aspects of true origins  
These are shown from the point of view of antidotes and abandonments. The eight 
antidotes indicated in the sutras abandon the eight strong adherences,28 the objects 
of abandonment.  
Topic 1: Subject-Aspects of the Antidote Class 52 
  
13. The three exalted wisdoms of a bodhisattva that are related to the aspects of 
cause  of true origins that: 
• are free from the attachment that is craving for desirable objects, 
• do not abide in a manner of grasping at non-separation from desirable 
objects, and 
• pacify the craving that is a liking for a body, 
   
 are the three aspects that are the respective antidotes to: 
• the aspiration that is a craving for objects, 
• the attachment that is a craving not to be separated, and 
• the craving that is an attraction to repeated existence. 
 
46. The three exalted wisdoms of a bodhisattva that are related to the aspects of 
source  directly realizing: 
• the non-existence of the referent object of attachment, 
• the non-existence of the referent object of hatred, and 
• the non-existence of the referent object of ignorance, 
    are the three aspects that are the respective antidotes to: 
• attachment, 
• hatred, and 
• ignorance. 
 
7.  The exalted wisdom of a bodhisattva that is related to the aspect of strong 
producer  directly realizing: 
• the non-existence of the referent object of the afflictive emotion of improper 
mental engagement 
 
is the aspect that is an antidote to: 
• improperly adhering to purity, being pleasurable, permanence, self, and so 
forth. 
 
Topic 1: Subject-Aspects of the Antidote Class 53 
 The definition of improper mental engagement is  a mental engagement that does not 
accord with the mode of existence of an object.  
 
Attachment and hatred arise from improper mental engagement. In the case of attachment, one engages an attractive object improperly by exaggerating its attractive qualities, whereupon attachment arises. Likewise, in the case of hatred, one improperly engages an unattractive object by exaggerating its negative qualities or superimposing negative qualities on it, whereby hatred arises.  
8.  The exalted wisdom of a bodhisattva that is related to the aspect of causal 
condition  directly realizing: 
• the non-existence of an independent sentient being, 
 
is the aspect that is an antidote to: 
• strong adherence to an independent sentient being. 
 An independent sentient being can be understood to mean either a permanent, unitary, independent sentient being or a self-sufficient, substantially existent sentient being. 
Seven aspects of true paths 
These are taught by means of thesis and proof. They are described in relation to the four aspects of true paths, namely path, suitable, an achieving and a deliverer. The first three have a pair of thesis and proof each, while the last has only the thesis. 
Path 
9.  The Mahayana path of seeing has the aspect of path that opens the 
opportunity for liberation to all sentient beings due to opening the opportunity for liberation to immeasurable sentient beings. 
10. How does it open the opportunity? It opens the opportunity for liberation to all 
sentient beings by not being based on the two extremes of cyclic existence and nirvana. 
Topic 1: Subject-Aspects of the Antidote Class 54 
 Suitable 
11. The Mahayana path of seeing has the aspect of being suitable due to directly 
realizing all phenomena as not different in being empty of true existence. 
12. How is it suitable? It realizes freedom from elaborations without holding the 
hearers and solitary realizers paths to be supreme. 
Achieving 
13. The Mahayana path of seeing has the aspect of an achieving due to directly 
realizing all phenomena as non-truly existent and not conceiving them to be 
truly existent. 
14. How is it an achieving? It directly realizes all phenomena as ultimately 
incomprehensible by a valid cognizer. 
Deliverer  
15. The Mahayana path of seeing has the aspect of a deliverer due to directly 
realizing all phenomena as the aspect of non-attachment to true existence. 
Five aspects of true sufferings 
These five aspects are shown by means of specific and general characteristics. The five appropriated aggregates are: 
Specific characteristics  
16. impermanent  due to disintegrating momentarily and continuously. 
17. miserable  because they are objects to be discarded by and are discordant with 
superiors. 
18. empty  because they are devoid of a self, an agent that is a different entity. 
19. selfless  because they are not established as the entity of a self of persons. 
General characteristics 
20. without characteristics  because they are empty of a truly existing entity. 
 
Topic 1: Subject-Aspects of the Antidote Class 55 
  The first four aspects are called specific characteristics because they characterize 
true sufferings in particular, while the fifth is a general characteristic in that it characterizes all phenomena. All compounded and uncompounded phenomena, as well as all contaminated and uncontaminated phenomena, are empty of true existence. 
Sixteen aspects of true cessations  
These sixteen aspects are shown by means of the beneficial qualities of cessation. They are related to the sixteen emptinesses, for it is through meditating on emptiness that one actualizes a true cessation. Here, a true cessation is a state of 
freedom attained through having abandoned the seeds of the conception of true 
existence, i.e., the knowledge obstructions. True cessations are divided into four categories in relation to their four aspects: namely, cessation, pacification, highly auspicious, and definitely liberating. In the Yogachara-Svatantrika system, a true cessation is a conventional truth and is, therefore, neither an emptiness nor an ultimate truth.   
2123. The three aspects of cessation , in relation to the emptinesses of 
• internal, 
• external, 
• both internal and external, 
 
are the three freedoms distinguished by stopping strong adherence to the true existence of, respectively, 
• internal objects, that is the five sense powers. 
• external objects, that is the five sense objects. 
• both internal and external objects, that is the five sense organs.

• emptiness, 
• the great, 
• the ultimate, 
• the compounded, 
• the uncompounded, 
• that passed beyond extremes,

 • that without beginning or end, and 
• that not to be discarded, 
 
are the eight freedoms distinguished by stopping strong adherence to the true existence of, respectively, 
• the emptiness of a self that is imputed by others; 
• the world of the environment; 
• the ultimate, which can refer to either ultimate truth or the exalted 
wisdom of meditative equipoise; 
• compounded phenomena produced by causes and conditions; 
• uncompounded phenomena not produced by causes and conditions; 
• the extremes of permanence and annihilation; 
• cyclic existence, which is without beginning or end; and 
• the realized qualities, such as the thirty-seven harmonies with enlightenment, that are not to be discarded. 
 
32.  The one aspect of highly auspicious , in relation to the emptiness of  
• nature 
 
is the freedom distinguished by stopping strong adherence to the true existence of 
• the agent that is imputed by other agents. 
 
3336. The four aspects of definitely liberating , in relation to the emptinesses of 
• all phenomena,

are the freedoms distinguished by stopping, respectively,

phenomena.

 • mistaking the three times, past, future, and so forth, as truly existent. 
• adherence to the entity of true existence with respect to all phenomena.
