Topic 1: Subject-Aspects of the Antidote Class 60 
 1. Four close placements of mindfulness 
 
The definition of close placement of mindfulness is  an exalted knower engaged in a path, 
which is included in either mindfulness or wisdom, that meditates by way of 
investigating each of the four − bodies, feelings, minds, and phenomena − in terms 
of both specific and general characteristics .30 
 
There are four close placements of mindfulness on the body, feelings, minds, and 
phenomena in which mindfulness repeatedly focuses on the thorough investigation of their specific and general characteristics. The specific characteristics of each object are: 
! impurity of the body, 
! the suffering nature of feelings, 
! impermanence of the mind, and 
! selflessness of phenomena, 
 
whereas the general characteristics − impermanent, miserable, empty, selfless, and 
empty of true existence   are applicable to all four objects.  
 Through the close placement of mindfulness on the body, one sees that the body is in the nature of
 pervasive compounded suffering; from this, one generates a wish 
to be free from this suffering. In this way, one comes to understand true sufferings.   Through the close placement of mindfulness on feelings, one sees that feeling induces craving, which is a cause of suffering; from this, one generates the wish to abandon craving. In this way, one comes to understand true origins.  Through the close placement of mindfulness on the mind, one realizes that the mind, which is distinct from a self, has the nature of being produced and ceased momentarily. From this, one no longer fears  losing the self and generates the wish to actualize cessation. In this way, one engages in true cessations.   Through the close placement of mindfulness on phenomena, one realizes that all phenomena of the thoroughly afflicted class are to be abandoned whereas those of the completely pure class are to be adopted; from this, one develops the wish to cultivate a path. In this way, one engages in true paths.   Thus, with the four close placements of mindfulness, one engages in meditation on the four noble truths. 
Topic 1: Subject-Aspects of the Antidote Class 61 
 2. Four thorough abandonings 
The definition of a thorough abandoning is  an exalted knower engaged in a path that is 
an effort that delights in rejecting and adopting abandonments and antidotes 
[respectively] .31  
 
The four thorough abandonings are in the nature of effort that acts to: 
! thoroughly abandon non-virtues already generated 
! not generate non-virtues that are not yet generated 
! increase virtues already generated 
! generate virtues not yet generated 
 
3. Four legs of magical manifestation 
The definition of leg of magical manifestation is  an exalted knower engaged in a path that 
is a meditative stabilization based on the eight applications that abandon, these 
being the antidotes to the five faults .32  
 
The four legs of magical manifestation are:  
! leg of magical manifestation of aspiration 
! leg of magical manifestation of effort 
! leg of magical manifestation of intention 
! leg of magical manifestation of analysis 
 
They are meditative stabilizations that, respectively , 
! attain a single-pointed mind in dependence on the meditative stabilization 
that aspires to the antidotes against the five faults. 
! attain a single-pointed mind in dependence on effort. 
! attain a single-pointed mind in dependence on the meditative stabilization of intention. 
! attain a single-pointed mind in dependence on the meditative stabilization of analysis, which thoroughly
 discriminates the doctrine taught by others. 
 
As well, each one possesses the eight antidotes that counter the five faults.   The four mental factors of aspiration, effort, intention and analysis themselves are not the legs of magical manifestation. They serve to induce the meditative 
Topic 1: Subject-Aspects of the Antidote Class 62 
 stabilizations and are developed in order to achieve the special powers of magical 
manifestation. 
 The five faults  (chart 1.3 ) mentioned hinder the development of a single-pointed 
mind of calm abiding. The five faults are: (1) laziness, (2) forgetfulness, (3) laxity and 
excitement, (4) non-application and (5) over-application. The four antidotes of (1) faith, (2) aspiration, (3) effort, and (4) pliancy counteract the fault of laziness. The antidote of (5) mindfulness counteracts forgetfulness, while (6) introspection guards against the fault of laxity and excitement. The fault of non-application of antidotes when it is necessary to do so is counteract by the antidote of (7) intention to apply the antidotes. When it is no longer necessary to apply the antidotes and yet one persists in their over-application, there is the antidote of (8) equanimity. 
 
 Chart 1.3  Faults and antidotes of calm abiding meditation  
Faults Antidotes  
   1. Laziness    1. Faith 
   2. Aspiration 
   3. Effort 
   4. Pliancy 
   2. Forgetfulness    5. Mindfulness 
   3. Laxity and Excitement    6. Introspection 
   4. Non-application    7. Intention 
   5. Over-application    8. Equanimity 
4. Five powers 
The definition of a power from within the thirty-seven harmonies with enlightenment is an 
exalted knower that independently produces a superiors path, its result.33 
 
There are five powers, which are the paths that prepare for clear realization of the true nature of existence. They are called powers because they empower one to achieve the result of enlightenment. They are: 
1. the power of faith, which is a conviction regarding the four noble truths. 
2. the power of effort, which delights in discarding true sufferings and true 
origins and adopting true cessations and true paths. 
3. the power of mindfulness, which does not forget the observed objects and 
aspects of the four noble truths. 
4. the power of meditative stabilization, which is a single-pointed mind 
focused on the four noble truths without distraction. 
Topic 1: Subject-Aspects of the Antidote Class 63 
 5. the power of wisdom, which discriminates the mode of abidance of the 
four noble truths. 
5. Five forces 
The definition of completely pure forces from within the thirty-seven harmonies with 
enlightenment is  an exalted knower that cannot be overcome by its discordant class.34 
 
The difference between the five forces and the previously explained five powers is 
that when practicing the five powers, their discordant class − non-faith, laziness, 
forgetfulness, distraction, and corrupt wisdom − can still arise, whereas at the time 
of the five forces, the discordant class can no longer arise, or at least they are greatly 
weakened. The five forces are enumerated in a manner similar to the five powers: 
6. the force of faith, which is a conviction regarding the four noble truths. 
7. the force of effort, which quickly realizes the four noble truths. 8. the force of mindfulness, which does not forget the observed objects and 
aspects of the four noble truths. 
9. the force of meditative stabilization, which is a single-pointed mind 
focused on the four noble truths without distraction. 
10. the force of wisdom, which discriminates individual aspects of the four 
noble truths. 
6. Seven branches of enlightenment 
The definition of a branch of enlightenment is  an exalted knower in a superiors 
continuum that acts as a cause of the enlightenment which is its result.35 
 
The seven branches of enlightenment on the path of seeing are as follows. 
11. The branch of enlightenment that is correct mindfulness    I t  i s  t h e  b r a n c h  o f  
abiding because with mindfulness one will not be distracted to other 
objects. 
12. The branch of enlightenment that is a fine discrimination of phenomena directly 
realizing selflessness   This is the branch of nature because it is in the nature 
of a non-conceptual exalted wisdom. 
13. The branch of enlightenment that is effort   This is the branch of definite 
emergence, for it is with effort that one distances oneself from the 
mundane levels. 
Topic 1: Subject-Aspects of the Antidote Class 64 
 14. The branch of enlightenment that is joy   This branch is described as that 
which benefits body and mind. 
15. The branch of enlightenment that is pliancy   Pliancy makes the body and 
mind serviceable and able to perform virtues due to overcoming the 
unserviceable states of body and mind. Hence, it is the branch of basis without afflictive emotions. 
16. The branch of enlightenment that is meditative stabilization   This is the branch 
of abiding without afflictive emotions because with meditative stabilization one generates a perfect wisdom that abandons afflictive emotions. 
17. The branch of enlightenment that is equanimity   With equanimity one 
counteracts an unbalanced state of mind that abides harmoniously with afflictive emotions; hence, this is the branch of entity without afflictive emotions. 
7. Eight branches of a superiors path 
The definition of a branch of a superiors path is  an exalted knower in a superiors 
continuum that is a cause of a superiors path, its result .36  
 
The eight branches of a superiors path are as follows. 
18. Correct view   which thoroughly analyzes in subsequent attainment the 
reality (emptiness) that was realized in meditative equipoise. 
19. Correct thought   which motivates speech that teaches ones own 
realizations to others. 
20. Correct speech   a completely pure action of speech that teaches ones own 
realizations to others. 
21. Correct aims of actions   a completely pure physical action, such as 
abandoning killing. 
22. Correct livelihood   a completely pure physical or verbal action that is 
separated from wrong livelihoods such as flattery. 
23. Correct effort   the effort with regard to the antidotes that abandon the 
objects of abandonment. 
24. Correct mindfulness   which is retaining without forgetting, and thus, the 
reason for calm abiding, heightened apprehension, and equanimity. 
25. Correct meditative stabilization   which is meditation on objects that are the 
special excellent qualities such as the clairvoyances. 
Topic 1: Subject-Aspects of the Antidote Class 65 
  
 These eight can be condensed into four: 
! the branch that thoroughly analyzes 
! the branch that brings about understanding 
! the branch that brings about trust in others 
! the branch that is an antidote to the discordant class 
 
Correct view  is included in the branch that thoroughly analyzes the truths realized 
during meditative equipoise. Correct thought  is included in the second branch that 
brings about understanding. Correct speech , correct aims of actions , and correct livelihood  
are included in the branch that brings about trust in others. The remaining three, correct effort , correct mindfulness , and correct meditative stabilization  are included in the 
branch that is an antidote to the discordant class. 
 These eight paths of the superiors (sometimes referred to as the eightfold path) can also be condensed into the three higher trainings: 
! higher training of ethics  correct speech, correct aims of actions, and correct livelihood 
! higher training of concentration  correct mindfulness and correct meditative 
stabilization 
! higher training of wisdom  correct view, correct thought, and correct effort 
 
These are the thirty-seven aspects of an exalted knower of all aspects that are similar to those of hearers. They are posited in relation to the basis, the hearer learners, who possess a similar set of such aspects in their continua. However, the bodhisattvas also cultivate these aspects.  
B. 34 aspects of an exalted knower of all aspects harmonious with 
bodhisattvas 
 
173 aspects of an exalted knower of all aspects 
1. 27 aspects of a knower of bases 
2. 36 aspects of a knower of paths 
3. 110 aspects of an exalted knower of all aspects 
• 37 aspects of an exalted knower of all aspects harmonious with hearers  
Topic 1: Subject-Aspects of the Antidote Class 66 
 • 34 aspects of an exalted knower of all aspects harmonious with bodhisattvas 
• 39 uncommon aspects of an exalted knower of all aspects 
 
These are aspects of the omniscient mind that are similar to realizations of bodhisattvas. There are altogether thirty-four aspects, divided into six groups: 
26. Three paths of antidotes in the continuum of a buddha superior  
27. Three paths of manifestation  28. Five paths that abide in happiness in this present life  29. Nine supramundane paths  30. Four paths of abandoning  31. Ten paths of buddhahood  
1. Three paths of antidotes in the continuum of a buddha superior  
In general, the definition of a path of antidote is  an exalted knower that destroys its 
respective discordant class. 
However, in this case,  
the definition of a path of antidote indicated here is  an exalted knower distinguished by 
the ability to quell the superimposition that is its object to be eliminated through 
realizing the non-existence of the referent object of the superimposition that is its 
object to be eliminated.37  
  We ordinary beings superimpose, or overlay, non-existent qualities upon phenomena. A path of antidote in this case eliminates this superimposition by realizing that these overlaid qualities do not, in fact, exist. For example, an exalted wisdom directly realizing emptiness quells the conception of true existence, a conception that all ordinary beings have. This conception is destroyed by way of 
realizing that the referent object  true existence  does not exist. Thus, the exalted 
wisdom directly realizing emptiness is a path of antidote. The bodhisattva cultivates this exalted wisdom, but only a buddha superior has it fully developed within his mental continuum.   There are three doors of liberation that are paths of antidotes: 
! emptiness 
! signlessness 
! wishlessness 
 
Topic 1: Subject-Aspects of the Antidote Class 67 
  There are different ways of positing these three. In general, the three doors of 
liberation are posited in terms of the following: (1) emptiness  is the emptiness of the 
entity of phenomena; (2) signlessness  is the emptiness of phenomena being truly 
produced from causes (i.e., there is no sign of truly existent causes); and (3) wishlessness  is the emptiness of phenomena truly producing a result (i.e., there is no 
wish for truly existent results). However, in the case of the three paths of antidotes 
they are posited in terms of the sixteen attributes of the four noble truths:   
! Emptiness  The first door of liberation is the exalted wisdom directly realizing 
the last two attributes of true sufferings, empty and selfless, in the continuum of a bodhisattva superior. It is an antidote to the view of a self by way of directly realizing that there is no permanent, unitary, independent self that is an entity other than the aggregates (empty) nor is there a self-sufficient, substantially existent self that is the same entity as the aggregates (selfless).   
! Signlessness  The second door of liberation is the exalted wisdom directly 
realizing the eight attributes of true cessations and true paths. It is a direct antidote to the improper conception grasping at the sign of true existence that arises due to the view of self.   
! Wishlessness  The third door of liberation is the exalted wisdom directly 
realizing the first two attributes of true sufferings (impermanent and miserable) and the four attributes of true origins. It is an antidote to the wish that considers the realms within cyclic existence to be a final object to be attained. 
 
This is the explanation of the three paths of antidotes according to Ornament . There 
are different presentations in Treasury of Knowledge (Abhidharmakosha)  and Ornament 
of the Mahayana Sutras (Mahayanasutralamkara) . 
 
2. Three paths of manifestation  
The definition of a path of manifestation indicated in this case is  an exalted knower 
distinguished by (1) freedom from certain types of obstructions to absorption in 
dependence on a final concentration that is its empowering condition, and (2) the 
ability to display various transformed manifestations.38  
 
Topic 1: Subject-Aspects of the Antidote Class 68 
  The purpose of the three paths of manifestation is to abandon attachment to 
forms and, in doing so, eliminate the obstacles to manifesting (or emanating) countless forms. With the three paths of manifestation, one is able to manifest without limit all types of forms in order to address the needs of sentient beings.  The three paths of manifestation are as follows: 
! liberation of the embodied looking at form 
! liberation of the formless looking at form 
! liberation of the attractive 
  The first two paths of manifestation eliminate the obstructions to absorption, the obstacles that would impede a yogi to manifest as he wished: 
! Liberation of the embodied looking at form   In dependence upon a final 
concentration,39 a yogi manifests many forms while his own body remains 
visible to others. 
! Liberation of the formless looking at form   In dependence upon a final 
concentration, the yogi considers himself as formless (possessing only four of the five aggregates: feelings, discriminations, compositional factors, and consciousnesses) and manifests many forms while his own body is observed as invisible. 
  The third door of liberation eliminates the fault of liking attractive forms and 
disliking unattractive forms. 
! Liberation of the attractive   In dependence upon a final concentration, the yogi 
develops the antidotes to (i) the joy of emanating attractive forms and (ii) the 
dislike of manifesting unattractive forms. Thus, with the third door of liberation, all forms appear to the yogi as having the single taste of being attractive. 
 This yogi can be a bodhisattva who abides on the great level of the Mahayana path of accumulation (or higher) or a Hinayana yogi who is either a never returner
40 who 
manifests a body or a foe destroyer who is free from the two obstructions, the afflictive obstructions and the obstructions to absorption.  Although in general the three paths of manifestation merely require a concentration or a formless absorption as a mental support, the paths of manifestation as aspects of an exalted knower of all aspects are dependent upon a final concentration. 
Topic 1: Subject-Aspects of the Antidote Class 69 
 3. Five paths that abide in happiness in this present life  
The definition of a path that abides in happiness in the present life is  an exalted knower 
that is not polluted by coarse afflictions in the continuum of a person who has 
attained a liberation.41 
 
 There are five paths that abide in happiness in this present life:  
32. the liberation of the absorption of infinite space 
33. the liberation of the absorption of infinite consciousness 34. the liberation of the absorption of nothingness  
35. the liberation of the absorption of peak of cyclic existence 
36. the liberation of cessation 
 
 The first four liberations are based on the four levels of absorption
42 of the 
formless realm. They are liberations because they act as causes of the path to liberation and because they are free of the manifest afflictions of their respective levels.  The fifth liberation, the liberation of cessation, is called a liberation because it is free from the obstructions to the cessation of coarse feelings and discriminations. The liberation of cessation is a meditative equipoise existing in the continuum of a 
superior that meditates on reality ( dharmata ) by depending upon both a 
supramundane path and a mind of the peak of cyclic existence. With the attainment 
of the liberation of cessation, one has stopped afflictions such as desire and anger that arise due to feelings and discriminations.  These five liberations are dependent upon a supramundane path; thus, they exist only in the continua of superiors. They are primarily cultivated by bodhisattva superiors, though they are also generated by some hearer and solitary realizer superiors. When hearers and solitary realizers absorb into these liberations, it is only to abide in happiness in this life. However, bodhisattvas absorb into these only for the welfare of other beings. Although a bodhisattva superior absorbs into the four formless absorptions, this does not mean, therefore, that he has the projecting karma to be reborn in the formless realm. He does not because he has extinguished the contaminated karma to be reborn in the formless realm. The formless realm is not a place to subdue sentient beings because the Dharma cannot be taught there. 
Topic 1: Subject-Aspects of the Antidote Class 70 
 4. Nine supramundane paths  
The nine supramundane paths are the nine serial absorptions. In reverse order, they 
are:   
9. Absorption of cessation 
  8. Absorption of peak of cyclic existence 7. Absorption of nothingness    6. Absorption of infinite consciousness    
5. Absorption of infinite space 
 4. Absorption of the fourth concentration 3. Absorption of the third concentration 2. Absorption of the second concentration
  
1. Absorption of the first concentration 
 
 
These nine are the four concentrations and the four formless absorptions that are supramundane paths, and the absorption of cessation; they are also called the nine serial absorptions. In general, the four concentrations and four formless absorptions themselves are not supramundane paths, but in this context they have been transformed into the entity of supramundane paths. The ninth, the absorption of cessation is called a supramundane path, but, because it is not a consciousness, it is 
not a path in actuality. The absorption of cessation is a non-associated compositional 
factor
43 in the continuum of a superior that remains after coarse feelings and 
discriminations have been stopped. It is called a supramundane path because it is 
attained by the power of a supramundane path.      Four formless 
absorptions  
Four concentrations of the form realm 
Topic 1: Subject-Aspects of the Antidote Class 71 
  The absorption of cessation is posited differently by the various tenet systems.  
According to Svatantrika-Madhyamaka, 
the definition of an absorption of cessation is  a non-associated compositional factor that 
is an absorption in the continuum of a superior, abiding in a type distinguished by 
the cessation of the coarse objects to be stopped along with their accompaniments 
on the subtle mental consciousness, the basis of the cessation.44  
  With the absorption of cessation, the coarse minds and mental factors are ceased, whereby the accompanying coarse feelings and discriminations are also ceased. It is initially attained on the basis of the peak of cyclic existence; it can only be attained by superiors, not by ordinary beings.   According to Prasangika-Madhyamaka, the absorption of cessation is posited not as a non-associated compositional factor but as an exalted wisdom, a consciousness.   
The definition of an absorption of cessation in Prasangika is  an exalted wisdom of single-
pointed meditative equipoise on reality ( dharmata ) that abides in a type free from 
attachment to the peak of cyclic existence in dependence on a supramundane path 
which attains it and an actual mind of the peak of cyclic existence.45  
   In short, the absorption of cessation in Svatantrika is the cessation of the arising of coarse feelings and discriminations achieved by means of stopping the coarse 
minds and their accompanying mental factors. The absorption of cessation is 
initially attained in dependence upon the support of a human body.   It is important to understand that coarse feelings and coarse discriminations do, in fact, bring us harm. For instance, upon encountering another person, a feeling and a discrimination arise, and as a result, we generally generate attachment or aversion. This shows that coarse feelings and coarse discriminations can give rise to many afflictions. If we are able to stop coarse feelings and discriminations, we will have fewer afflictions. 
5. Four paths of abandonment  
A path of abandonment has four characteristics: (a) being a Dharma forbearance, (b) having the aspect of an uninterrupted path, (c) being a knower (consciousness) that is included in the four truths, and (d) not being associated with the thoroughly afflicted.  There are four types of paths of abandonment: 
1. Dharma forbearance of true sufferings 
Topic 1: Subject-Aspects of the Antidote Class 72 
 2. Dharma forbearance of true origins 
3. Dharma forbearance of true paths 4. Dharma forbearance of true cessations 
 
 These four paths refer to the Dharma forbearances, or uninterrupted paths
46 of 
seeing, related to the four noble truths. The meditative equipoise of the path of seeing consists of two periods: an uninterrupted path and a path of release. The uninterrupted path is an exalted wisdom that acts to eliminate the objects of abandonment, the acquired obstructions, and is immediately followed by the path of release,
47 which is an exalted wisdom free of these objects of abandonment. The four 
paths of abandonment refer to the uninterrupted paths that are the four Dharma forbearances. 
6. Ten paths of buddhahood  
The definition of a path of buddhahood is  an exalted knower abiding in a type of path 
that is a means for progressing to the buddha ground.48  
 The paths of the buddhas are the ten perfections, which are successively the focus of practice over the course of the ten bodhisattva grounds. Although they are termed perfections, Svatantrika maintains that the bodhisattva superior merely engages in 
the surpassing  practice  of the ten perfections since the actual perfections exist only on 
the buddha ground.  
1. Generosity   the intention to give. There are three types: (a) generosity of 
material objects, (b) generosity of Dharma, and (c) generosity of protection 
from fear. In the context of tantra there is a fourth type, (d) the generosity of love. 
2. Ethics   the intention to observe or guard. It can also be defined as the 
intention to abandon misdeeds. There are three types: (a) the ethics of abstaining from non-virtue, (b) the ethics of accumulating virtue, and (c) the ethics of benefiting others. 
3. Patience   an undisturbed mind. The three types are:  (a) the patience of not 
retaliating to harm,  (b) the patience of voluntarily accepting suffering or difficulties, and (c) the patience of definitely bearing phenomena. The patience of definitely bearing phenomena refers to the patience of meditating on emptiness without the fear of losing the self or the fear of phenomena becoming non-existent. 
Topic 1: Subject-Aspects of the Antidote Class 73 
 4. Joyous effort   delight in virtue. 
5. Concentration   a mind single-pointedly focused on a virtuous object. 
6. Wisdom   that which discriminates phenomena. 
7. Skillful means   that which issues forth great results with little difficulty. It is 
said that the perfection of skillful means is composed of two groups: inner 
skillful means and outer skillful means. In general, inner skillful means refers to the achievement of buddhahood in ones own continuum, while outer skillful means refers to the ripening of sentient beings.  
8. Prayer   a wish to accomplish something. While in the scriptures it is said that 
there are ten types of prayer, other texts condense prayer into five: (a) prayer for bodhichitta, (b) prayer for higher rebirth, (c) prayer to become an object of use, (d) perfect prayer, and (e) great prayer. 
9. Power   the strength gained in order to not be overcome by the discordant 
class. There are thirteen powers: (a) power of thought, (b) power of extraordinary thought, (c) power of retention, (d) power of meditative stabilization,  (e) power of perfectly enriched wealth,  (f) power of control/mastery,  (g) power of self-confidence,  (h) power of prayer,  (i) transcendent power,  (j) power of great love, (k) power of great compassion, (l) power of reality, and (m) power of being blessed by all the tathagatas. 
10. Exalted wisdom   that which understands exactly the presentation of the way in 
which phenomena are posited. There are two types of exalted wisdom: that knowing ultimate truth and that knowing conventional truth.  
  When condensed into six perfections, the last four of these ten perfections  skillful means, prayer, power, and exalted wisdom  are included in the sixth perfection, the perfection of wisdom.  
C. 39 uncommon aspects of an exalted knower of all aspects  
173 aspects of an exalted knower of all aspects 
1. 27 aspects of a knower of bases 
2. 36 aspects of a knower of paths 
3. 110 aspects of an exalted knower of all aspects 
• 37 aspects of an exalted knower of all aspects harmonious with hearers 
• 34 aspects of an exalted knower of all aspects harmonious with bodhisattvas 
• 39 uncommon aspects of an exalted knower of all aspects 
 
Topic 1: Subject-Aspects of the Antidote Class 74 
 The thirty-nine aspects of an exalted knower of all aspects are uncommon in the 
sense that they are unique to a buddha. They do not exist in the learner superiors - the hearer, solitary realizer, and bodhisattva superiors. Therefore, these thirty-nine are unsurpassable; they are the highest qualities that can be attained.   There are seven groups of uncommon aspects: 
1. Ten powers 
2. Four fearlessnesses 3. Four individual correct knowledges 4. Eighteen unshared attributes of buddhas 5. Aspect of thusness, the entity 6. Self-arisen aspect, the base 7. Aspect of buddha, the object of intent 
1. Ten powers 
The definition of a power of buddha is  a final exalted knower dependent on a final 
concentration, which is its empowering condition, that is distinguished by the ability 
to overcome its discordant class.49  
 
A power in this context is that which cannot be overcome by antagonists, but instead is capable of overcoming them. The ten powers are exalted knowers; thus, they are consciousnesses. They are possessed by all buddhas. 
1. Knowledge of sources and non-sources   This is the exalted knower that knows the 
result of actions; a buddha knows the appropriate and inappropriate causes of results. For example, the knowledge that happiness arises from virtue is a knowledge of sources since a buddha knows the appropriate cause (virtue) for the result (happiness). The knowledge that happiness does not arise from non-virtue is a knowledge of non-sources since a buddha knows that non-virtue is not a source of happiness.  
2. Knowledge of the fruition of actions   This is the exalted knower that directly 
realizes actions and their results without exception; buddhas know the specific causes of particular results. Although some bodhisattvas know the law of actions and results roughly, only buddhas know it in detail.  
3. Knowledge of the varieties of interests   This power is an exalted knower of the 
varieties of interests of sentient beings, who are predisposed to different paths leading to high status and definite goodness. High status refers to a rebirth in 
Topic 1: Subject-Aspects of the Antidote Class 75 
 the upper realms as a human being or god, while definite goodness refers to 
liberation. There are different paths to high status and definite goodness. A buddha knows the varieties of interests of trainees who are predisposed or inclined to the different paths.  
4. Knowledge of the many natures of the universe   There are two different 
interpretations of this. Some interpret this as referring to the interests of the trainees. However, the preferred interpretation is that this is an exalted knower of the realms of existence, elements, and constituents. 
5. Knowledge of who are of superior and inferior faculties   A buddha knows who has 
superior, middling, and inferior faculties. The level of ones faculties is related to the strength of the five powers: faith, effort, mindfulness, meditative stabilization, and wisdom. If the five powers are strong, then ones faculties are superior; if their strength is middling, then ones faculties are middling, and if their strength is weak, then ones faculties are inferior. Superior and inferior faculties are also determined by intelligence. 
6. Knowledge of paths that proceed everywhere   This power knows the paths that 
progress to high status and definite goodness without obstruction and impediment. For example, a buddha knows that to attain a high rebirth one needs to create meritorious or immovable karma; without doing this, one cannot attain such a rebirth.  
7. Knowledge of the thoroughly afflicted and the completely pure   This power knows 
thoroughly afflicted phenomena and completely pure phenomena. A buddha knows the obscurations caused by attachment to absorption in the concentrations and liberations, which are thoroughly afflicted phenomena, and knows the freedom from these obscurations, which are completely pure phenomena. Here, thoroughly afflicted phenomena refers to the attachment that arises when one enters the absorption of concentrations and liberations, while completely pure phenomena refers to freedom from such attachment.  
8. Knowledge mindful of former states   This power remembers ones own and 
others past lives. This means that a buddha knows where he and all others were born in the past.  
9. Knowledge of death and rebirth   This is the knowledge of when others will die 
and where they will be reborn. This power is related to future states, while the previous power is related to past states of rebirth. 
Topic 1: Subject-Aspects of the Antidote Class 76 
 10. Knowledge of the extinction of contaminations   This power knows that while 
hearers and solitary realizers have eliminated merely the afflictive 
obstructions, a buddha has eliminated all stains, both afflictive and knowledge obstructions. 
2. Four fearlessnesses 
The definition of a fearlessness is  a final exalted knower dependent on a final 
concentration, which is its empowering condition, that is distinguished by a lack of 
anxiety while teaching the holy Dharma in the midst of a retinue.50  
 Buddhas possess four types of fearlessness, which are posited with respect to four assertions: 
• Fearlessness with regard to perfect realization   A buddha is fearless with respect to 
proclaiming that I have directly realized all phenomena and I have become completely enlightened. 
• Fearlessness with regard to indicating hindering phenomena   A buddha is fearless 
regarding the presentation of the obstacles to liberation and omniscience: attachment and so forth are afflictive obstructions that impede liberation, and knowledge obstructions impede omniscience. 
• Fearlessness with regard to teaching the path of definite emergence   A buddha is 
fearless with respect to presenting the antidotes (such as the knower of bases and the knower of paths) that lead to liberation and omniscience. 
• Fearlessness with regard to perfect abandonment   A buddha has no fear of 
proclaiming that he has abandoned all contaminations without exception.  
  A buddha is able to proclaim his qualities without any fear. The four fearlessnesses can be condensed into two: 
1) Fearlessness regarding ones own welfare . This includes the first and the last:  
a) fearlessness with regard to perfect realization 
b) fearlessness with regard to perfect abandonment 
2) Fearlessness regarding others welfare . This includes the remaining two:  
a) fearlessness with regard to indicating hindering phenomena  b) fearlessness with regard to teaching the path of definite emergence 
Topic 1: Subject-Aspects of the Antidote Class 77 
 3. Four individual correct knowledges 
The definition of an individual correct knowledge that is a subject-aspect of an exalted knower 
of all aspects is  a final exalted knower dependent on a final concentration, which is its 
empowering condition, that is distinguished by having abandoned obstructions and 
impediments to the subject to be explained and the means of explaining it.51  
 
There are four individual correct knowledges: 
1. individual correct knowledge of phenomena  
2. individual correct knowledge of meanings 
3. individual correct knowledge of definite terminology 
4. individual correct knowledge of self-assurance 
 
 The first two are without impediment with regard to what is to be explained, while the last two are without impediment with regard to explaining. 
Without impediment with regard to what is to be explained:  
• Individual correct knowledge of phenomena   A meditative stabilization and 
wisdom that know the many enumerations of names of thoroughly afflicted phenomena and completely pure phenomena without obstructions and impediments. It also refers to knowing the many synonyms for the same thing.  
• Individual correct knowledge of meanings   A meditative stabilization and wisdom 
that know the general and specific characteristics of phenomena without obstructions and impediments. 
Without impediment with regard to explaining: 
• Individual correct knowledge of definite terminology   A meditative stabilization 
and wisdom that know the languages of all sentient beings of the six realms without obstructions and impediments to explaining.  
• Individual correct knowledge of self-assurance   A meditative stabilization and 
wisdom that know the classifications of entities and aspects of phenomena. A buddha has self-assurance in discriminating between them without obstructions and impediments. 
 
Topic 1: Subject-Aspects of the Antidote Class 78 
  A similitude of the four individual correct knowledges is present on the ninth 
bodhisattva ground, but only a buddha possesses the actual individual correct knowledges.  
4. Eighteen unshared qualities of buddhas 
The definition of the unshared qualities of buddhas is  a final exalted knower dependent 
on a final concentration, which is its empowering condition, that is distinguished by 
types and classes of excellent qualities that are not even slightly shared with lesser 
superiors.52  
 
These eighteen qualities of buddhas are unique in that they are not shared even 
remotely with bodhisattvas. In other words, bodhisattvas cannot generate even a similitude of these qualities. In contrast, bodhisattvas can develop a similitude of the other uncommon aspects of an exalted knower of all aspects.  The unshared qualities of buddhas are of eighteen types, divided into four groups:  
a) six unshared behaviors  
b) six unshared realizations c) three unshared enlightened activities d) three unshared exalted wisdoms 
a.  Six unshared behaviors 
Body 
1. Unmistaken bodily behavior   A buddha does not have any mistaken physical 
behavior such as stepping on snakes or taking a wrong road. 
Speech 
2. Without senseless speech   A buddha does not have uncontrolled speech such as 
shouting. 
Mind 
3. Without decrease in mindfulness   A buddha does not have memory lapses nor 
does his mindfulness degenerate.  
4. Without non-equipoise of mind    A  b u d d h a  s  m i n d  i s  a l w a y s  i n  m e d i t a t i v e  
equipoise on emptiness.  
5. Without discrimination of difference   A buddha knows that both cyclic existence 
and nirvana are not different in being empty of true existence. 
Topic 1: Subject-Aspects of the Antidote Class 79 
 6. Without the indifference of not investigating individually   A buddha constantly 
investigates whether sentient beings are ready to be ripened. When conditions 
are ripe, he engages in enlightened activities for their sake.  
b.  Six unshared realizations  
1. Non-decrease in aspiration   A buddha constantly acts for sentient beings 
welfare due to his love and compassion.  
2. Non-decrease in effort   A buddha always exerts joyous effort to enact the 
welfare of even one single sentient being in hundreds of realms.  
3. Non-decrease in mindfulness   A buddha is constantly mindful, without 
forgetfulness, of benefiting sentient beings.  
4. Non-decrease in meditative stabilization   This refers to a buddhas mind, which 
is always set in equipoise on the emptiness of all phenomena.  
5. Non-decrease in wisdom   A buddha possesses the wisdom discriminating all 
phenomena. He has an exalted knower that teaches the Dharma according to the needs of sentient beings. 
6. Non-degeneration of liberation   A buddhas liberation, the abandonment of 
obstructions, does not degenerate. It lasts until all sentient beings are free from cyclic existence. 
c.  Three unshared enlightened activities 
1. Enlightened physical activities   There are four modes of behavior by which a 
buddha performs enlightened activities of body. There are two ways of presenting these four modes of behavior: (a) the four behaviors of walking, sitting, lying, and standing, and (b) the four behaviors of radiating rays of light, walking, gazing with open eyes, and closing his eyes. 
2. Enlightened verbal activities   A buddha gives teachings in accordance with the 
interest, dispositions, and needs of sentient beings. 
3. Enlightened mental activities   A buddha has constant love and compassion. 
  If something is an enlightened activity of a buddha, it is necessarily an enlightened activity of the body, speech, or mind of a buddha. These three enlightened activities are not shared by lesser superiors. They are preceded by exalted 
wisdom, accompanied by exalted wisdom, and followed by exalted wisdom. They are 
preceded by exalted wisdom because the empowering condition for these three is 
Topic 1: Subject-Aspects of the Antidote Class 80 
 exalted wisdom. In addition, these three arise simultaneously with exalted wisdom 
and are followed by exalted wisdom.  
d.  Three unshared exalted wisdoms 
There are three types of unimpeded, unobstructed exalted wisdoms: those regarding (1) the past, (2) the future, and (3) the present.  A buddha directly knows all phenomena of the past, future, and present without impediment or obstruction. The three times are defined differently in the various systems. According to Svatantrika-Madhyamaka,  
the definition of the past at this time is  disintegrated at this time .53  
 
The definition of the future at this time is  (1) being either of the three: past, future, and 
present at this time, and (2) a common locus of not produced and not ceased at this 
time .54  
 
The definition of the present at this time is  a common locus of produced at this time and 
not ceased at this time .55 
5. Aspect of thusness, the entity 
The definition of an aspect of thusness that is a subject-aspect of an exalted knower of all 
aspects is  an exalted wisdom of meditative equipoise on thusness56 in a manner of 
not arising again.57  
 The emptiness of a buddhas mind is the aspect of thusness because it is the thusness explained by all the buddhas of the three times qualified by the extinction of all stains. From the point of view of the subject, the aspect of thusness is a buddhas exalted wisdom of meditative equipoise observing thusness in a manner of not arising again, that is, the omniscient mind is always absorbed in meditative equipoise on thusness, or emptiness. A buddha descends into the world for the purpose of clarifying the reality of phenomena to sentient beings, and because all buddhas explain thusness, it is emphasized here.   The term entity refers to the entity of the quality of a buddhas mind, which is continuously in meditative equipoise on thusness.  
Topic 1: Subject-Aspects of the Antidote Class 81 
 6. Self-arisen aspect, the base 
The definition of an aspect of self-arisen that is a subject-aspect of an exalted knower of all 
aspects is  a final exalted wisdom distinguished by having attained mastery over 
turning the wheel of Dharma without exception.58 
 
The fully developed perfection of wisdom that is principal is the aspect of the self-arisen that has mastered all the Dharma because it is an exalted wisdom that has attained mastery over turning the wheel of Dharma for trainees without exception. A buddha who appears as a supreme emanation body
59 has perfectly mastered 
turning the wheel of Dharma for those beings who need to be subdued. He does so in dependence upon an exalted knower of all aspects.  
 The wheel of Dharma is of two types: scriptural and realizational. The scriptural 
wheel of Dharma  is a very positive quality included by words of the Buddha; it 
includes the three wheels of Dharma turned by Shakyamuni Buddha. The realizational wheel of Dharma  is any clear realization in the continuum of one who has 
entered the path. Turning the wheel of Dharma refers to the first, the scriptural 
wheel of Dharma.  The term base is used because a buddha has many qualities such as the ten powers and so forth that are based on the aspect of the self-arisen that has mastered all the Dharma. In short, it is the base of all the above-mentioned qualities of a buddha.  
7. Aspect of buddha, the object of intent 
The definition of an aspect of buddha itself indicated here is  a final exalted wisdom that is 
the realization of manifest, fully actualized, complete, perfect enlightenment 
regarding all phenomena, the modes and varieties, in all ways.60  
 The exalted knower of all aspects is an aspect of buddha because it is the realization of manifest complete enlightenment regarding all phenomena in all aspects. 
Conclusion 
In conclusion, the 173 aspects can refer to either the 173 subject-aspects or the 173 object-aspects. However, chapter 4 mainly focuses on the subject-aspects.  In summary, there are 173 subject-aspects that a bodhisattva must cultivate in order to attain enlightenment. The consciousness that cultivates, or trains in, these 
Topic 1: Subject-Aspects of the Antidote Class 82 
 173 aspects is called an application in the complete aspects. These are divided as 
follows: 
• 27 aspects of a knower of bases   These exist in the continua of all superiors: 
hearer, solitary realizer, and buddha superiors. 
• 36 aspects of a knower of paths   These are cultivated only by Mahayana 
superiors: bodhisattva and buddha superiors. 
• 110 aspects of an exalted knower of all aspects   These exist only in the continua 
of buddhas. 
  In order to attain enlightenment, the bodhisattva cultivates these 173 aspects again and again together with a union of calm abiding and special insight. When the bodhisattva is able to correctly meditate on all 173 aspects serially in the shortest moment of a complete action, he will attain buddhahood in the next moment.

Topic 2: Principal Applications  88 
 Topic 2:  Principal Applications 
 
   Having presented the 173 aspects of the three exalted knowers that are to be cultivated, the applications that are to be engaged in are presented. Prior to the explanation of the actual applications, in order to better understand them, the person who engages in these applications is described. There are four types of persons who engage in the applications; they are differentiated on the basis of the type of practice they carry out in relation to the teachings of the Perfection of Wisdom sutras.  The actual applications include nineteen principal applications of a sharp-facultied bodhisattva and one belonging to a dull-facultied bodhisattva (application 6). These nineteen principal applications are a bodhisattvas yoga conjoined with a union of calm abiding and special insight observing emptiness. Therefore, they are found only in bodhisattvas who have realized emptiness by way of a union of calm abiding and special insight, that is, bodhisattvas from the path of preparation upward.  
 The term application is the translation of the Tibetan, sbyor ba  (pronounced jor 
wa). This word in Tibetan has the connotation of joining and connecting. It is 
almost synonymous with the term bodhisattvas yoga, where yoga is usually 
explained etymologically as joining the mind to that which is real or true. 
The person who engages in the applications 
The definition of a person who is the support, the cultivator of the applications that are 
explicitly indicated in this case is  a bodhisattva explicitly indicated in this case who 
possesses both inner and outer conditions .62 
 
4.6 Those who did their duties with respect to the buddhas, 
Sowed roots of virtue in regard to them, 
And are under the protection of virtuous spiritual friends Are vessels for listening to this.  
 
Topic 2: Principal Applications  89 
 4.7 Due to having served the buddhas, questioned, and 
The conduct of generosity, ethics, and so forth, 
Vessels who keep, retain, and so forth Are considered to be holy beings.  
 
There are four types of persons (called vessels) who engage in the applications shown in the root text:  
1. one who listens to the mother, the Perfection of Wisdom sutras 
2. one who memorizes and remembers the words of the Perfection of Wisdom 
sutras 
3. one who retains the meanings of the words of the Perfection of Wisdom 
sutras  having thoroughly questioned the buddhas and cleared up ones doubts 
4. one who properly reflects on the meanings and also practices them, in 
particular, the practice of the ten perfections 
 
 A bodhisattva on the path of accumulation is a vessel for listening to the three Perfection of Wisdom sutras, the extensive, middling, and brief, because 
1. he has served the buddhas. He has created the great actions of showing 
respect to numberless buddhas in the past by, for example, constructing residences for buddhas and bodhisattvas. 
2. he has sowed roots of virtue in regard to them. He has sown roots of virtue 
through making offerings and so forth to buddhas of the past and present. 
3. he is cared for by virtuous friends. He is under the guidance or protection of 
a Mahayana virtuous spiritual friend. 
  The vessels who memorize the words of the mothers, retain the meanings without forgetfulness, and complete the practices are considered to be holy beings by buddhas and bodhisattvas, because for this purpose they have served countless 
buddhas, questioned doubted words and meanings, and practiced the conduct of 
the ten perfections  generosity, ethics, and so forth  which are the objects of questioning. 
Topic 2: Principal Applications  90 
 The actual applications 
The definition of a principally indicated application explicitly indicated in this case is  a 
bodhisattvas yoga conjoined with a union of calm abiding and special insight 
observing emptiness that is explicitly indicated in this case .63  
 
Boundaries : It exists from the heat level of the Mahayana path of preparation through 
the end of the continuum as a sentient being. 
 
4.8 Because forms and so forth do not abide, 
Because of stopping the application in that, 
Because their thusness is profound, Because they are difficult to fathom,  
 
4.9 Because they are immeasurable, 
Because of realizing slowly with great difficulty, Because of prophecy, because of irreversibility, Definitely emerging, without interruption,  
 
4.10 Enlightenment, soon, 
Others welfare, no increase and no decrease, Not seeing qualities, non-qualities, and so forth, Not seeing inconceivable forms and so forth,  
 
4.11 Not conceiving forms and so forth, 
Their definitions, and their entities, Issuing forth a precious result, and That purity, along with boundaries.  
 
Topic 2: Principal Applications  91 
 There are twenty applications mentioned in the root text, which are divided into five 
groups: 
1) in relation to the nature of the applications 
2) in relation to the person who is the support of the application 3) in relation to the method of completing the applications 4) in relation to the result 5) in relation to the time 
1.  In relation to the nature of the applications 
The first five applications are posited on the path of preparation. 
1. Application of not abiding on forms and so forth This application realizes the non-true existence of the phenomena included in 
the bases, paths, and aspects and then meditates on that. 
2. Application that stops application 
This application stops grasping at the previous application as truly existent. 
3. Application in the profound 
This realizes as profound the thusness of form and so forth included in the 
bases. 
4. Application in the difficult to fathom 
This realizes as difficult to fathom the thusness of form and so forth included 
in the paths. 
5. Application in the immeasurable 
This realizes as immeasurable the thusness of form and so forth included in 
the aspects. 
 All phenomena are included in bases, paths, and aspects. In this context, bases can be understood as referring to the four noble truths and their sixteen attributes, or to the 108 phenomena from form through the exalted knower of all aspects. Paths refers to the fifteen paths of the three vehicles, and aspects refers to all aspects of the objects of knowledge. 
2.  In relation to the person who is the support of the application 
The next eight applications refer to the different applications that are developed in the continua of bodhisattvas at different levels of the path. There can be two types of persons who cultivate the applications.  
Topic 2: Principal Applications  92 
 6. Application that realizes emptiness over a long period of time and with great difficulty. 
 
 One type has not as yet realized emptiness with a wisdom arisen from meditation and, therefore, will develop great fear of emptiness. This person is of dull faculties and will generate a realization of the mother  the perfection of wisdom  only after a long period of time. The application of such a person is an application that realized emptiness over a long period of time and with great difficulty.  For those who have realized emptiness with a wisdom arisen from meditation, there will be no occasion for fear to arise toward emptiness. Applications of these individuals can be of two types: 
! application for ones own welfare  
! application for others welfare 
Application for ones own welfare 
7. Application of (one who) attains prophecy. This is the application of a bodhisattva 
on the heat level of the path of preparation who receives a prophecy of his 
future attainment of buddhahood from the buddha. 
8. Application of (one who) is irreversible.  This is an application of a bodhisattva on 
the peak level of the path of preparation that perfectly apprehends the mother 
through hearing, reflecting, and so forth due to being superior to the heat level. 
9. Application of definite emergence.  At this tolerance level of the path of 
preparation, a bodhisattva attains a stable realization of method and wisdom and thereby passes beyond the interferences of the mental engagements of hearers and solitary realizers that seek only their own welfare. 
10. Application without interruption.  On the occasion of the supreme mundane 
qualities level of the path of preparation, this application of such a bodhisattva acts as a direct cause of the path of seeing whereby one enters into direct perception of the ocean-like sphere of reality without any interruption. 
11. Application close to enlightenment.  This application of a bodhisattva on the path 
of seeing is close to enlightenment because it is an application that is the very basis of newly attaining the uncontaminated qualities of the Mahayana paths.  
12. Application of (one who) will soon become a buddha.  This application quickly 
achieves the resultant truth body. It is posited to be present in bodhisattvas 
Topic 2: Principal Applications  93 
 from the second through the seventh grounds. However, the boundaries of 
these applications are posited differently in different commentaries. 
Application for others welfare 
13. Application of others welfare.  This application thoroughly turns the wheel of 
Dharma for the welfare of others since, out of compassion, it is able to turn the wheel of Dharma for all three lineage holders. This application is posited for a bodhisattva of the eighth ground. In regard to this, one can ask: Can a bodhisattva on the eighth ground actually turn the wheel of Dharma? Although an eighth ground bodhisattva is able to turn the wheel of Dharma out of compassion, it is only a buddha who can turn the complete wheel of Dharma. 
3.  In relation to the method of completing the applications 
This includes the next four applications:  
14. Application of realizing that increase and decrease do not ultimately exist.  The 
bodhisattva realizes that ultimately there is no increase or decrease, but that conventionally faults and excellent qualities do decrease and increase respectively. All phenomena, from form through the exalted knower of all aspects, conventionally have occasions of increase and decrease, but not ultimately. 
15. Application of not seeing qualities and non-qualities as ultimate.  The bodhisattva 
does not see qualities and non-qualities as ultimately existing, although they are seen conventionally. Quality and non-quality are respectively positive and negative qualities. They are so called from the point of view of their result. If something brings a fruitional result of happiness, it is a positive quality, whereas if it brings a fruitional result of suffering, it is a negative quality. 
16. Application of not seeing inconceivable forms and so forth.  The bodhisattva does 
not conceive of the inconceivable aspects of forms and so forth as truly existent. This application can mean that forms and so forth are not conceivable ultimately in that they are beyond conception. 
17. Application of not conceiving the entities of form and so forth, their definitions, and 
their definienda as truly existent.  The bodhisattva does not conceive the true 
existence of the entities of the illustrations (forms and so forth), their 
definitions (suitable to be form and so forth), and their definienda. In 
Topic 2: Principal Applications  94 
 general, on the basis of a single object, there is an illustration, a definition, 
and a definiendum. For example, white color is an illustration of form, suitable to be form is the definition of form, and form is the definiendum.  
  These four applications are posited as exalted wisdoms of the eighth ground. 
4.  In relation to the result of the applications 
18. Application of issuing forth precious results.  This application bestows the results 
from stream enterer through the highest complete enlightenment. It is posited on the ninth ground. 
19. Application of complete purity.  This application completely purifies the 
conception of true existence of all phenomena. It is posited on the tenth ground. 
5.  In relation to the time 
20. Application along with boundaries.  A bodhisattva quickly cultivates the mothers 
without giving up strong effort in meditation, having divided the periods of time into cycles of days, months, and years. 
Conclusion 
In conclusion, the applications along with the suitable vessels for engaging in these applications are presented. In order to be one of the four types of persons to engage in the applications, one must develop the different qualities presented here, namely, a great accumulation of merit through serving the buddhas, being cared for by a virtuous friend, and so forth. These persons who are the support, or cultivator, of the applications have also been described as the support who accumulates roots of virtue, indicating the importance of building up the collection of merit. The applications themselves are presented through describing the nature of the applications, the applications at different levels of the bodhisattva path, the ways to complete the application, the beneficial results, and the relation with time.  
Topic 3: Excellent Qualities of the Applications 95 
 Topic 3:  Excellent Qualities  of the Applications  
 
   By the force of cultivating the Mahayana applications presented in the second topic, the bodhisattva will achieve fourteen types of excellent qualities. These excellent qualities are presented in order to inspire the practitioner to cultivate the applications.  
The definition of an excellent quality of a Mahayana application is  an excellent quality 
attained through the force of cultivating a Mahayana application that is the method 
for attaining it.64  
 
Boundaries : It exists from the Mahayana path of accumulation through the buddha 
ground. 
 
4.12ab Overcoming the strength of maras and so forth 
Are the fourteen types of excellent qualities.  
 Bodhisattvas who meditate very joyfully on the applications receive fourteen benefits, or excellent qualities: 
1. Overcoming the strength of the maras
65 by the force of cultivating an application.  
Through cultivating an application the bodhisattvas receive blessings from the buddhas, whereby they overcome the strength of the maras. There are four types of maras, but in general, maras can be any being who jealously hinders those who are cultivating a path. 
2. Authorization and being known by the buddhas. These bodhisattvas cultivate the 
applications with authorization from the buddhas. 
3. Being directly perceived by the buddhas.  These bodhisattvas applications become 
distinguished in dependence upon being directly perceived by the buddhas.  
4. Nearing complete enlightenment.  These bodhisattvas are very close to perfect 
complete enlightenment. 
Topic 3: Excellent Qualities of the Applications 96 
 5. Great meaning, great result, great benefit, great fruition, so forth.  These bodhisattvas 
possess the greatness of meaning of not being separated from the buddhas, 
the greatness of result of becoming a buddha, the greatness of benefit of taking rebirth in the happy migrations, and the greatness of fruition due to engaging in others welfare. 
6. Making use of places.  Due to having achieved the state of performing some 
activities of buddhas, such as teaching the perfection of wisdom to others and so forth, these bodhisattvas receive the benefit of receiving a prophecy from the tathagatas about the place where they will be reborn and so forth. 
7. Completing all uncontaminated qualities. Due to having attained the thorough 
completion of some types of qualities of the antidotes included in the path perfection of wisdom,
66 these bodhisattvas receive the benefit of completing 
all uncontaminated excellent qualities. 
8. Becoming one who propounds. Because of receiving advice from the buddhas 
regarding the exalted knower of all aspects, these bodhisattvas have attained the state of beings who propound the perfection of wisdom to others. 
9. Being indivisible by adversaries. Because they are presently cultivating the 
applications accompanied by the buddhas, these bodhisattvas cannot be separated from the path to the exalted knower of all aspects (omniscient mind) by maras. 
10. Generating uncommon roots of virtue.  Because they now develop extensive joy in 
cultivating the applications, these bodhisattvas generate roots of virtue that are not similar to those of hearers and solitary realizers, such as placing many beings in highest complete enlightenment. 
11. Accomplishing aims exactly as promised. Because the buddhas rejoice in the 
bodhisattvas promises to cultivate the perfection of wisdom, these bodhisattvas achieve the result of their promise of accomplishing the welfare of others. 
12. Thoroughly holding vast results.  These bodhisattvas obtain the vast results of great 
waves of virtue created in other lives because presently they have attained a strong desire for the profound Mahayana Dharma. 
13. Accomplishing the welfare of sentient beings. These bodhisattvas have attained the 
accomplishment of the welfare of sentient beings, such as giving away all their possessions in other lives, because presently they have attained the enactment 
Topic 3: Excellent Qualities of the Applications 97 
 of the welfare of sentient beings, such as placing many living beings in 
enlightenment. 
14. Definitely attaining the mother.  Because in this life these bodhisattvas have 
found the complete words and meanings of the perfection of wisdom, they will receive the definite attainment of the perfection of wisdom in a future life.  
  Thus, there are fourteen benefits or excellent qualities that a bodhisattva attains as a result of cultivating the Mahayana applications.  
Topic 4: Hindrances to the Applications  98 
 Topic 4: Hindrances to the Applications  
 
  There are forty-six hindrances that interfere with engaging in the twenty Mahayana applications. To put it simply, they hinder the trainee in realizing emptiness. Some of the hindrances impede the initial generation of the applications, some impede the continued abiding on the applications, and some impede one from the bringing the applications to completion. They are presented here in order for the trainee to identify and avoid them, enabling him to attain the applications. One becomes free from the hindrances upon reaching the eighth bodhisattva ground; prior to that, one is susceptible to them.  
The definition of a hindrance to the applications is  a maras action that interferes with the 
production, abidance, or completion of an application.67  
 
Boundaries : They exist from prior to entering the path through the seventh ground. 
 
4.12cd The hindrances are definitely to be understood as six 
Along with four sets of ten.  
 The forty-six hindrances are divided into three main categories: 
1. twenty adverse conditions to an application based upon oneself 
2. twenty-three incomplete conducive conditions based upon either oneself or 
others 
3. three adverse conditions based upon others 
1. Twenty adverse conditions to an application based upon oneself 
These twenty conditions obstruct ones engagement in the applications. They are 
based upon oneself alone. They are subdivided into four groups, those related to: 
a) self-confidence  
b) assuming negative states  c) causes of degenerating from the Mahayana  d) distraction from the Mahayana 
Topic 4: Hindrances to the Applications  99 
 a)  Self-confidence (2) 
There are two hindrances related to self-confidence: 
1. Attainment with great difficulty. Ascertaining the words and meanings of the 
perfection of wisdom after a long time with great difficulty. 
2. Sudden self-confidence. Being too quick to try to ascertain the words and 
meanings of the perfection of wisdom without first examining the reason for 
doing so, whereby one becomes tired and gives up. 
 
 These two types of self-confidence, that gained with difficulty over a long period 
of time and that arising extremely quickly or suddenly, are both due to maras.  
b)  Assuming negative states (3) 
There are three hindrances related to assuming negative states that pertain to ones study of the perfection of wisdom. 
3. Assuming negative physical states. Questioning the perfection of wisdom very 
apathetically without respect for oneself  for example, while yawning. 
4. Assuming negative mental states.  Questioning the perfection of wisdom with 
attachment, or becoming distracted away from ones virtuous spiritual friend while becoming attached to others. One then thinks that the experience of the perfection of wisdom does not exist and leaves ones meditation. 
5. Recitations which are made improperly. Reciting the perfection of wisdom with an 
improper motivation, such as wanting to receive offerings, respect, and so forth. 
c)  Causes of degenerating from the Mahayana (7) 
There are seven causes of degenerating from the Mahayana: 
6. Holding reasons for turning away from the mother . Expecting to receive a prophecy 
regarding ones enlightenment, when one does not receive a prophecy, one abandons listening to the perfection of wisdom. 
7. Degeneration of faith . A bodhisattva engages in listening to the perfection of 
wisdom, but then degenerates faith in the Mahayana scriptures, the cause for attaining enlightenment, due to having previously studied the Hinayana scriptures. Ones faith degenerates due to strongly settling on the causes of an exalted knower of all aspects as truly existent. 
Topic 4: Hindrances to the Applications  100 
 8. Degeneration in relishing the highly auspicious taste of the mother . One tastes the 
perfection of wisdoms high auspiciousness through study, but then loses this 
taste, studies the Hinayana, and does not attain enlightenment. 
9. Degeneration in continually upholding the supreme vehicle in all ways . This is to 
initially consider the mothers as supreme, but then to lose faith in them due to not understanding their meaning.  
10. Degeneration in the constant object of intent . One considers the Hinayana and the 
Mahayana to be equal, whereby one seeks to actualize the state of perfect complete enlightenment by means of the Hinayana.  
11. Degeneration of the relationship between cause and effect . One wishes to attain the 
effect, complete enlightenment, through merely practicing the Hinayana, which is not a cause that corresponds with the effect. 
12. Degeneration from the causes of the three bodies, of which there is no higher . There is 
no relationship between the causes of the three bodies and holding both the Mahayana and Hinayana baskets to be similar. This bodhisattva wrongly considers the Mahayana and Hinayana Dharma baskets to be similar, whereby he degenerates the causes for attaining enlightenment. 
d)  Distraction from the Mahayana (8) 
The following group lists the eight distractions from the Mahayana. 
13. Arising of an exuberance, which is a conception with respect to the many aspects of 
objects.  This is the arising of an exuberance, or self-confidence, that becomes 
distracted to beautiful forms, sounds, smells, etc., thinking that sense objects 
are best. Thus, one is distracted from the perfection of wisdom. 
14. Strongly settling on the mere written letters of the mother as being the mother . One 
considers the mere writing of the words of the perfection of wisdom to be the real perfection of wisdom. 
15. Settling on the non-existence of things that are dependent arisings as the meaning of the 
mode of abidance . This is to wrongly grasp at the non-existence of dependent 
arisings as the actual meaning of reality. 
16. Strongly settling on the letters as the mother . One strongly clings to the words of 
the perfection of wisdom as being the actual perfection of wisdom. 
17. Strongly settling on the non-existence of letters as the mother . Strongly clinging to 
that which is without words as the perfection of wisdom, thereby becoming 
distracted from the perfection of wisdom. 
Topic 4: Hindrances to the Applications  101 
 18. Interest in places and so forth . Being extremely attracted to different places, one 
is not satisfied to merely think about them; rather, this bodhisattva must 
actually go there. 
19. Relishing material gain, honor, and verses of praise . One relishes material gain, 
respect, and words of praise. 
20. Seeking skillful means in what are not paths . One seeks methods that are taught 
by the maras that are not actually paths that lead to buddhahood. 
 
2. Twenty-three incomplete conducive conditions based on either 
oneself or others 
This group of twenty-three are incomplete conducive conditions that interfere with practicing the trainings properly. They are divided into three groups: 
a) based on the master 
b) based on oneself c) based on both 
a)  Based on the master (14) 
These fourteen hindrances pertain to conditions where the master and the student 
are not concordant, whereby the student has virtuous qualities that are lacking in the master.  
21. Weakened by aspiration and indolence . The student strongly aspires to listen to 
teachings on the perfection of wisdom, while the teacher is indolent, not wanting to teach. 
22. Weakened by difference of desired places . The student wishes to listen in one 
place, while the master wishes to teach somewhere else. 
23. Weakened by small desires and desires that are not small . While the student has few 
desires, the master has strong desires. 
24. Having and lacking the excellent qualities of training . The student has the twelve 
excellent qualities of training regarding food, clothing, and dwelling, while the master does not. 
25. Having virtuous and not virtuous characters . The student possesses virtues, such 
as faith and so forth, while the master has a non-virtuous nature. The master is not willing to teach the perfection of wisdom. 
Topic 4: Hindrances to the Applications  102 
 26. Giving away and being miserly . The student is able to give away all possessions 
while the master is miserly. 
27. Being generous and not accepting . The student wants to give things to the master, 
while the master does not want to accept them. 
28. Understanding through terse statements and understanding through elaborate ones . 
While the student is able to understand the meaning by way of short 
explanations, the master likes to give extensive explanations due to his own lack of understanding and his own need for extensive explanations. 
29. Knowing the Dharma of the sutras well and poorly . The student knows the 
Dharma of the twelve branches of scripture, the sutras and so forth, well, while the master does not know them well. 
30. Having and lacking the six perfections . The student possesses the six perfections 
while the master does not. 
31. Being skilled in means and not skilled . The student is skilled in the means for 
becoming enlightened, while the master is not. 
32. Obtaining and not obtaining the retention of not forgetting . The student has 
obtained the retention that does not forget, while the master has not. 
33. Desiring and not desiring to write the letters . The student likes to write the words 
of the mother while the master does not. 
34. Separated from and not separated from desirous aspiration . The student is free from 
the five obstructionsharmful intent, desirous aspiration, lethargy and sleep, excitement and regret, and doubtwhile the master is not. 
 In short, the master and student do not have a common wish to be together, whereby the practice of Dharma degenerates. These are the incomplete conducive conditions based on the master. Because the master does not have particular qualities, the conducive conditions are incomplete. 
b)  Based on oneself (2) 
These two incomplete conducive conditions do not depend upon a master. 
35. Turning away from bad migrations . Rather than going to the lower realms in 
order to help others, one mentally turns away from going there after hearing disparaging talk about the hells and so forth. 
36. Pleasure in progressing to happy migrations . One craves rebirth in upper realms 
due to hearing admiring talk about the god realms and so forth. One should 
Topic 4: Hindrances to the Applications  103 
 not crave to be reborn in the upper realms since this is a hindrance to 
attaining liberation. 
c)  Based on both (7) 
In this case, the master and student are also not concordant. Here, the master is associated with the first quality, while the student is associated with the latter. However, the order could be reversed and would still be counted as a fault. 
37. Delighting in solitude and in company . The master wants to be around few 
people, while the student wants to be around many people. Or, the student wants to be around few people while the master likes to be around many. 
38. Not giving an opportunity and desiring to associate . The master does not want 
others to follow him while the student wants to follow others. 
39. Desiring only a few material possessions and not desiring to give . The master wants 
only a minimum of material possessions, while the student does not want to give them to him. 
40. Going where there will and will not be obstacles to life . The master wants to go 
where there is danger to his life, while the student does not. 
41. Going and not going where there is famine . The master wants to go where there is 
famine, but the student does not want to go there. 
42. Going and not going where there is trouble from bandits and so forth . The master 
wants to go where there is much commotion, such as bandits, thieves, and so forth, while the student does not want to go there. 
43. Being pleased and displeased upon seeing householders . The master generates 
mental happiness by seeing a householder who makes offerings, while the student does not. 
3. Three adverse conditions based on others 
The last three are conditions that are contradictory to the applications that are based on others. They depend on something other than the master and student. 
44. Maras try to separate people from the Dharma . The maras try to separate one from 
the Dharma by coming in the disguise of someone training in virtue and saying, As the mother arises from my basket, it does not arise from the three, the extensive, middling, and brief (mothers). 
Topic 4: Hindrances to the Applications  104 
 45. Bringing about a counterfeit mother . One practices a counterfeit perfection of 
wisdom that presents the meditation on repulsiveness as if it were a 
presentation of the meaning of reality. 
46. Developing faith for an improper object . One generates joy in taking an improper 
object, e.g., a mara appearing in the guise of a buddha, as an object of ones faith. 
 
 If one is free from all these hindrances, one will have proper guru devotion, the 
foundation of the path. Thus, the need for guru devotion is implicitly presented here. 
Conclusion 
In summary, there are forty-six hindrances that are obstacles to learning the Perfection of Wisdom and engaging in the Mahayana applications. There are twenty adverse conditions based on oneself, twenty-three incomplete conducive conditions based on either oneself or others, and three adverse conditions based on others. These forty-six hinder our ability to realize emptiness and progress along the Mahayana path. We should be aware of them and attempt to avoid them.
