Topic 9: Application of the Equality of Cyclic Existence and Peace 150 
 Topic 9:  Application of the Equality of Cyclic Existence 
and Peace 
 
   Having attained the signs of being irreversible from highest complete enlightenment, 
the bodhisattva must then exert effort in order to attain enlightenment. Upon 
reaching the eighth ground, the bodhisattva must train to attain the three bodies
89 
of a buddha  the truth body (Skt. dharmakaya ), the complete enjoyment body (Skt. 
sambhogakaya ), and the emanation body (Skt. nirmanakaya ). The  application of the 
equality of cyclic existence ( samsara ) and peace ( nirvana ) is the cause of the truth 
body. By way of realizing that cyclic existence and peace are equally empty of true existence, the bodhisattva achieves the application of the equality of cyclic existence and peace.  
 The truth body consists of two parts  a wisdom truth body (Skt. j
ñanakaya ) and a 
nature body (Skt. svabhavikakaya ). The wisdom truth body is the buddhas 
omniscient mind. The nature body is a final sphere that possesses the two purities, 
purity of natural stains and purity of adventitious stains. To put it simply, the nature body is the ultimate nature, or emptiness, of the omniscient mind.   The causes of the other two bodies of a buddha, the complete enjoyment body and the emanation body, are discussed in the tenth and eleventh topics.  
The definition of an application of the equality of cyclic existence and peace is  an exalted 
wisdom of the pure grounds that totally quells the opportunity for the generation of 
the manifest conception of true existence in states of subsequent attainment by way 
of being conjoined with the wisdom directly realizing the emptiness of true existence 
of cyclic existence and peace.90 
 
Boundaries : It exists on the three pure grounds.91 
 This topic will be described in two sections: 
1. The actual meaning 
2. Dispelling objections 
Topic 9: Application of the Equality of Cyclic Existence and Peace 151 
 1. The actual meaning 
4.60ab Because phenomena are like a dream  
Not conceiving existence and peace. 
 All phenomena are like a dream in being empty of true existence. Cyclic 
existence and peace ( nirvana ) are likewise not truly existent, and thus in this regard, 
cyclic existence and nirvana are equal. Maitreya uses the simile of a dream because 
the objects in a dream, although appearing to be real, are not actually real. Similarly, cyclic existence and peace do not exist in the way that they appear. They appear to exist truly or ultimately, but in fact, they do not. They exist conventionally, but they do not exist ultimately.
92 
 By means of the application of the equality of cyclic existence and peace, the bodhisattva on the eighth ground directly realizes that cyclic existence and peace are equally empty of true existence. As a result, he completely destroys the possibility of generating a manifest conception of true existence, even after arising from meditation. In other words, although this bodhisattva has not yet completely 
abandoned the seeds
93 of the conception of true existence in his mindstream, the 
conception grasping at true existence cannot arise once he reaches the eighth 
ground. According to the Madhyamaka-Svatantrika school, the seeds of the conception of true existence are not eliminated until one reaches the buddha ground. In contrast, the Madhyamaka-Prasangika school posits that these seeds are eliminated when one reaches the eighth ground.  To a bodhisattva on the seventh ground or lower, when conventionalities appear to the mental consciousness, they appear as truly existent; conventionalities rarely a p p e a r  l i k e  a n  i l l u s i o n  o r  l i k e  t h e  r e f l e c t i o n  o f  a  f a c e  i n  a  m i r r o r .  H o w e v e r ,  t o  a  bodhisattva on the eighth ground and above, conventionalities appear during subsequent attainment like an illusion or like the reflection of a face in a mirror.  
2. Dispelling objections 
4.60cd The replies to disputes such as the non-existence of actions 
And so forth have already been explained as set forth. 
 There are many misunderstandings about  Maitreyas statement that phenomena are 
like a dream (in the root text stanza 4.60ab). Some objections regarding this statement are included in the sutras; they are presented and refuted below:  
Topic 9: Application of the Equality of Cyclic Existence and Peace 152 
 Dispute 1: Rejecting the absurd consequence that there is no complete path of 
virtuous and non-virtuous karma.94  
Opponent:  If phenomena are like a dream, then virtuous and non-virtuous karma are 
not collected even during the day because karma and its results do not exist 
ultimately; they are empty like a dream.  
 
Reply (our system):  We accept that karma is not ultimately  created during the day. 
However, this does not mean that karma is not created during the day conventionally . 
Karma does conventionally exist; therefore, it is correct to say that karma is created 
conventionally during the day. 
Dispute 2: Rejecting the absurd consequence that there is a thoroughly complete 
path of action even in dreams.  
This dispute arises in reply to Subhutis statement: When someone kills during the day and kills in a dream, afterwards, if these are nourished by strong settling, then those two actions will similarly become paths of actions. How do you respond?  
Opponent: If that were so, then we would accumulate karma in a dream just as during 
the day. During a dream there would be a complete path of action of one who kills 
and one who is killed because the waking state and the dream states would be similar. There would be the absurd consequence that a fully ordained monk would lose his vows due to dreaming that he killed a man.  
Reply (our system):  This is not correct. The actions done during the waking state and 
those done during a dream are different in terms of heaviness. Although the karma created during a dream can become a path of action, it cannot become a complete  
path of action that includes the four factors  object, intention, commission, and 
completion. If a fully ordained monk dreams of killing someone, it is not a cause of breaking his vow because breaking this vow depends on formulated rules and, according to the Vinaya, a dream is similar to non-existence.   The karma created during a dream can become a virtuous or non-virtuous path of action if it is nourished by strong settling upon awakening. For example, if 
someone kills during a dream and rejoices in the killing upon awakening, then a 
non-virtuous path of action is committed. Similarly, if a person dreams of having protected someones life and rejoices upon awakening, this becomes a virtuous path of action. 
Topic 9: Application of the Equality of Cyclic Existence and Peace 153 
 Dispute 3: Rejecting the contradiction through scripture.  
Opponent:  In the sutras it says, Dreams are devoid of all actions and intentions, so 
it is incorrect to say that there is any collection of karma from dreams.  
 
Reply (our system): This is not contradictory because the sutra passage means that 
dreams are ultimately  devoid of all actions and intentions; however, conventionally  
actions and intentions do exist. It is clear that the opponent has misunderstood the 
purpose of this sutra passage.   In short, cyclic existence and peace are equally empty of true existence, like a dream. All of the above refutations are simply intended to negate any misconceptions of what phenomena are like a dream means. 
Conclusion 
The bodhisattva must exert effort to attain the wisdom truth body of a buddha. He does this by cultivating the application of the equality of cyclic existence and peace on the three pure grounds, which realizes that cyclic existence and peace are equally empty of true existence. By the force of this application, the manifest conception of true existence can no longer arise. 
Topic 10: Application in a Pure Land  154 
 Topic 10:  Application in a Pure Land 
 
   The tenth topic is application in a pure land, which is the cause for attaining the 
complete enjoyment body ( sambhogakaya ) of a buddha, the form body that possesses 
the five certainties.
95 By meditating on the equality of cyclic existence and peace, one 
purifies ones future pure buddha land where one will become a buddha. Thus, 
application in a pure land is mutually inclusive with application in the equality of cyclic existence and peace. Purifying ones buddha land actually refers to purifying ones own mind; it does not refer to purifying the external environment. By purifying our mind, we purify our buddha land.  
The definition of application in a pure land is  a yoga of the pure grounds empowered 
with the ability of causal roots of virtue, prayers and so forth, to establish a special 
buddha land where oneself will be buddha.96 
 
Boundaries : It exists on the three pure grounds. 
 
4.61 The world of sentient beings, likewise 
The world of the environment, are impure.  
Establishing them in the purity of that Is the purity of a buddha land. 
 
After attaining the eighth bodhisattva ground, the bodhisattva cultivates an application of a completely pure buddha land, which is the cause for establishing the special land where he will become a buddha. In dependence upon accumulating extensive roots of virtue, the bodhisattva purifies the two  the world of sentient beings and the world of the environment  in order to prepare the two types of buddha lands: 
1. World of sentient beings   In order to purify the world of sentient beings, the 
bodhisattva prays that sentient beings have the enjoyments of the gods, 
practice the ten virtues, love each other, train in the three vehicles with steady 
Topic 10: Application in a Pure Land  155 
 effort, develop the concentrations and formless absorptions, and abandon all 
wrong views. In this land, there will not even be the words lower realm, hunger, and thirst. Moreover, the bodhisattva practices the six perfections in order to purify the world of sentient beings. What is purified? The ten non-virtues, the five obscurations,
97 and wrong views. 
2. World of the environment   In order to purify the world of the environment to 
be free from stones, thorns, etc., the bodhisattva makes prayers and visualizes a pure golden ground that has many designs made with sapphires. This practice is similar to that of a tantric practitioner who visualizes the worlds inhabitants as deities and the environment as a pure mandala having the nature of the exalted wisdom of bliss and emptiness.  
  Purifying the buddha land in this way is the means of cultivating the application in a pure land. However, in addition to the above methods, one must practice the six perfections  generosity, ethics, patience, joyous effort, concentration, and wisdom  in order to accumulate the two collections and attain ones own pure buddha land. Actually, practicing the six perfections is the principal cause of attaining ones own pure buddha land; the above visualizations are merely secondary causes.  
Conclusion 
In short, one should practice the six perfections and make prayers to be born in a buddha land where there are no impurities as mentioned. This is the application in a pure land, which must be cultivated by a bodhisattva on the three pure grounds prior to actualizing buddhahood. It is the cause for actualizing the complete enjoyment body of a buddha.  
Topic 11: Application in Skillful Means  156 
 Topic 11 : Application in Skillful Means 
 
   In order to perform the deeds of a buddha in accordance with the various dispositions of sentient beings, a bodhisattva engages in the application in skillful means. This application serves mainly to create the imprint for the attainment of the 
emanation body ( nirmanakaya )
98 of a buddha. The application in skillful means is an 
application of the pure bodhisattva grounds that directly knows the proper and 
improper time to benefit sentient beings. It spontaneously accomplishes enlightened activities free from coarse exertion.   In comparison with a bodhisattva, all the enlightened activities of a buddha are spontaneous and effortless, carried out at the proper time and in complete accordance with the inclinations of sentient beings. 
 
The definition of an application in skillful means is  a yoga of the pure grounds that 
spontaneously accomplishes perfect enlightened activities in a manner of the 
quiescence of coarse exertion.99  
 
Boundaries : It exists on the three pure grounds. 
 
4.62 Objects along with this application: 
That passed beyond the enemies, 
Not abiding, according to the force, The characteristic of not being shared,    
4.63 Not attached, not observing, 
Signs, extinguishment of wishes, The signs of that, and immeasurable Are the ten types of skillful means.   
 
Topic 11: Application in Skillful Means  157 
  In the root text, objects refers to the objects of the application, which can be 
the three doors of liberation, the thirty-seven harmonies with enlightenment, the nine final absorptions, the ten powers, and the eighteen unshared qualities.
100 This 
application refers to the application in skillful means and the rest of the stanzas refer to the ten divisions of this application. These are explained below.   The application in skillful means 
1. that is victorious over the four maras . The exalted wisdom of a bodhisattva on 
the tenth ground surpasses the maras, such as the son of the gods because it surpasses any interference through the power of practice. 
2. that abides nominally and does not abide ultimately . The exalted wisdom of a 
bodhisattva on the tenth ground possesses the application of abiding nominally on all phenomena by not abiding ultimately because it perfectly meditates on all phenomena as equal in being empty of true existence. 
3. that enacts the welfare of others impelled by the force of previous prayers . The 
exalted wisdom of a bodhisattva on the tenth ground possesses that which propels it to accomplish others welfare by the force of former prayers of the bodhisattva because the prayers, such as May I enact others welfare spontaneously, have been accomplished. 
4. that is not shared . The exalted wisdom of a bodhisattva on the tenth ground 
possesses an application that is not shared with the hearers and solitary realizers because it has familiarized with all the difficult activities that enact others welfare. 
5. that is without a nature of attachment to true existence with respect to all 
phenomena . The exalted wisdom of a bodhisattva on the tenth ground 
possesses non-attachment, or non-grasping, at true existence since it is 
completely purified of the conception of true existence by having familiarized with all the positive qualities of the paths and grounds. 
6. that does not observe true existence . The exalted wisdom of a bodhisattva on the 
tenth ground possesses an application that does not observe the entity of the self of persons and of phenomena as truly existent because it has familiarized with the emptiness door to complete liberation for many countless eons. 
7. that is signless . The exalted wisdom of a bodhisattva on the tenth ground 
possesses an application that realizes the non-existence of signs of truly 
Topic 11: Application in Skillful Means  158 
 existent causes because it has familiarized with the signlessness door to 
complete liberation for many countless eons. 
8. that is wishless . The exalted wisdom of a bodhisattva on the tenth ground 
possesses an application that realizes the non-existence of wishes for truly existent results because it has familiarized with the wishlessness door to complete liberation for many countless eons. 
9. through the sign of irreversibility . The exalted wisdom of a bodhisattva on the 
tenth ground possesses skillful means regarding the signs of irreversibility because it possesses a special nominal ability to make others understand the bodhisattvas own irreversibility. When others who question his irreversibility request the bodhisattva, he can express the qualities of irreversibility that accord with his attainment of special signs of irreversibility. 
10.  that is immeasurable . The exalted wisdom of a bodhisattva on the tenth 
ground possesses skillful means of immeasurable realizations because it is skilled in the five sciences (medicine, logic, grammar, poetry, and inner science/philosophy) and has cultivated the meaning of reality for many countless eons whereby it knows the entire range of methods and can provide answers to questions. 
Conclusion 
In conclusion, the application in skillful means is the cause for actualizing the emanation body of a buddha. Such emanation bodies are manifested by the buddha for the sake of subduing sentient beings according to their individual inclinations. Bodhisattvas on the three pure grounds perform this application, which directly knows the proper time at which to benefit and the improper time at which not to benefit sentient beings.

Appendix 2: The Four Noble Truths  162 
 Appendix 2: The Four Noble Truths 
 
   Buddha Shakyamuni taught the four noble truths soon after his enlightenment, during the first turning of the wheel of Dharma. According to Svatantrika-
Madhyamaka, the four noble truths are called noble ( arya) truths because they are 
truths that are realized by superiors, beings who have perceived selflessness 
directly.
104  
 The Buddha taught that one should understand the four truths. True sufferings  
are to be known, true origins  are to be abandoned, true cessations are to be actualized, 
and true paths  are to be cultivated. Furthermore, the Buddha said that true sufferings 
are to be known but are not truly existent objects to be known. In other words, true sufferings are to be known although they are not to be conceived of as truly existent objects to be known. Likewise, true origins are to be abandoned but are not to be conceived of as truly existent objects to be abandoned. True cessations are to be actualized but are not to be conceived of as truly existent objects to be actualized. And true paths are to be cultivated but are not to be conceived of as truly existent objects to be cultivated.   The first two truths, true sufferings and true origins, are to be abandoned. They belong to the thoroughly afflicted class, or the class of cyclic existence. In contrast, the third and fourth truths, true cessations and true paths, are to be adopted. They belong to the completely pure class, the class of nirvana.  Within the thoroughly afflicted class, true origins are the causes that bind us to cyclic existence, and true sufferings are the results that are bound. In other words, true origins give rise to true sufferings. Contaminated actions and afflictions are the true origins that cause our suffering. Within the completely pure class, true paths are the means by which one is released from cyclic existence and reaches true cessations, the (nominal) result.
105 Thus, there are two sets of causes and results. 
 According to the order of cause and effect, the four noble truths should be presented in the following order: true origins, true sufferings, true paths, and true cessations. However, rather than presenting them in the order in which they arise, 
the Buddha presented the four truths in the order of their realization   true 
sufferings, true origins, true cessations, and true paths. In other words, the Buddha 
presented them in the order in which we need to understand or realize them. First, we recognize true sufferings, which prompt us to develop a strong wish to be free 
Appendix 2: The Four Noble Truths  163 
 from them. We then investigate the causes of these sufferings and come to 
understand that they are the result of contaminated karma and afflictions, true origins. Upon further reflection, we come to realize that true origins can be eliminated and true cessations of sufferings can be actualized. To actualize these true cessations, we must cultivate the causes, true paths. In this way, the four noble truths are posited.  
True Sufferings 
The definition of true sufferings is  a truth of the thoroughly afflicted class that is 
particularly distinguished by the four attributes of (1) impermanent, (2) miserable, 
(3) empty, and (4) selfless.106 
  In brief, true sufferings are the sufferings that occur due to the force of contaminated actions and afflictions, true origins. In order to realize true sufferings, one must meditate on the four attributes. 
 
Chart A2.1  The sixteen attributes of the four noble truths 
Four Truths 16 attributes  
True Sufferings 1. Impermanent 
2. Miserable 
3. Empty 4. Selfless 
True Origins 5. Cause 6. Source 
7. Strong Producer 
8. Causal condition (of suffering) 
True Cessations 9. Cessation 10. Pacification 
11. Highly auspicious 
12. Definitely liberating 
 True Paths 13. Path 14. Suitable 15. Achiever 
16. Deliverer 
 
The four attributes of true sufferings are as follows: 
1. Impermanent  is that which is momentary, changing moment by moment. It has 
two types: coarse and subtle. An illustration of coarse impermanence is the 
breaking of a cup or the death of our bodies. An illustration of subtle impermanence is the momentary disintegration of the cup.  
Appendix 2: The Four Noble Truths  164 
 2. Miserable  includes three types: the suffering of suffering, the suffering of 
change, and pervasive compounded suffering. An illustration of the suffering 
of suffering is kidney pain. An illustration of the suffering of change is the enjoyment of tasting good food that turns into suffering when we eat too much. An illustration of pervasive compounded suffering is our contaminated aggregates caused by karma and afflictions. 
3. Empty  refers to being empty of a self that is a different entity from the 
aggregates. This refutes the non-Buddhist assertion that posits a self separate from the body and mind. Such a self is empty of being permanent, unitary, and independent. According to Svatantrika-Madhyamaka, this is the coarse selflessness of persons.  
4. Selfless , or the lack of a self of persons that is the same entity as the aggregates, 
refers to the absence of a self-sufficient, substantially existent self. Such a self-sufficient, substantially existent self would be the controller of the aggregates. According to Svatantrika-Madhyamaka, this is the subtle selflessness of persons; in Prasangika-Madhyamaka, it is the coarse selflessness of persons. 
(See the Overview of Svatantrika Tenets section, pp. 2530, for more details.)  
 
 For example, our five contaminated aggregates are true sufferings because they 
have the four attributes. They are impermanent  because they arise occasionally. They 
are miserable  because they are under the control of karma and afflictions. They are 
empty  and selfless  because they lack a self that is a different entity from the aggregates 
as well as a self-sufficient, substantially existent self that is the same entity as the 
aggregates.  
True Origins 
The definition of true origins is  a truth of the thoroughly afflicted class that is 
particularly distinguished by the four attributes of: (1) cause, (2) source, (3) strong 
producer, and (4) causal condition [of suffering].107 
  True origins are the causes of suffering. They are the afflictions and the contaminated actions that result from the afflictions.    The four attributes of true origins are as follows: 
1. Cause   Afflictions and contaminated actions are causes of suffering because 
they are the root of suffering. Craving, the eighth link of the twelve links of dependent arising, is an example because craving, in conjunction with 
Appendix 2: The Four Noble Truths  165 
 grasping, nourishes the projecting actions that bring about rebirth in cyclic 
existence. 
2. Source   Afflictions and contaminated actions are sources of suffering because 
they repeatedly produce suffering as their result. 
3. Strong producer   Afflictions and contaminated actions are strong producers of 
suffering in that they give rise to suffering with great force. 
4. Causal condition   Afflictions and contaminated actions are causal conditions 
of suffering because they are cooperative conditions that give rise to suffering. 
True Cessations 
The definition of true cessations is  a truth of the completely pure class that is 
particularly distinguished by the four attributes of: (1) cessation, (2) pacification, (3) 
highly auspicious, and (4) definitely liberating.108 
  A true cessation is the removal forever of an object of abandonment, either an afflictive obstruction or a knowledge obstruction. Therefore, it is not merely a temporary abandonment, but an eradication of an object of abandonment such that it will never arise again. A true cessation is attained by means of an uninterrupted path, a wisdom consciousness that directly realizes selflessness. In other words, a true cessation is attained by means of a true path.   The four attributes of true cessations are as follows: 
1. Cessation   True cessations are cessations because they are a state of freedom 
in which suffering has been extinguished by the power of an antidote. 
2. Pacification   This refers to the pacification of sufferings and afflictive 
emotions. True cessations are pacification because they are a state of freedom in which the afflictions have been abandoned. 
3. Highly auspicious   True cessations are highly auspicious because they are a 
liberation in which benefit and happiness arise. 
4. Definitely liberating   True cessations are definitely liberating because they are a 
liberation in which all sufferings have been abandoned such that they cannot rise again. 
Appendix 2: The Four Noble Truths  166 
 True Paths 
The definition of true paths is  a truth of the completely pure class that is particularly 
distinguished by the four attributes of: (1) path, (2) suitable, (3) achiever, and (4) 
deliverer.109 
 
 A true path is the wisdom directly realizing selflessness, which, according to Svatantrika, can be either the lack of a self-sufficient, substantially existent self (selflessness of persons) or the non-true existence (selflessness of phenomena) of the person or phenomena.
110 Because a true path is a direct realization of selflessness 
without the medium of a meaning generality, it first arises on the path of seeing. There are three divisions of true paths: the path of seeing, the path of meditation, and the path of no more learning.   The four attributes of true paths are as follows: 
1. Path  True paths, such as the wisdom directly realizing selflessness, are clear 
realizers, or paths, that lead to liberation. 
2. Suitable  True paths are suitable as a direct antidote to the afflictions. 
3. Achiever   True paths are achievers because they are exalted wisdoms that 
directly and unmistakenly realize the final reality of the mind. 
4. Deliverer   True paths are deliverers because they are antidotes that act to 
completely extinguish suffering and deliver one to irreversible liberation. 
Conclusion 
The four noble truths should be understood as similar to recovery from a sickness. 
As it says in Maitreyas Uttaratantra : The sickness is to be known, the cause is to be 
abandoned, well-being is to be actualized, and the medicine is to be applied. 
Similarly, true sufferings are to be known, true origins are to be abandoned, true cessations are to be actualized, and true paths are to be applied. Whenever we experience pain, we should first recognize it, then identify its causes, and then, with the wish to be free from it, look for a method to become free from it. Therefore, we should meditate on these four noble truths in order to achieve liberation from cyclic existence.
111  
Appendix 3: Differences between Svatantrika and Prasangika-Madhyamika 167 
 Appendix 3: Differences between Svatantrika and 
Prasangika-Madhyamaka 
 
   
Ornament  is generally studied in relation to Yogachara-Svatantrika-Madhyamaka 
tenets. While Yogachara-Svatantrika assertions agree for the most part with 
Prasangika, which is regarded as the highest school by the Gelug tradition, there are a few differences between the two schools. Moreover, teachings of the stages of the 
path ( lam rim ) or thought transformation ( blo sbyong , pronounced lo jong ), which are 
frequently taught in many Tibetan Buddhist centers, are usually presented in terms 
of the Prasangika view. In order to prevent any possible misunderstanding or confusion, some of the main differences of tenets between these two schools are 
explained below.  
Assertions regarding the obstructions 
! The conception of true existence along with its seeds is a knowledge obstruction 
according to Yogachara-Svatantrika, but according to Prasangika, it is included in afflictive obstructions. The Prasangika posits the latencies left by the conception of true existence as knowledge obstructions. These latencies give rise to the mistaken dualistic appearance of true existence and are usually compared to the smell of garlic remaining in a pot when the garlic has been removed from it. 
 
! Yogachara-Svatantrika asserts a coarse knowledge obstruction, the conception of subject and object being different substantial entities, that is abandoned by solitary realizer foe destroyers. Prasangika does not accept this. 
 
! Yogachara-Svatantrika identifies the conception of a self-sufficient, substantially 
existent person as the main obstruction to be abandoned for the attainment of 
liberation. It is not necessary to abandon the conception of true existence, which is a knowledge obstruction, in order to attain liberation. However, Prasangika asserts that the conception of true existence has to be abandoned in order to attain liberation; thus, all hearer, solitary realizer, and bodhisattva superiors must, therefore, realize the emptiness of true existence. 
Appendix 3: Differences between Svatantrika and Prasangika-Madhyamika 168 
 Chart A3.1  The two obstructions according to the two Madhyamaka schools 
 Yogachara-Svatantrika 
Madhyamaka  Prasangika Madhyamaka  
Afflictive 
obstructions  Conception of a self of persons and 
afflictions along with their seeds Conception of a self of persons and phenomena, as well as the afflictions 
along with their seeds 
Knowledge 
obstructions  Conception of self of phenomena along with its seeds Latencies of mistaken dualistic appearances 
 
! In Yogachara-Svatantrika, both afflictive and knowledge obstructions are 
abandoned simultaneously beginning from the Mahayana path of seeing through the tenth bodhisattva ground. When the subtlest afflictive and knowledge obstructions are abandoned on the tenth ground, buddhahood is attained. However, in Prasangika, the abandonment of the two obstructions occurs sequentially. All afflictive obstructions are abandoned from the path of seeing through the seventh bodhisattva ground, after which the knowledge obstructions are abandoned on the three pure bodhisattva grounds, i.e., grounds eight through ten.

and small-small innate AO

Appendix 3: Differences between Svatantrika and Prasangika-Madhyamika 169 
 Assertions regarding the two selflessnesses 
! According to Yogachara-Svatantrika, the coarse selflessness of persons is the 
emptiness of a permanent, unitary, and independent person, while the subtle selflessness of persons is the emptiness of a self-sufficient, substantially existent person. The coarse selflessness of phenomena is the emptiness of subject and object being different substantial entities, while the subtle one is the emptiness of true existence. Prasangika, instead, asserts the emptiness of true existence as the subtle selflessness of both persons and phenomena. Prasangika regards the subtle selflessness of persons of the Yogachara-Svatantrika as only a coarse selflessness of persons. 
 
! For Yogachara-Svatantrika, the measure of true existence is an object established from its own side by way of its own uncommon mode of subsistence without being posited by the force of appearing to a non-defective awareness. Yogachara-Svatantrikas assert that phenomena do not exist in this way. They do assert, however, that phenomena exist inherently. For Prasangikas, inherent existence and true existence are synonymous. These terms refer to existence by way of its own character and nothing whatsoever exists in this way. 
 
Chart A3.3  Selflessness according to the two Madhyamaka schools 
 Yogachara-Svatantrika 
Madhyamaka  Prasangika 
 Madhyamaka  
 
Coarse selflessness 
of persons 
  
Emptiness of a permanent, unitary, independent self  Emptiness of a self-sufficient, substantially existent self 
 
Subtle selflessness 
of persons 
  Emptiness of a self-sufficient, substantially existent self  Emptiness of an inherently existent person 
 
Coarse selflessness 
of phenomena 
  Emptiness of subject and object being different substantial entities  None 
 
Subtle selflessness 
of phenomena 
  Emptiness of true existence  Emptiness of inherently existent phenomena 
 
Appendix 3: Differences between Svatantrika and Prasangika-Madhyamika 170 
 Assertions regarding objects 
! Yogachara-Svatantrikas maintain that any established base (i.e., object) necessarily 
exists by way of its own character, because regarding any phenomenon, if the imputed object is sought, it is findable. Prasangikas assert just the opposite; they maintain that when an imputed object or phenomenon is sought, it cannot be found within its basis of imputation and thus does not exist by way of its own character. 
 
! All four noble truths − true sufferings, true origins, true cessations, and true 
paths − are conventional truths in Yogachara-Svatantrika. Prasangika, however, 
holds that true cessations are an ultimate truth, whereas the other three are conventional truths. 
 
! Yogachara-Svatantrika-Madhyamaka asserts that there are no external objects; in other words, the object and the subject (the mind) are not different substantial entities. Both the subject and the object arise from the same latency that is stored within the mind. They assert that a belief in external objects is a coarse knowledge obstruction and that it is abandoned by solitary realizers. However, Prasangika asserts that external objects do exist and that objects along with the consciousness perceiving them do not arise from a latency stored in the mind. 
 
Appendix 4: Four Close Placements of Mindfulness 171 
 Appendix 4: A Presentation of the Close Placements of 
Mindfulness 
 
by Gyalwa Chökyi Gyaltsen 
 
rGyal ba chos gyi rgyal mtshan gyis brtsom pa dran pa nyer bzhag bzhugs. 
 
   There are eight parts to this final analysis of the close placements of mindfulness: object of observation, manners of meditation, reasons for mediating, entity, boundaries, divisions, etymology, and demonstrating that the close placements of mindfulness of the Mahayana is superior.  
1. Objects of Observation 
There are four objects of the close placements of mindfulness: bodies, feelings, minds, and phenomena.
 
 There are three bodies: the outer body of forms that are not bases of the sense powers such as sound; the inner body of the five sense powers such as the eye sense power; and the body that is both outer and inner such as the forms that serve as bases of the sense powers (i.e. the sense organs). There are three feelings: pleasant, unpleasant, and neutral. Minds refer to primary consciousness (such as eye consciousness and so forth), and phenomena refer to all mental factors that are not feelings and all non-associated compositional factors and unproduced phenomena. 
Compendium of Knowledge  states: 
 
What are the objects of observation of the close placements of mindfulness: bodies, feelings, minds, and phenomena? 
 The reason why these four are posited as the objects of observation is so the 
immature will abandon grasping at the bodies as the dwelling place of the self; the feelings as the source of enjoyments of the self, the minds as the thing which is the self, and phenomena such as attachment as afflicting and faith as purifying the self. 
Compendium of Knowledge states: 
 
Appendix 4: Four Close Placements of Mindfulness 172 
 The thing that is the dwelling place of the self, the bases of resources 
for the self, the thing that is the self, and things that afflict and purify the self. 
 
2. Manners of Meditation 
There are two manners of meditation: common and uncommon. The first manner, common, is done by investigating both the general and specific characteristics of bodies, feelings, minds, and phenomena. 
 
 Treasury of Knowledge  states: 
 
One should meditate the close placements of mindfulness by 
thoroughly investigating the two characteristics of bodies, feelings, minds, and phenomena. 
 
Moreover, impermanence, suffering, empty, and selfless are the general characteristics. They are explained as general characteristics since they are pervasive qualities applied to particular bases. That is to say: all products are impermanent; all that is contaminated is suffering; and all phenomena are empty and selfless. 
Commentary to Treasury of Knowledge  (states): 
 
Their specific characteristics are their individual entity. The general characteristics are that all products are impermanent; all that is contaminated is suffering; and all phenomena are empty and selfless. 
 
Hence, the specific characteristics with regard to bodies are the bodies elements and evolutes; with regard to feelings the entity of experience; with regard to minds the entity of observer; and with regard to phenomena such as the mental factors, each has its own entity. The above examples are merely indicative. 
The second manner of meditation, uncommon, has three parts: objects of 
observation, mental engagement, and attainment. 
The first, objects of observation: hearers and solitary realizers observe only their 
own bodies and so forth, while bodhisattvas observe the bodies of both themselves and others. 
The second, mental engagement: Hearers and Solitary Realizers mentally engage 
impermanence, and so forth, while bodhisattvas meditate on the non-observable sign of phenomena. 
Appendix 4: Four Close Placements of Mindfulness 173 
 The third, attainment: hearers and solitary realizers meditate solely to be free 
from the contaminated body and so forth, whereas bodhisattvas do not meditate for 
the sake of freedom or non-freedom from that, but in order to achieve a non-abiding nirvana. 
 
3. Reasons for Meditating 
The reasons for meditating in such a way is to engage into what is to adopted and rejected with regard to the four truths. 
Through meditating close placements of mindfulness of bodies you will 
understand that the appropriated pernicious state of the contaminated body is the entity of suffering. 
Because pleasant and unpleasant feelings are the causes of cravings which desire 
non-separation and separation and since, considered from the point of view of results, cravings are the principal origin of suffering, through meditating close placements of mindfulness of feelings, you will act to abandon them.  Through meditating close placements of mindfulness of minds the basis of self-
grasping, the mind, is analyzed as impermanent and so forth, thereby stopping the conception of that. Then, due to the absence of fear of the annihilation of the self, you will actualize cessation. 
Through meditating repulsiveness and close placements of mindfulness of 
phenomena you will understand that all thoroughly afflicted phenomena are incompatible and all pure phenomena are antidotes for that. 
Through understanding that these very [meditations] are the means for turning 
away from harm and knowing how to develop them, you will enter into the Four Truths. 
 
It is stated in Discrimination of the Middle Way and the Extremes : 
 
Because being the pernicious state, 
Because being the cause of craving, Because being the basis, And because being non-obscuration, Are the entry into the four truths, Meditate the close placements of mindfulness.  
Appendix 4: Four Close Placements of Mindfulness 174 
 4. Entity 
The definition of the close placements of mindfulness is: an exalted knower having 
entered a path which is included in either a meditative mindfulness or wisdom 
upon having examined both the general and specific characteristics of bodies, 
feelings, minds, and phenomena.  
 
 Compendium of Knowledge  states: What is the entity? It is wisdom and 
mindfulness. And Treasury of Knowledge  states: The close placements of 
mindfulness are wisdom. 
 
5. Divisions 
When close placements of mindfulness are divided, there are four: the close placements of mindfulness on bodies and so forth.  
6. Boundaries 
The close placements of mindfulness exist from the path of accumulation through the buddha ground.  
7. Etymology 
Through mindfulness an object of observation of wisdom is placed near, that is, it is not forgotten, therefore [this practice] is termed the close placements of mindfulness.  
8. Demonstrating That the Close Placements of Mindfulness of the 
Mahayana Are Superior 
The close placements of mindfulness of the Mahayana are superior to the close 
placements of mindfulness of the Hinayana because meditation on them is superior in fourteen ways: 
1) Its aim is the Mahayana. 2) It relies upon wisdom. 3) It acts as an antidote to the sixteen mistaken views. 4) It engages you in meditating upon the four truths. 
Appendix 4: Four Close Placements of Mindfulness 175 
 5) It observes bodies and so forth of yourself and others. 
6) It mentally engages bodies and so forth as empty. 7) Having separated yourself from the contaminated body, an 
uncontaminated body is attained. 
8) It accords with the practices of the six perfections. 9) It accords with leading hearers, solitary realizers, and so forth. 10) It understands bodies to be like illusions, feelings to be like dreams, 
minds to be like space, and phenomena to be like clouds. 
11) In accordance with your intentions, you will be reborn in cyclic existence 
as a wheel-turning monarch and so forth. 
12) If you meditate the close placements of mindfulness, you will naturally 
have sharp faculties. 
13) Its meditation is not mixed with the aspiration and so forth of the 
Hinayana. 
14) You will come to achieve a nirvana without remainder. 
 
 It is stated in Ornament for the Mahayana Sutras : 
 
Since the wise are dissimilar In fourteen ways In meditating the close placements of mindfulness, They are superior to others. 
 
And it is stated: 
 Reliance and antidote, Similarly, engagement, Aim, mental engagement,  Attainment, superiority of meditation, Accords with followers, Thorough understanding, rebirth, Greatness, superiority, Meditation, and perfect achievement. 
 
I have merely indicated [the presentation of the close placements of 
mindfulness]. It should be understood in terms of other, more extensive explanations.   
Translated into English by Jampa Gendun relying on previous versions.

Appendix 6: Supplementary Topic on the Four Maras 177 
 Appendix 6: Supplementary Topic: An Explanation of the 
Four Maras 
 
   There are four maras taught in the scriptures: (1) mara of aggregates, (2) mara of 
afflictions, (3) mara of the lord of death, and (4) mara of the sons of the gods. They 
are explained as follows: 
1. The mara of aggregates  is defined as a category of the four maras that is either 
the contaminated aggregates arising in dependence upon their cause, actions and afflictions, or that necessarily depends on a level of the latency of ignorance.
113 It is divided into coarse and subtle. The coarse mara of 
aggregates refers to the five appropriated aggregates, while the subtle is the mental body that arises in dependence upon a level of the latency of ignorance, which is possessed by Hinayana foe destroyers. 
2. The mara of afflictions  is defined as a category of the four maras abiding in a 
class of obstructions that mainly hinders the attainment of liberation. The coarse mara of afflictions is the six root and twenty secondary afflictions, while the subtle mara is the seeds of afflictive obstructions. 
3. The mara of the lord of death  is defined as a category of the four maras abiding 
in a class of factors that uncontrollably stops the life force. The coarse mara of the lord of death is a factor that uncontrollably stops the power of life through karma and afflictions. The subtle mara refers to the inconceivable death of the high bodhisattvas. It is so called because we cannot understand how they die. 
4. The mara of the sons of the gods  is defined as a category of the four maras that 
hinders passing beyond the three other maras and is included in a class of desire realm gods. 
  These four maras are posited in relation to death: that which dies (mara of aggregates), the means to die (mara of afflictions), death itself (mara of the lord of death) and that which hinders passing beyond death (mara of the sons of the gods). They are known as maras because they interfere with passing beyond sorrow (attaining nirvana). 
Appendix 6: Supplementary Topic on the Four Maras 178 
  Those following the Hinayana surpass the mara of the sons of the gods upon 
attaining the path of seeing. They surpass the mara of afflictions when they attain the state of a foe destroyer. When they are liberated from the two obstructions (the obstructions to absorption and the obstructions to liberation), they pass beyond the mara of the lord of death. When they pass into remainderless nirvana, they have surpassed the mara of aggregates. For those following Mahayana, upon attaining a sign of irreversibility, they surpass the mara of the sons of the gods. Upon attaining the eighth ground, bodhisattvas surpass the coarse form of the other three maras. With the attainment of buddhahood, all maras are surpassed in their entirety.

Appendix 8: Answer Key for Sample Quiz Questions  
 189 
Appendix 8:  Answer Key for Sample Quiz Questions  
Topic 1  Subject-Aspects of the Antidote Class  
1. What is an aspect in general? 
In general an aspect is an attribute of a phenomenon. Whatever is an existent is 
necessarily an aspect.  
2. What is an aspect in the context of the application in complete aspects, and what 
are the different divisions and the number of aspects included in each? 
In the context of the application in complete aspects aspect refers to a subject-aspect of the antidote class. This is an exalted knower that is able to overcome its own 
discordant class. There are three divisions: 
(1) subject-aspects of the knower of bases  27 aspects divided in terms of the four 
noble truths; 
(2) subject-aspects of the knower of paths  36 aspects divided in terms of the four 
noble truths; and 
(3) subject-aspects of the exalted knower of all aspects  110 aspects, of which 37 are 
common to hearers, 34 are common to bodhisattvas and 39 are unique to buddhas.  
3. Describe the four ways of meditating on the 173 aspects. 
(1) Meditation in which a belief is taken to mind. Here one is incapable of generating in 
the mind the character of the object meditated exactly as it is, but generates merely a 
similitude of it. 
(2) Meditation in which the mind is generated into the entity of the object meditated 
exactly as it is. 
(3) Meditation in which the character of an aspect is analyzed. The characters of 
higher qualities, which are objects to be attained later, are taken to mind individually 
identifying them as this is that and are analyzed with wisdom. 
(4) Meditation on an aspect of a wish. This is taking to mind by way of a wish thinking, 
May I attain the four fearlessnesses of a buddha! 
4. How does a subject-aspect of the antidote class overcome the discordant class in 
the case of misconceiving true sufferings to be permanent? 
The exalted wisdom in the continuum of a bodhisattva that directly realizes true 
sufferings such as the aggregates to be impermanent acts as an antidote to the 
superimposition of permanence on the aggregates. It opposes the misconception 
apprehending the aggregates as permanent through the clear appearance of 
impermanent aggregates.  
Appendix 8: Answer Key for Sample Quiz Questions  
 190 
Aspects of a knower of bases  
5. How are the subject-aspects of the knower of bases categorized with respect to the 
Four Noble Truths? 
The 27 subject-aspects of the knower of bases are categorized as follows: true 
sufferings4 aspects, true origins4 aspects, true cessations4 aspects and true 
paths15 aspects.  
6. Name the three categories of the aspects of true paths of the knower of bases and 
the obstructions that are abandoned by them. 
(1) An uncontaminated path of seeing that is an antidote to the afflictive obstructions. 
(2) A contaminated path of meditation that is an antidote to the coarse knowledge 
obstructions, the conception of forms and so forth as external objects. 
(3) An uncontaminated path of seeing that is an antidote to the coarse knowledge 
obstructions, the conception of forms and so forth as external objects.  
7. With one of the five similes from the aspects of true path that are contaminated 
paths of meditation, explain what is the realization that serves as an antidote to 
the coarse knowledge obstructions. 
The realization of forms and so forth not having the entity of being external objects (or 
the realization of emptiness of subject and object as different substantial entities) 
realizes forms and so forth are like dreams, since they are mere appearances that 
arise from latencies. Just as the dream mansion, elephant etc. appear to exist in a dream but do not, likewise, forms and so forth appear to exist externally but they do 
not. 
Aspects of a knower of paths  
8. What is the order of presentation of the Four Noble Truths in relation to the 
subject-aspects of the knower of paths? Why are they presented in this order? 
The Four Noble Truths are presented in the order of cause and effect: (1) true origins, 
(2) true paths, (3) true sufferings and (4) true cessations. This is to show the forward order of entering into cyclic existence in dependence on the thoroughly afflicted, true 
origins being the cause and true sufferings being the effect. It also shows the reverse 
order for exiting from cyclic existence in dependence on the completely pure, true 
paths being the cause and true cessations being the nominal effect.  
9. How are the subject-aspects of the knower of paths categorized with respect to  the 
Four Noble Truths and how are these aspects presented in the Ornament ? 
(1) True origins - 8 aspects are shown from the point of view of antidotes and 
abandonments. 
(2) True paths - 7 aspects are shown by means of thesis and proof. 
Appendix 8: Answer Key for Sample Quiz Questions  
 191 
(3) True sufferings - 5 aspects are shown by means of specific and general 
characteristics. 
(4) True cessations - 16 aspects are shown by means of the beneficial qualities of 
cessation. 
Aspects of an exalted knower of all aspects  
10. Describe how the 110 subject-aspects of an exalted knower of all aspects are 
categorized. 
The 110 subject-aspects of an exalted knower of all aspects are categorized into 3 divisions: 
(1) 37 aspects harmonious with hearers, 
(2) 34 aspects harmonious with bodhisattvas, and 
(3) 39 aspects unique to buddhas. 
Those aspects harmonious with hearers or bodhisattvas refer to similar aspects that 
are also generated in the continua of hearers and solitary realizers or bodhisattvas. 
However, those unique to buddhas are not found in the continua of beings  
11. Name the seven groups of the aspects of an exalted knower of all aspects 
harmonious with hearers. 
(1) Four close placements of mindfulness. (2) Four thorough abandonings. 
(3) Four legs of magical manifestation. 
(4) Five powers. 
(5) Five forces. (6) Seven branches of enlightenment. 
(7) Eight branches of a superiors path. 
12. Explain what is a close placement of mindfulness and what are the objects of the 
close placements of mindfulness. 
A close placement of mindfulness is an exalted knower engaged in a path, which is included in either mindfulness or wisdom, that meditates by way of investigating each 
of the four 
− bodies, feelings, minds and phenomena − in terms of both specific and 
general characteristics. 
13. Explain the manner of meditating on the close placements of mindfulness. 
This is done by investigating both the general and specific characteristics of the 
objects of observation. The general characteristics are impermanence, suffering, 
empty, selfless and empty of true existence which are applicable for all objects. The 
specific characteristics are to investigate the body as being impure, feelings as suffering, mind as being impermanent, and phenomena as selfless. 
14. How is the meditation on close placements of mindfulness related to engaging in 
the Four Noble Truths? 
Appendix 8: Answer Key for Sample Quiz Questions  
 192 
The meditation on the close placement of mindfulness on bodies is related to engaging 
in true sufferings, that on feelings is related to engaging in true origins, that on minds is 
related to engaging in true cessations while that on phenomena is related to engaging 
in true paths.  
15. Describe the four thorough abandonings. Why are the generation and increase of 
virtues also given the name abandoning? 
The four thorough abandonings are the effort that acts to: 
(1) abandon non-virtues already generated 
(2) not generate non-virtues not yet generated 
(3) increase virtues already generated 
(4) generate virtues not yet generated 
 
They are all called abandonings since even the development of virtues involves the 
process of abandoning their respective discordant classes. 
16. Name the five faults that hinder the development of a single-pointed mind of 
calm abiding and their respective antidotes. 
(1) Laziness is counteracted by (a) faith, (b) aspiration, (c) effort and (d) pliancy. 
(2) Forgetfulness is counteracted by (e) mindfulness. 
(3) Laxity and excitement is counteracted by (f) introspection. 
(4) Non-application is counteracted by (g) intention. 
(5) Over-application is counteracted by (h) equanimity.  
17. Describe the five powers. How do the five powers differ from the five forces? 
The power of faith is a conviction with regard to the four noble truths. The power of 
effort is an effort that delights in discarding true sufferings and true origins, and 
adopting true cessations and true paths. The power of mindfulness is an apprehension 
that does not forget the observed objects and aspects of the four noble truths. The 
power of meditative stabilization is a single-pointed mind that focuses on the four noble 
truths without distraction. The power of wisdom is a wisdom that discriminates the mode of abidance of the four noble truths.  
18. Name the seven branches of enlightenment and the eight branches of a 
superiors path. 
The seven branches of enlightenment are: 
(1) correct mindfulness, (2) fine discrimination of phenomena, (3) effort, (4) joy, (5) 
pliancy, (6) meditative stabilization, and (7) equanimity. 
The eight branches of a superiors path are: 
(1) Correct view, (2) correct realization, (3) correct speech, (4) correct aims of action, 
(5) correct livelihood, (6) correct effort, (7) correct mindfulness, (8) correct meditative stabilization. 
Appendix 8: Answer Key for Sample Quiz Questions  
 193 
Aspects of a an knower of all aspects harmonious with bodhisattvas 
19. The thirty-four aspects of an exalted knower of all aspects similar to those of 
bodhisattvas are divided into six groups. List these six groups and explain the 
meaning of similar to those of bodhisattvas.  
(1) Three paths of antidotes 
(2) Three paths of manifestation 
(3) Five paths that abide in happiness in this present life 
(4) Nine supramundane paths 
(5) Four paths of abandoning (6) Ten paths of buddhahood 
Similitudes of these 34 aspects of an exalted knower of all aspects are also generated 
in the continuum of a bodhisattva. Hence they are called those similar to those of 
bodhisattvas. 
20. Give the definition of the path of antidote as taught here. What is the 
superimposition mentioned in the definition and how is it eliminated by means 
of the path of antidote? 
A path of antidote is an exalted wisdom distinguished by the ability to destroy the 
superimposition which is its own object to be eliminated by way of realizing the non-
existence of the referent object of the superimposition to be eliminated.  
The superimposition is a wrong conception that conceives of a non-existent referent 
object, i.e., a self, to exist. 
The path of antidote is the wisdom consciousness that directly realizes the non-
existence of the referent object of that superimposition, whereby the superimposition, 
the conception of self, is eliminated. 
21. Explain the three paths of antidote as taught here. 
(1) The emptiness door of liberation  is the exalted wisdom that directly realizes 
selflessness in the continuum of a bodhisattva superior as the entity of the 
attributes of empty and selfless of true sufferings because it is a direct antidote to 
the view holding a self to exist either as the same entity or as a different entity as the aggregates, 
(2) The signlessness door of liberation  is the exalted wisdom that directly realizes 
the eight entities of the aspects of true cessations and true paths because it is a direct antidote to the improper conception grasping at a sign having as its causal factor a view of self. 
The wishlessness door of liberation is the exalted wisdom directly realizing the first two attributes of true sufferings (impermanent and miserable) and the four attributes of true 
origins. It is an antidote to the wish that considers the realms within cyclic existence to 
be a final object to be attained. 
Appendix 8: Answer Key for Sample Quiz Questions  
 194 
22. Explain the three paths of antidotes that are not explicitly taught here, that is, the 
three doors of liberation. 
Within a Prasangika context, 
Emptiness   the emptiness of inherent existence of phenomena 
Signlessness   the emptiness of inherent existence of phenomena from the point of 
view of its not having been produced from inherently existent causes 
Wishlessness  - the emptiness of inherent existence of phenomena from the point of 
view of its not producing inherently existent effects.  
23. Give the definition of paths of manifestation as taught here. 
A  path of manifestation is  an exalted knower distinguished by (1) freedom from 
certain types of obstructions to absorption in dependence on a final concentration that 
is its empowering condition, and (2) the ability to display various transformed 
manifestations. 
24. Briefly describe each of the three paths of manifestation. 
(1) The liberation of the embodied looking at form  the yogi emanates many forms while his own body remains visible to others. 
(2) The liberation of the formless looking at form  the yogi emanates many forms while his own body is invisible to others 
(3) The liberation of the attractive which eliminates obstacles  all forms appear to the 
yogi as having the single taste of being attractive, having eliminated the fault of 
liking attractive forms and disliking unattractive forms.  
25. Give the definition and divisions of the path for abiding in happiness in this 
present life. Why are they known as liberations? 
A path that abides in happiness in the present life : an exalted knower not polluted 
by coarse afflictions in the continuum of a person who has attained a liberation. 
There are five divisions:  (1) The liberation of the absorption of infinite space 
(2) The liberation of the absorption of infinite consciousness 
(3) The liberation of the absorption of nothingness 
(4) The liberation of the absorption of peak of cyclic existence 
(5) The liberation of cessation 
The first four liberations are called liberations because they act as causes of the path 
to liberation and because they are free of the manifest afflictions of their respective 
levels. 
The fifth liberation, the liberation of cessation, is called a liberation because it is free 
from the obstructions to the cessation of coarse feelings and discriminations. 
Appendix 8: Answer Key for Sample Quiz Questions  
 195 
26. Briefly describe the four liberations. 
The first four liberations act as a cause of the path to liberation, are liberations of a 
formless absorption, and are the nature of the path that abides accordingly without 
being polluted by afflictions of their own level. Through them, one experiences the bliss 
of the respective level. 
27. Briefly describe the fifth liberation. 
The fifth liberation, the liberation of cessation, liberates or frees one from the obstruction to cessations that are gross discriminations and feelings. With the attainment of it, one has achieved the cessation of these gross discriminations and 
feelings. 
28. What are the different reasons hearers and solitary realizers and bodhisattvas 
absorb in these liberations? 
The hearers and solitary realizers absorb into them in order to abide in happiness in the present life, while the bodhisattvas absorb into these only for the sake of 
demonstrating them to others.   
29. What are the obstructions that are abandoned by these liberations? 
For the first four liberations, the obstructions to experiencing the bliss of the respective levels of formless absorption. 
For the liberation of cessation, the obstructions are manifest attachment and hatred 
which arise from gross feelings and discriminations. 
30. Name the nine supramundane paths and explain why they are called 
supramundane. 
(1-4) the four concentrations that are supramundane paths (5-8) the four formless absorptions that are supramundane paths 
(9) the absorption of cessation. 
Although in general the four concentrations and four formless absorptions are not 
necessarily supramundane paths, in this context they are called supramundane 
because they can been transformed into the entity of a supramundane path. There is 
debate about whether the nine serial absorptions are supramundane paths or not. 
Terminologically they are supramundane paths. 
The absorption of cessation is a non-associated compositional factor and cannot be 
generated into a path. However, it is called a supramundane path because it is attained by relying upon a supramundane path.  
31. Describe the absorption of cessation as defined by the Prasangika Madhyamikas. 
According to Prasangika Madhyamika, it is an exalted wisdom of single-pointed meditative equipoise on reality (dharmata) that abides in a type free from attachment 
to the peak of cyclic existence in dependence on a supramundane path which attains it 
and an actual mind of the peak of cyclic existence. 
Appendix 8: Answer Key for Sample Quiz Questions  
 196 
32. With respect to the absorption of cessation according to the Svatantrika 
Madhyamikas: 
(1) What is its definition? 
An absorption of cessation is a non-associated compositional factor that is an 
absorption in the continuum of a superior, abiding in a type distinguished by 
the cessation of the coarse objects to be stopped along with their 
accompaniments on the subtle mental consciousness, the basis of the 
cessation. 
In short, the absorption of cessation in Svatantrika is the cessation of the 
arising of coarse feelings and discriminations achieved by means of stopping 
the coarse minds and their accompanying mental factors. 
(2) What are the objects to be stopped in order to attain it? 
With the absorption of cessation, the coarse minds and mental factors are 
ceased, whereby the accompanying coarse feelings and discriminations are 
also ceased.  
(3) How does it differ from the absorption of cessation according to 
Prasangika?  
In brief, according to Svatantrika, the absorption of cessation is a non-associated compositional factor, while Prasangikas posit it as an exalted wisdom, a consciousness. 
33. What is the pervasion between a non-associated compositional factor and a path? 
Mutually exclusive. There is nothing that is both. A path is necessarily a consciousness, while a non-associated compositional factor is necessarily NOT a 
consciousness. (There is more information in Collected Topics).  
34. What are the four characteristics of the paths of abandonment as presented here? 
What are the four types? 
The four characteristics are: 1. Being a dharma forbearance 
2. Having an aspect of an uninterrupted path 3. Being an object-possessor (subject or consciousness) that is included in the four 
noble truths, the objects 
4. Not being associated with the thoroughly afflicted  
The four types are: 
1. Dharma forbearance of true sufferings 2. Dharma forbearance of true origins 
3. Dharma forbearance of true paths 
4. Dharma forbearance of true cessations 
35. Give the definition of the path of buddhahood and explain Svatantrikas 
assertion regarding whether a bodhisattva can attain the fully qualified ten 
perfections. 
Appendix 8: Answer Key for Sample Quiz Questions  
 197 
A path of buddhahood is an exalted knower abiding in a type of path that is a means 
for progressing to the buddha ground  
According to Svatantrika, the ten fully qualified perfections only exist on the buddha 
ground. Therefore, only buddhas have the fully qualified ten perfections. Bodhisattvas, 
however, attain a surpassing practice of each of the ten perfections on the respective 
ten grounds.A path of buddhahood is an exalted knower abiding in a type of path that is a means for progressing to the buddha ground  
36. Name the ten paths of buddhas and give a brief description for each. 
1. Perfection of generosity  the intention to give 2. Perfection of ethics  the intention to abandon misdeeds 
3. Perfection of patience/tolerance  an undisturbed mind 
4. Perfection of joyous effort  a delight in virtue 
5. Perfection of concentration  a mind single-pointedly focused on a virtuous object 
6 . Perfection of wisdom  that which discriminated phenomena 7. Perfection of method  that which issues forth great results with little difficulty 
8. Perfection of wishes/prayers  in general, a wish to accomplish something 
9. Perfection of power  strength gained to not be overcome by the discordant class 
10. Perfection of exalted wisdom  that which understands exactly the presentation of 
the way in which phenomena are posited.  
Uncommon aspects of an exalted knower of all aspects  
37. Give the definition of the subject-aspect of an exalted knower of all aspects. Why 
are they called uncommon aspects? 
It is a final subject-aspect that is attained by the force of cultivating the trainings of the exalted knower of all aspects which is its method of attainment being the ability to fulfill 
completely the wishes of buddha superiors and their children. 
They are called uncommon aspects because they are fully perfected final exalted 
wisdoms of buddha superiors and are not found in the continua of lower superiors.
