40. With respect to the following four assertions, identify which of the four 
fearlessnesses with which they correspond:  
(1) Afflictions are obstacles to liberation and knowledge obstructions are 
obstacles to omniscience. 
Fearlessness with regard to indicating hindering phenomena.  
(2) I have completely abandoned all obstructions. I am fully enlightened. 
Fearlessness with regard to perfection abandonment.  
(3) I am completely and perfectly enlightened with respect to all 
phenomena. 
Fearlessness with regard to perfect realization.  
(4) The knower of all and the knower of paths are the paths of deliverances 
and they lead to liberation and omniscience. 
Fearlessness with regard to teaching the path of definite emergence.. 
41. Name the four individual correct knowledges and briefly explain what is known 
by each of them. 
Individual correct knowledge of phenomena   knowing the enumeration of names 
(or synonyms) of thoroughly afflicted and completely pure phenomena. 
Individual correct knowledge of meanings   knowing the general and specific 
characteristics of phenomena. Individual correct knowledge of definite terminology   knowing native languages 
of all sentient beings. 
Individual correct knowledge of self-assurance   self-assurance regarding the 
divisions of entities and aspects of phenomena. A buddha teaches these without any 
fear of making a mistake.  
42. The eighteen unshared attributes of a buddha can be grouped into four. Name 
these four groups and give an example for each of them. 
1) Six included in behavior: e.g., unmistaken bodily behavior 
2) Six included in realization: e.g., non-decrease in aspiration 
3) Three related to enlightened activities: e.g., physical activities of walking, sitting, 
lying, and standing 
4) Three included in exalted wisdom: e.g., unimpeded unobstructed exalted wisdom 
regarding the past.  
Appendix 8: Answer Key for Sample Quiz Questions  
 200 
43. Which of the aspects of the three exalted knowers are explained to be 
contaminated and which are uncontaminated? Explain why they are posited in 
this way. 
The 27 aspects of the knower of bases in the continuum of learner hearer superiors 
are said to be uncontaminated because hearers take afflictive obstructions as their 
main object of abandonment. 
The 27 aspects of the knower of bases and the 36 aspects of the knower of paths in 
the continuum of bodhisattva superiors are said to be contaminated by afflictions 
because bodhisattvas do not take afflictive obstructions as their main object of 
abandonment. 
The 110 aspects of an exalted knower of all aspects are only uncontaminated because 
a buddha superior has abandoned all types of afflictive and knowledge obstructions 
together with their latencies.  
Topic 2  Principal Applications  
1. Describe the four types of persons who are vessels that engage in the applications. 
(1) The one who listens to the Perfection of Wisdom Sutras . 
(2) The one who memorizes and remembers the words of the Perfection of Wisdom 
Sutras . 
(3) The one who retains the meanings of the words of the Perfection of Wisdom 
Sutras . 
(4) The one who properly reflects on the meanings and also practices them  
2. What qualities do they possess in order to become a vessel for the Perfection of 
Wisdom? 
This person (1) has served the buddhas by showing respect to numberless buddhas in the past, (2) has sowed roots of virtue in relation to the buddhas through making 
offering and so forth, as well as (3) being cared for by virtuous spiritual friends.  
3. Briefly explain what is the meaning of application used in this context. 
It refers to a bodhisattvas yoga that is conjoined with a union of calm abiding and special insight observing emptiness. The word conjoined denotes that this bodhisattvas yoga is accompanied by or influenced by the union of calm abiding and 
special insight observing emptiness.  
4. Among the 20 applications listed, what is the difference between the 19 that are 
principal and the one that is not? Name and describe this last application. 
The main difference is that the 19 principal applications exist from the heat level of Mahayana path of preparation where the bodhisattva has already attained a wisdom 
arisen from meditation realizing emptiness, but not for the last applicationthe 
Appendix 8: Answer Key for Sample Quiz Questions  
 201 
application that realizes emptiness over a long period of time and with great difficulty
because it is an application of a dull-faculty bodhisattva. This person generated fear of 
emptiness and will only generate a realization of the perfection of wisdom after a long 
period of time.  
Topic 3  Excellent Qualities of the Applications  
1. Give the definition, boundaries and number of divisions of the excellent quality 
of the application. 
Definition of excellent quality of a Mahayana application : an excellent quality 
attained through the force of cultivating a Mahayana application which is the method for attaining it.  
Boundaries : It exists from the Mahayana path of accumulation through the buddha 
ground. 
There are fourteen divisions, or excellent qualities.  
2. Why are the excellent qualities presented after the applications? 
The excellent qualities are presented after the applications in order to inspire one to 
cultivate the applications.  
3. Give a brief description of three of these excellent qualities which you feel will 
inspire others to engage in the applications. 
(1) Overcoming the strength of the maras by the force of cultivating an application. Through cultivating an application the bodhisattvas receive blessings from the 
buddhas, whereby they overcome the strength of the maras.  
(7) Completing all uncontaminated qualities  Due to having attained the thorough 
completion of some types of qualities of the antidotes included in the path perfection of 
wisdom, these bodhisattvas receive the benefit of completing all uncontaminated 
excellent qualities.  
(13) Accomplishing the welfare of sentient beings  they also accomplish the welfare 
of sentient beings, such as placing beings in enlightenment.  
Topic 4  Hindrances to the Applications  
1. What are the definition and boundaries of the hindrances to the applications?  
Definition of hindrances to the applications : a maras action that interferes with the 
production, abidance, or completion of an application.  
Boundaries : They exist from prior to entering the path through the seventh ground.  
Appendix 8: Answer Key for Sample Quiz Questions  
 202 
2. How many hindrances are there, and what are the main categories in which they 
are subsumed? Briefly describe each of the main categories and give an example 
from each category. 
The 46 hindrances are subsumed in three main divisions: 20 adverse conditions to 
an application based upon oneself, 23 incomplete conducive conditions based upon 
either oneself or others, and three adverse conditions based upon others.  
• The 20 adverse conditions to an application based upon oneself  hinder 
ones engagement in the applications and are based on oneself alone. There are 
four subdivisions: 1) self-confidence, 2) assuming negative states, 3) causes of 
degenerating from the Mahayana, 4) distraction from the Mahayana. For example, 
sudden self-confidence is a hindrance because it does not last due to arising 
without proper examination. 
• The 23 incomplete conducive conditions based upon either oneself or 
others refer to the incomplete cooperative conditions that interfere with ones 
practicing the applications. Within this, there are three groups: those based on the 
master, based on oneself, and based on both. For example, a hindrance is having 
a master who is indolent while the student is not. 
• The three adverse conditions based upon others  are conditions that are 
contradictory to the applications that are based on others. They depend on 
something other than the master and student. For example, maras trying to 
separate people from the Dharma.  
3. Why are the hindrances to the applications presented after the excellent qualities 
of the applications? 
After the excellent qualities are explained, the hindrances are presented  in order for 
the trainee to identify and avoid these hindrances, enabling him to attain the 
applications.  
Topic 5  Characteristics of the Applications  
1. Explain how a characteristic is related to that which is characterized and the 
illustration. 
A characteristic helps in understanding that which is characterized, taking the 
illustration as the basis. For example, taking the fire from burning grass as an 
illustration, we come to know fire, that which is characterized, as having the 
characteristic of hot and burning.  
2. What is a yoga of path perfection of wisdom? 
It is a bodhisattvas yoga which is a union of calm abiding and special insight that is the means for attaining a resultant perfection of wisdom, which is its object of attainment.  
3. Name the four types of characteristics and what they individually characterize. 
Appendix 8: Answer Key for Sample Quiz Questions  
 203 
(1) Knowledge characteristics  characterize the applications of each of the three 
exalted knowers in relation to what is known by them. 
(2) Differentiating characteristics  characterize the applications as superior to the 
applications of hearers and solitary realizers. (3) Functional characteristics  characterize the applications as having the special 
function of bringing about the welfare of sentient beings. 
(4) Entity characteristics  are the actual applications and are the illustrations for the 
other characteristics.  
Topic 6  Mahayana Partial Concordance with Liberation  
1. Give the definition and boundaries for the Mahayana partial concordance with 
liberation explicitly taught in this context.  
The definition of partial concordance with liberation explicitly indicated in this case 
is a clear realizer of doctrine of a bodhisattva skilled in achieving an exalted knower of 
all aspects in his own continuum.  
Boundaries : It is only on the Mahayana path of accumulation. 
It is also called a mahayana path of accumulation.  
2. What is another name for the Mahayana partial concordance with liberation? 
Explain the etymology of the term partial concordance with liberation.  
It is also called a mahayana path of accumulation. The path of accumulation is a path that is partially concordant or harmonious with the 
attainment of liberation and, therefore, is called partial concordance with liberation. It 
is the initial path to liberation.  
3. List and briefly describe the five bindings or main factors that constitute the skill 
of the bodhisattva on the path of accumulation. 
A bodhisattva is said to be skilled in practicing the six perfections in dependence on the five factors: 
1) Faith  that observes the Mahayana paths and results, the buddha and so forth 
2) Effort  that delights in all, generosity and so forth 
3) Mindfulness  that is included in mind generationthe perfect intention 
4) Meditative  equipoise  that does not conceptualize with respect to phenomena 
5) Wisdom  that thoroughly knows all aspects of phenomena (or complete knowledge 
of all phenomena)  
4. Describe two ways to differentiate the followers of faith and followers of Dharma. 
The measure of one being a follower of Dharma or a follower of faith depends on the 
strength of the five factors described above. If the five factors are strong, one is a 
follower of Dharma, whereas if they are weak, one is a follower of faith. Followers of 
Appendix 8: Answer Key for Sample Quiz Questions  
 204 
Dharma are also quick with regard to their speed of analysis; they follow the teachings 
only after examining and analyzing the content of what has been taught. In contrast, 
followers of faith are generally slower in analysis; they follow the teachings only out of 
faith, without analyzing the teachings. In general, a trainee intent on Ornament  is a 
follower of Dharma.  
Topic 7  Mahayana Partial Concordance with Definite Discrimination  
1. What is another name for the Mahayana partial concordance with definite 
discrimination? 
It is also called the Mahayana path of preparation. Definite discrimination refers to a definite discrimination of the four noble truths, that is a direct realization of them. The 
path of preparation is partially concordant with that definite discrimination of the four 
noble truths.  
2. Give the definition and boundaries of the Mahayana partial concordance with 
definite discrimination explicitly taught in this context. 
The definition of Mahayana partial concordance with definite discrimination 
explicitly indicated in this case is a bodhisattvas clear realizer of meaning explicitly 
indicated in this case, qualified mainly by a factor of method. 
Boundaries : It is only on the Mahayana path of preparation  
3. What is the observed object of the subsequent attainment of the path of 
preparation?  
The observed object of bodhisattvas of the Mahayana path of preparation during 
subsequent attainment is all sentient beings.  
4. How many subject-aspects are posited for the heat level and give three examples. 
There are ten aspects generated on the heat level. For example, a bodhisattva on the 
small heat level generates even-mindedness or equanimity and a mind of love. The 
bodhisattva on the middling heat level generates a mind of benefit, of non-anger, and 
of non-harmfulness.  
5. The remaining nine subject-aspects ranging over the upper three levels of the 
path of preparation can all be described by a similar set of three factors. What is 
this general set of three factors and apply it using an example. 
For each of the last three levels of the path of preparationpeak, forbearance, and 
supreme mundane qualitiesthere are three subject-aspects, in total making nine aspects on these three levels: 
(1). The mind of placing others in positive activities. 
(2). Verbally expressing praise for those who engage in these without being exhorted 
to do so. 
(3). The mind of rejoicing in those who act in accordance with the previous two 
aspects. 
Appendix 8: Answer Key for Sample Quiz Questions  
 205 
For example, a bodhisattva on the middling peak level: 
(1). Places others in positive activities by practicing generosity and exhorts others to 
do the same. 
(2). Praises those who engage in the practice of generosity 
(3). Rejoices in those who practice generosity.  
Topic 8  Signs of Irreversibility  
1. What is the meaning of irreversibility in this context? How can one infer the 
attainment of irreversibility? 
Irreversibility refers to being irreversible from attaining the highest complete 
enlightenment. It can be understood from two perspectives: 
• From the point of view of thought , one has eliminated the opportunity for the 
arising of the manifest thought striving for the liberation, which is ones welfare, 
alone. 
• From the point of view of conduct , one possesses a stable realization of 
special method and knowledge. 
We can infer the attainment of irreversibility by the reason of possessing the powerful 
causes for enlightenment. This can be inferred from the signs of possessing special 
method and wisdom as well as having eliminated the occasion of manifesting the 
thought of striving for ones welfare alone.  
2. If someone is irreversible from highest, complete enlightenment, is he necessarily 
one who possesses the sign of irreversibility? Explain your answer. 
If someone is irreversible from highest, complete enlightenment, it is not necessary for him to have manifested any of the 40 signs. For example, a bodhisattva on the great level of the Mahayana path of accumulation is irreversible from enlightenment but he 
does not yet possess the sign of irreversibility.  
3. What is a sign of irreversibility? What are the division of the bodhisattva sangha 
who has attained the sign of irreversibility? 
A sign of irreversibility is a perfect proof which serves to convince someone that the person who possess such a sign is irreversible from highest, complete enlightenment. 
The bodhisattva sangha  who has attained any of the 44 signs of irreversibility are 
divided into three groups: 
(1) Bodhisattvas of sharp faculty who manifest the signs of irreversibility on the 
Mahayana path of preparation. 
(2) Bodhisattvas of middling faculty who manifest the signs of irreversibility on the 
Mahayana path of seeing. 
Appendix 8: Answer Key for Sample Quiz Questions  
 206 
(3) Bodhisattvas of dull faculty who manifest the signs of irreversibility on the 
Mahayana path of preparation.  
4. In regard to the signs of irreversibility of one on the path of seeing, why are they 
designated as the 16 moments of the path of seeing? Can the 16 moments 
themselves be the actual signs? Explain your answer. 
The purpose of calling the special signs of body and speech, which are the signs of 
irreversibility, the 16 moments of the path of seeing is to show that the signs arise due 
to these 16 moments of the meditative equipoise of the path of seeing. This being a 
case of giving the name of the cause to the result. However, the 16 moments, being 
the exalted wisdom of meditative equipoise, are not the actual signs of irreversibility 
because they cannot be perceived by others who have inferior realizations and therefore cannot be signs for them to realize that this bodhisattva is irreversible.  
5a.Why is the path of meditation described as profound? 
The path of meditation is described as profound because it has a profound mode of abiding, which is thusness. This mode of abiding is profound because it is a sphere of 
reality that is free from the extremes of superimposition and deprecation. 
In another way, the path of meditation is profound because it directly realizes the 
profound, that is, thusness.  
5b.What is the meaning of these lines from the  Ornament : The path of meditation is 
that which / Repeatedly reflects, assesses, and definitely investigates . 
The path of meditation familiarizes with the realization of emptiness of true existence attained on the paths of preparation, seeing and meditation. Repeatedly reflects 
means to analyze the teachings with reasonings after having studied them, and it 
refers to the wisdoms arisen from hearing and reflecting. Assesses means to see the 
reflected meaning with a mind of calm abiding. And definitely investigates is to 
investigate the meaning with special insight and this refers to the wisdom arisen from meditation 
8. How is the path of meditation divided in relation to its objects of abandonment? 
The path of meditation is divided into small, middling and great levels, each of which is further sub-divided into small, middling and great, making a total of nine divisions. 
These nine divisions are generated starting from the small-of-the-small through the 
great-of-the-great level. These oppose and abandon the nine divisions of the objects of abandonment in the order beginning from the great-of-the-great through the small-of- 
the-small level of the objects of abandonment.  
9. What is the objection to the division of the path of meditation as definitely nine 
and how is this refuted? 
Objection: The path of meditation needs not have definitely nine divisions because it is stated in sutra that countless, immeasurable results and merits are produced from 
each division of the path of meditation. 
Appendix 8: Answer Key for Sample Quiz Questions  
 207 
Response: Although ultimately the results of the path of meditation do not exist, 
conventionally each of the nine divisions of the path of meditation can yield countless 
results. Therefore there is no necessity for the path of meditation to not have definitely 
nine divisions, even though the results arising from the path of meditation are 
countless.  
8a.A challenger objects that the path of meditation will be unable to perform its 
function if it does not exist ultimately. Explain the basis upon which this objection is made. 
The opponent is holding the two truths, conventional and ultimate truths, to be 
contradictory. He holds that if something does not exist ultimately, it does not exist at 
all, and if something merely exists conventionally, it is only an imaginary and is unable to perform a function. He holds that if the path of meditation does not exist ultimately 
and merely exists conventionally, it cannot act to abandon the objects of abandonment 
and progress to the attainment of enlightenment. 
8b.How is this refuted using the example of enlightenment? 
Just as the exalted wisdom of highest complete enlightenment does not exist ultimately, but still it conventionally enacts the welfare of sentient beings, similarly, the path of meditation does not exist ultimately but still it acts to decrease the objects of 
abandonment and increase the strength of the antidotes.  
9a.In relation to enlightenment, describe the three possible ways in which former 
and latter moments of mind generation may produce enlightenment and why they are not able to do so, according to the disputant. 
The disputant holds that even conventionally the former and latter moments of mind 
generation are unable to produce enlightenment because: 
(1) a former or latter moment of mind generation alone does not produce 
enlightenment because all the causes of enlightenment do not appear to each of them. 
(2) former and latter moments of mind generation do not produce enlightenment 
simultaneously because it is impossible for all of them to arise simultaneously. 
(3) former and latter moments of mind generation do not produce enlightenment 
sequentially because as soon as the latter arises, the former has ceased.  
9b.With the analogy of a butter lamp explain how former and latter moments of 
mind generation can bring forth the result of enlightenment . 
If such former and latter moments of mind generation are not truly existent, then 
through their mutual reliance with former and latter moments arising consecutively, their result of highest complete enlightenment is produced. This is like the mutual 
reliance of the former and latter moments of a butter lamp that act to consume the wick 
and the oil. 
Appendix 8: Answer Key for Sample Quiz Questions  
 208 
Topic 9  Application in the Equality of Cyclic Existence and Peace  
1. Give the definition, boundaries, divisions, and principal result of training in the 
equality of existence and peace. 
Definition of application of the equality of cyclic existence and peace : an exalted 
wisdom of the pure grounds that totally quells the opportunity for the generation of the 
manifest conception of true existence in states of subsequent attainment by way of 
being conjoined with the wisdom directly realizing the emptiness of the true existence of cyclic existence and peace. 
Boundaries : it exists on the three pure grounds. 
Divisions : there are three: the exalted wisdoms of the three pure grounds that directly 
realize emptiness. 
Its principal result  is the truth body.  
2. What is meant by the equality of cyclic existence and peace in this context? 
Cyclic existence and peace, or nirvana, are equal in the sense that they are equally 
empty of true existence. 
The Ornament of Clear Realizations  says: 
Not conceiving existence and peace 
Because phenomena are like a dream. The replies to the disputes of the non-existence of karma And so forth are explained. 
3. What is meant by phenomena are like a dream for an eighth ground 
bodhisattva? Does this experience occur in meditative equipoise or subsequent 
attainment? 
Just as in a dream, things do not actually exist as they appear. Likewise, although all 
phenomena appear to be truly existent, the eighth ground bodhisattva due to his 
realization of emptiness understands that they do not exist as such. This experience 
occurs in the subsequent attainment because in meditative equipoise only emptiness 
appears.  
4. Respond to the following disputes with accept, reason not established, and no 
pervasion in relation to the above quoted root text and explain your answers.  
a .  I t  f o l l o w s  t h a t  e v e n  d u r i n g  t h e  d a y  i t  i s  i n c o r r e c t  t h a t  v i r t u o u s  a n d  n o n -
virtuous karma are accumulated ultimately  because karma and results do not 
ultimately exist, like a dream. 
Accept.  The reason and the pervasion are established: karma and results do not 
ultimately exist, and if they do not ultimately exist it is incorrect that virtuous and 
non-virtuous karma are accumulated ultimately.  
Appendix 8: Answer Key for Sample Quiz Questions  
 209 
b .  I t  f o llo w s t h at  e ve n  d uri n g  t h e  d ay i t  i s i n co r re ct  t h at  vi rt u o us an d  n o n -
virtuous karma are accumulated conventionally  because karma and results do 
not ultimately exist, like a dream. 
No pervasion . If something does not ultimately exist, it is not pervaded by being  
(it is not necessarily) a non-existent. Therefore, even though karma and results 
do not ultimately exist, it is correct to posit the conventional accumulation of virtuous and non-virtuous karma.  
c. It follows that during a dream there is a complete path of action of killing 
because the waking state and dream state are similar.  
No pervasion.  Although waking and dream states are similar from the point of 
view of their respective objects not existing in the way that they appear, the 
action of killing in a dream is not a complete path of action due to the absence of 
one or more of the four factors which determine a complete path of action  object, intention, commission, and completion. Such an action only becomes a 
path of action when upon waking from the dream it is nourished by strong 
settling.  
Topic 10  Application in a Pure Land  
1. Give the definition, boundaries, and principal result of an application in a pure 
land. 
Definition of application in a pure land : a yoga of the pure grounds empowered with 
the ability of causal roots of virtue, prayers and so forth, to establish a special buddha field where oneself will be buddha.  
Boundaries : it exists on the three pure grounds. 
Its principal result is the complete enjoyment body.  
2. Describe how a bodhisattva trains in purifying the world of sentient beings and 
the environment. What is the principal practice in which she must engage to 
actualize this pure land? 
A bodhisattva purifies the world of sentient beings  by visualizing them as having the 
enjoyments of the gods as an antidote to impurities such as hunger, thirst, and so on. 
She purifies the world of the environment  by visualizing a golden ground with varied 
designs made of sapphires and so forth as an antidote to the impurities of stones, thorns, and so forth.  
The principal practice that will actualize this pure land is the practice of the six 
perfections.  
Appendix 8: Answer Key for Sample Quiz Questions  
 210 
Topic 11  Application in Skillful Means  
1. Briefly describe the application in skillful means.  
The application in skillful means is a bodhisattvas yoga of the pure grounds that 
spontaneously accomplishes enlightened activities free from coarse exertion. It directly 
knows the proper and improper time to benefit sentient beings.  
2. Name and describe one of the ten applications in skillful means. 
The application in skillful means that does not observe true existence is an application 
possessed by the exalted wisdom of a bodhisattva on the tenth ground that does not 
observe the entity of the self of persons and phenomena as truly existent. This is 
because the bodhisattva has familiarized with the emptiness door of complete 
liberation for many countless eons. 
3. Identify the four types of maras and when they are surpassed by Hinayana 
practitioners and Mahayana practitioners. 
The four types of maras are: 
(1) mara of the aggregates, 
(2) mara of afflictions, 
(3) mara of the lord of death, and 
(4) mara of sons-of-the-gods. 
Hinayana practitioners surpass the mara of the sons-of-the-gods upon attaining the 
Hinayana path of seeing. They surpass the mara of afflictions when they attain the 
state of a foe destroyer. When they are liberated from the two obstructions to 
absorption and liberation, they surpass the mara of the lord of death. When they have 
passed into nirvana-without-remainder, they surpass the mara of the aggregates. 
Mahayana practitioners surpass the mara of the sons-of-the-gods upon attaining a 
sign of irreversibility. Upon attaining the eighth ground, they surpass the coarse form of 
the other three maras. With the attainment of buddhahood, all maras are surpassed in their entirety.

Appendix 10: Meditations and Reflections  
Topic 1: Subject-Aspects of the Antidote Class  
Four close placement of mindfulness  
Reflection 1  
To meditate on the close placement of mindfulness on feelings, recall a past pleasant 
experience of the body, for example, receiving a really good body massage, having a nice meal, or listening to a good music performance. These are all examples of having pleasant physical feelings. See if you can identify the different causes and conditions that have to come together for this experience to arise.   This helps us to be mindful of the dependent arising nature of such feelings.   Also try to recall how long this experience lasts and what happens at the end. Does it just fade away or is it abruptly replaced by another feeling?   This helps us to be mindful of the impermanent nature of pleasant feelings.    Repeat the exercise but using different experiences  pleasant experience of the mind, unpleasant experience of the body, and so forth. Even neutral experiences can be considered. See if you can find one single experience/feeling that has remained unchanged and lasted forever.   This would be mindfulness of feelings in relation to their impermanent nature. 
16 attributes of the four noble truths 
Meditation on true origins and their four attributes  
Bring to mind a recent event when strong disturbing emotions arise in your mind, for example, being infatuated with someone or getting angry at someone. Lets take the case of strong jealousy. Try to remember your experience when you are jealous of another person. Examine what state of mind you have at that time, is it a peaceful one or a highly disturbed one? Would you say that it is a pleasant or an unpleasant experience?  
Appendix 10: Meditations and Reflections 
 223 
 Then examine what are your reactions to jealousy, what actions of body or 
speech do you commit under the influence of your jealousy? Do you lash out at someone or behave in some other unskillful ways? Can you see how this jealous 
mind is a source  or origin  that gives rise to unpleasant feelings and unskillful actions? 
Can you realize how it strongly produces  suffering? Moreover, can you also see that this 
jealousy and the reactions in its wake act as condition  for more suffering, like harming 
ones relationship with another or aggravating an already difficult relationship?  
 In this way, check to see if jealousy, being a disturbing emotion, has the four attributes of true origins.  This meditation can be repeated using different disturbing emotions and also unskillful physical and verbal actions.    A much more powerful way of meditating on true origins will be to become aware or mindful of these disturbing emotions immediately when they just arise. Then one will be able to observe firsthand the effects they have on ones body and mind and see clearly how they motivate oneself to act unskillfully. 
Meditation on true sufferings and their four attributes  
By definition many phenomena can be classified as true sufferings. However, in terms of practice or meditation, it is more meaningful to use ones own appropriated aggregates as the object for meditating on true sufferings. Among these five aggregates  body (form), feeling, discrimination, compositional factor and consciousness  body, being the grossest, is the easiest while consciousness, being so subtle, will be most difficult. 
Reflection 1 
Recollect how your body looks like and feels like when you are very young and in your minds eye track how it changes over the years until the present time. Then extrapolate and imagine how it will be as aging continues until you are in your 
eighties or nineties. Are you convinced that your body is impermanent  and ever-
changing? 
Reflection  2 
When we are healthy we are not aware of the suffering nature of our body. Try to 
recall times when you are hurt physically or when you are sick, and focus on how 
Appendix 10: Meditations and Reflections 
 224 
unpleasant it is, how much suffering arises in your body. In fact, if you are now 
sitting in meditation posture without moving it wont be long before you come face t o  f a c e  w i t h  p h y s i c a l  d i s c o m f o r t  o r  p a i n  t h a t  a r e  i n h e r e n t  i n  t h i s  b o d y  o f  y o u r s .  
Would you agree that your body is miserable  or suffering by nature? 
Reflection  3 
To reflect on the bodys empty  and selfless  nature, we want to examine if there is a 
body that exists as a concrete, independent entity, that somehow is able to stand on 
its own. We want to see if our usual commonsense notion of a body that is a single, 
enduring thing is true or not. To do so we look among the different parts of the body for the body.   Mentally go through all the parts of your body, from the hairs on your head down to the sole of your feet. See if you can point to one or several parts of your body and declare, This is my body.   If you fail to find it you may think that the collection of all these parts is the body. Lets see if we can find this collection. Surely if we find the collection we will find the body. Now, imagine picking up one part, say your thumb, and check if it is the collection of parts. If it isnt then put it aside. Do this for your ten fingers, ten toes, four limbs, torso and head. Can you find the collection?  If the body is not found among the parts or the collection of parts, maybe it is to be found elsewhere, outside of these parts. Can you point to something apart from the parts of the body, like a table, a chair or a lamp, and call that your body?  At any point of this analysis, when you experience a strong sense of the unfindability of this solid, concrete body, as if you have lost your body, set aside the analysis and let your mind rests in this experience for as long as possible. This is a way to familiarize our mind with the emptiness or selflessness of the body. [Note: This meditation is more from the point of view of Prasangika school]  
Meditation on true cessations and their four attributes   
Reflection  1 
If we are still ordinary beings who have not realized emptiness directly, we do not have true cessations. In other words, we have never experienced a true cessation from the beginningless past till now. However, we can meditate on true cessations by reflecting on a similitude of them, by looking at experiences that we do have which 
Appendix 10: Meditations and Reflections 
 225 
are slightly similar to a true cessation and in this way come to appreciate these four 
attributes.  A good similitude is when we have an experience of some physical pain and later manage to gain a relief from this pain through some methods, for example, having a headache and then, through taking some medication, the pain goes away. Or we experience pain from sitting in the meditation posture and when we relax this posture we experience some form of relief from the pain.   Can you see that by changing the conditions we attain a temporary cessation of physical suffering?   Can you determine that this cessation has the quality of freedom from pain, the quality of being something beneficial or good and to some extent, quite liberating   To conclude, can you arouse a strong aspiration to attain the actual true cessations from all sufferings, not just the physical sufferings, as well as the causes of sufferings? 
Reflection  2 
A similar meditation can be done focusing on mental suffering. This is more difficult to do. Recall a time/event when you moved from strong attachment/desire for someone or something to an experience of letting go of the clinging to him/her/it, usually due to the passage of time, or due to physical separation, or better still, the application of an antidote (like meditating on the disadvantages of attachment).   If you are blessed with such an experience, you can recall how it feels like initially, when your mind is filled with strong, compelling desire or clinging to this person, when you cant bear to be separated from the object of attachment. Then shift your attention to the latter experience when this desire cools, when the mind relinquishes its clinging and lets go of the object, when you may look back at your object of clinging and ask, why was I so caught up with it in the past? Is there a sense of relief, of letting go of a burden that has been weighing on your mind/heart?  
 See if you can focus on this experience and notice that in it there is (1) cessation  
of suffering and pain that comes from your attachment, (2) pacification  of 
attachment, a quenching of the fire of desire, (3) that this freedom from desire is something good and beneficial, something highly auspicious , and (4) that it is 
liberating , like regaining freedom after being bound for a long time? 
 Do you want to experience such cessations not just temporarily but permanently? 
 Can you arouse a strong aspiration to attain the actual true cessations from sufferings and the causes of sufferings? 
Appendix 10: Meditations and Reflections 
 226 
Topic 3: Excellent Qualities of the Applications  
You are surrounded by countless sentient beings, all of whom are suffering. So first 
meditate on the suffering of sentient beings. Put yourself into their place and feel their suffering. We tend to have a lot of resistance to pain and want to keep this on an intellectual level, but we cant develop genuine bodhichitta unless we are willing to take on others suffering. Rather lets try to really feel at least some of their physical and mental pain. Develop a genuine wish to free others from suffering.  With this motivation to free others from suffering, imagine that you are a high level bodhisattva that is practicing these applications. You are able to keep your mind focused as long as you want on whatever object you want. Your mind is so clear and focused, and it seems like you can stay in meditation forever. You can even easily analyze emptiness for as long as you wish. Think about how powerful a mind this is, and how easy it would be to attain realizations!   Now start to contemplate the excellent qualities of the applications. Go through the excellent qualities one by one, and really contemplate what it would be like to attain this excellent quality. For example with regard to the first, overcoming the strength of the maras, meditate on what it would be like to be completely free from afflictions. Contemplate how afflictions cause us so many problems  our anger, attachment, jealousy, pride, ignorance, etc. In fact, our afflictions cause all of our problems! Spend some time and imagine how it would feel to never have anger, never have attachment, jealousy, pride, etc. You have a completely peaceful mind all of the time.   Next, meditate on how much suffering we experience due to having this contaminated body that gets sick, hurt, weak, hungry, etc.  Imagine how all of this suffering would go away if we no longer had a contaminated body, that we would never again feel physical pain.   Continue in this way and meditate on those excellent qualities that really inspire you. Feel that it is possible to attain these excellent qualities, that we can  attain them. We all have buddha nature and we can attain all excellent qualities if we only practice consistently over time.  
Topic 4: Hindrances to the Applications  
Bring to mind some benefits of practicing the applications. That you will soon attain enlightenment and be free from suffering forever. However, there are some pitfalls that we need to avoid in order to continue to make progress along the path.   
Appendix 10: Meditations and Reflections 
 227 
Firstly, there are two hindrances related to self-confidence: attainment with great 
difficulty and sudden self-confidence. It is obvious how the first one, understanding the words and meaning of the perfection of wisdom with great difficulty and over a long period of time,  is a hindrance to ones practice. Think about how to avoid this, how to purify these obstacles and create the causes to be able to understand the perfection of wisdom more quickly.    Next, contemplate how the second hindrance, sudden self-confidence, can be a hindrance to ones practice.     How would you be able to avoid this hindrance?  Continue one by one through the other forty-six hindrances. For each hindrance, think about why it is a fault and how you could avoid it. If you have this fault, think about what you could do over time to eliminate this fault. Remember that we all have many of these faults, so it is important to have kindness towards ourselves and others for having them and not to get down on ourselves or others for having them. Merely resolve to practice as best as we can and be realistic about what we can accomplish.  
Topic 6:  Mahayana Partial Concordance with Liberation  
In this meditation, we will try to develop a practical understanding of the five factors that determine ones skill in achieving enlightenment  faith, effort, mindfulness, meditative stabilization, and wisdom. At this point, we probably dont have much strength in these factors, but as we progress along the path, the strength of these five factors will gradually increase. 
  For each of the five factors, reflect on its meaning and how you could determine and 
develop its strength in daily life.  
 Faith   m editate on what faith re ally me ans. Does it m ean that you be lieve in  
something blindly? Or can faith arise from logic or experience?  Reflect on some 
things in which you have faith. Do you have faith in doctors, scientists, buddhas? What does it mean to have faith in the three jewels and the mahayana paths and results? Why would it be beneficial to have strong faith in them? How could you develop your faith further? 
 Joyous effort   meditate on  what joyous effort really means. What are the things in 
your life that you are very happy to do, that bring you the most joy?  When you sit 
down and do your Dharma practice, what feelings arise? When you think about doing things for others, what feelings arise? What does it mean to have joyous effort 
Appendix 10: Meditations and Reflections 
 228 
in this context? Why would it be beneficial to have strong joyous effort? How could 
you increase your joyous effort? 
  
Continue in this way through the remaining factors  mindfulness , meditative  
stabilization , and wisdom . Think about what each of them really means and how they 
can be applied in daily life. Also, reflect on the benefits of developing each, thinking about how they could be helpful in your life. Finally, think about what things you could do to further develop each of these qualities.  Finish this meditation by generating a strong resolve to develop each of these five factors in your mind.  
Topic 7: Mahayana Partial Concordance with Definite Discrimination  
Reflection 1  Evenmindedness or equanimity  
Evenmindedness   bring to mind two people that you dont like or that irritate you. 
Examine the feelings and sensations that arise when you think about these people. Are they pleasant or unpleasant feelings? How does your body feel? Now examine why you dont like these people, or why you feel irritated. Is it because of something that they have done to you? If so, do you feel the same way when they do something similar to someone else? Have you always disliked these people, or was there a time when you felt differently?   Now bring to mind some people that are close to you and some strangers. Why are some of them your friends, while the others remain strangers? Are they so different from each other? Or is it because your friends have done nice things for 
you, while the strangers have not? Also, examine whether your friends have always 
been your friends, or have there been times when they were strangers or even people that you didnt like? Are the friends you had when you were growing up still your friends, or have some of them become strangers or people you dislike?   Since our friends, enemies, and strangers are changing all of the time, does it make sense to cling to our friends, have aversion to our enemies, and be indifferent to strangers? Does this make us suffer? 
 
Appendix 10: Meditations and Reflections 
 229 
Reflection 2  Minds of non-anger and non-harmfulness  
Bring to mind a time when you were really angry. Perhaps when someone insulted 
you or treated you unfairly. How did this feel? Was it a pleasant or unpleasant experience? Were you feeling in control, or did you feel controlled by the anger? Did you want to hurt someone? Were you able to care about others in this mind state? How did your anger affect others? Did others like to be around you when you are angry? How does anger affect our relationships with others?   Now bring to mind a time when you felt loving toward others. Did your mind feel tight or relaxed? Was this a pleasant  or unpleasant experience? Did you feel light 
or heavy? How did this affect others? Did they like to be around you when you were feeling loving?  
 
You can reflect similarly on some of the remaining types of minds generated on the path of preparation, those that you find particularly interesting or inspiring. 
General meditation on the meaning of conjoined  
In this meditation we will explore the meaning of being conjoined with. You may have noticed that many of the definitions given in the study manual contain these words (conjoined with), and here we will try to develop some experience of what this means.  Bring to mind someone for whom you feel great tenderness and protectiveness. It is easy to do this with a small puppy, kitten or baby rather than a partner or parent toward whom you have more complicated feelings. So bring to mind an image of this being, reflect on how sweet/cute/defenseless he/she is, and allow your heart to open and fill with loving feelings.  With this strong feeling of tenderness, imagine 
that a gang of kids are throwing rocks at this tiny defenseless being and you have a 
strong urge to rescue and protect him/her. This is compassion conjoined with love: your urge to protect this being from harm (compassion) is strongly influenced by the love you generated for him/her.  Now imagine that this puppy/kitten/baby becomes extremely agitated, screaming, kicking you and scratching at your face. Perhaps you now feel somewhat irritated. When irritation arises, what happens to the loving feeling? Can you feel both love and irritation at the same moment?  Is this irritation conjoined with, or strongly influenced by, love? Or do these mind states of irritation and love alternate, not existing simultaneously? 
Appendix 10: Meditations and Reflections 
 230 
 To conclude, lets contrast these two scenarios  the first when our compassion 
is conjoined with love, and the second when our irritation is not conjoined with 
love. Try to feel the difference between the mind being conjoined with and not 
being conjoined with. Notice that negative emotions such as anger and irritation cannot arise as long as our mind is conjoined with love.  
Topic 8 : Signs of irreversibility  
Reflection  1  
Sometimes the long list of signs of irreversibility may seem a bit daunting and it may seem unrelated to our life or practice. Therefore, it is very helpful to pick one or a few of these signs that are particularly meaningful to you in some ways and reflect on them in more details.   For example, regarding the sign that a bodhisattvas body will always be clean or his belongings etc. will always be clean, you can think about stories of teachers who are sick and yet their bodies never smell. In fact, they seem to give out a pleasant odor when other people would smell really bad if they are in similar situations. Try in different ways to make these signs meaningful to you. 
Reflection  2  
H.H. Dalai Lama has taught that we can infer the inner qualities of a spiritual teacher from the external manifestations in the behavior of the teacher, just as we can tell that there is a fish in the water by observing the presence of ripples on the water surface.   So lets spend some time reflecting on what sort of external manifestations in the body and speech of a person will lead us to infer the presence of some inner qualities or realizations. Consider the ten qualities of a Mahayana spiritual teacher. Choose one of them and reflect on how will this quality manifests externally.  
Topics 9, 10, and 11  
Reflection on the meaning of emptiness of true existence 
In this exercise we will try to get a feel for how we experience objects as existing truly and then try to loosen up this conception somewhat. We will use our right hand as an illustration.  
Appendix 10: Meditations and Reflections 
 231 
  
 First, look at your hand, taking in merely the general outline without examining it closely. You have a sense that the hand is over there, at the end of my arm. The h a n d  s e e m s  t o  b e  o v e r  t h e r e ,  c a s t i n g  i t s  a p p e a r a n c e  t o  m y  m i n d .  T h e n  m y  m i n d  thinks hand. It appears that the hand casts its appearance to my mind, causing my mind to label it hand. It seems as if the hand exists as a separate entity in and of itself, and the existence of the hand does not depend on my mind or anyone elses mind, that it is there, it exists completely independently of mind. Spend some time and try to get a clear sense of this, of how you actually perceive your hand.   The way you perceive your hand is mistaken. This is the reason we continue to circle in cyclic existence, because both the way that the hand appears and the way we think about the hand are mistaken. Because all phenomena appear mistakenly to our minds, and we grasp to them as existing in that way, thus generating more afflictions and karma.   Lets try to loosen that conception a bit. The hand appears to exist as an entity 
separate from the arm. It is obvious  this is the hand, that is the arm. Now try to find the spot where your hand ends and where your arm begins. Exactly where is the border between the hand and the arm?   Here you see that when we examine closely, it is very difficult to find the exact point where the hand ends and where the arm begins. The border is kind of fuzzy. In general, this is the hand and that is the arm, but when you try to find a precise 
dividing line, it is not so obvious. Why is one spot the hand and the spot right next 
to it the arm? Does it have anything to do with how the mind looks at them?   Now lets think back to the time when you were first conceived. Slowly you began to grow in the womb. At some point, you developed arms and legs and hands. At what point did your right hand first exist? What is the exact moment in which you first had a hand? Did your hand not exist the previous moment? Why?     A s  w e  c a n  s e e  f r o m  t h i s  e x e r c i s e ,  i t  i s  e a s y  t o  f i n d  a  h a n d  w h e n  y o u  d o n  t  r e a l l y  analyze.  However, the more closely you examine it, the more difficult it is to find. In this exercise we tried to find its exact boundaries. It is difficult to find where the hand ends and where the arm begins. It is also difficult to find the exact moment in which your hand first existed. This is because the hand does not exist truly; it does not exist from its own side without being posited by an awareness. Rather it exists conventionally; it comes into existence in dependence on the mind. Try to get a sense of the role the mind takes in positing the existence of the hand.

 This study guide, a Masters Program Materials Project, was compiled and written in 
2001 by Emily Hsu and Kok Wai Cheong, two students of the FPMT Masters Program at Istituto Lama Tzong Khapa, Pomaia, Italy, for the use of other students throughout the FPMT, especially in the FPMT Basic Program, to be able to study 
even one chapter of this most illuminating text, Maitreyas Ornament for Clear 
Realizations , one of Maitreyas five treatises.  
The work has been lightly edited and fine-tuned in August 2001 by Ven. Constance 
Miller, FPMT Education Services, for inclusion on the Second Edition August 2001 Basic Program CD-Rom. 
Extra appendices including sample quizzes with answer keys, discussion topics and 
suggestions for meditation were added in March 2003.   
All errors are the sole responsibility of the editor. May the benefits of this work 
come to fruition in the form of many, many realizations in the hearts and minds of sentient beings.  
 
©  FPMT, Inc., 2005 
All rights reserved.

1. This section was partly taken from Thurman, Robert. The Speech of Gold , pp. 
289. 
2. For more information on Maitreya, see Landaw and Weber, Images of 
Enlightenment . 
3. Jetsün Chökyi Gyältsän, Grounds and Paths . 
4. When these eight are referred to as eight clear realizations, the last one is called 
resultant wisdom body. This is related to a technical point  a clear realization 
is a consciousness and is, therefore, impermanent, whereas a resultant truth body is permanent and, thus, cannot actually be a clear realization. 
5. For more information on calm abiding and special insight, see Meditative States  
by Lati Rinpoche, Walking through Walls  by Geshe Gedün Lodrö, Calm Abiding 
and Special Insight  by Geshe Gedün Lodrö, or Meditation on Emptiness by Jeffrey 
Hopkins. 
6. In general, it is true that Mahayana mundane paths exist in the continua of 
ordinary beings. There is an exception with regard to a person who has realized emptiness directly on the Hinayana path prior to entering the Mahayana. In this case, this person is a superior (although not a Mahayana superior) yet still resides on a Mahayana mundane path.  
7. An inferential realization is one that is based on a correct sign, or reason. Thus, 
it is a conceptual consciousness. For more information about inferential 
cognizers, see Mind in Tibetan Buddhism  by Lati Rinpochay. 
8. More information on meaning generalities can be found in Mind in Tibetan 
Buddhism by Lati Rinpochay . 
9. From a commentary to Jetsün Chökyi Gyältsäns Necklace of Learned Explanation  
by Geshe Jampa Tegchok, Nalanda Monastery, France, 19857. Translated by Thubten Sherab Sherpa.  
10. From a commentary to Jetsün Chökyi Gyältsäns Necklace of Learned Explanation  
by Geshe Jampa Tegchok, Nalanda Monastery, France, 19857. Translated by Thubten Sherab Sherpa.

11. According to Prasangika, there are no acquired knowledge obstructions. The

39. Some scholars say that the final concentration is the fourth concentration, 
while others say that it is all four concentrations. According to Geshe Gedün 
Lodrö in Calm Abiding and Special Insight : The meditator begins with a mind of 
the Desire Realm, proceeds up to the eighth absorption then back down to the mind of the Desire Realm, and then up again to the fourth concentration. That fourth concentration receives the name final concentration.  
40. A never returner is a Hinayana superior who has suppressed all of the 
afflictions of the desire realm in dependence upon having developed an actual concentration. More information on never returners can be found in Hopkins,

46. The definition of an uninterrupted path  is an uncontaminated exalted wisdom that

47. The definition of a path of release  is an exalted wisdom of meditative equipoise 
that is free from the respective obstructions of the uninterrupted path that is its 
direct substantial cause. ( Ocean of Sport , p. 112). See the Paths and Grounds 
section for more details.

65. Maras are hindrances to practicing the Dharma. For more details, refer to the 
Explanation of the Four Maras described in topic 11. 
66. There are four nominal divisions of the perfection of wisdom: (1) the natural 
perfection of wisdom, (2) the scriptural perfection of wisdom, (3) the path perfection of wisdom, and (4) the resultant perfection of wisdom. The path perfection of wisdom refers to the path for realizing the resultant perfection of wisdom, omniscience.

69. An illustration of a certain phenomenon is that which is ascertained as the 
definition of that phenomenon before realizing it as the definiendum of that phenomenon.  
70. Abiding in a type of Hinayana realization denotes that the realization is a 
principal object of cultivation by Hinayana practitioners and has a similarity of observed object and aspect as that cultivated by them.

definendium given follows the commentary by Geshe Jampa Gyatso, which identifies this as a knowledge characteristic.

path of preparation, (3) path of seeing, (4) path of meditation, and (5) path of 
no-more-learning. 
77. Jetsün Chökyi Gyältsän, Ocean of Sport , vol. 2, p. 195. 
78. There are three kinds of wisdom: (i) wisdom arisen from hearing, (ii) wisdom 
arisen from reflecting, and (iii) wisdom arisen from meditating. On the path of accumulation, one primarily generates a wisdom arisen from hearing and a wisdom arisen from reflecting.

86. Jetsün Chökyi Gyältsän, Ocean of Sport , vol. 2, p. 243. 
87. The two mind generations refer to conventional and ultimate mind generation. 
Conventional mind generation, or mind of enlightenment, generally denotes the altruistic intention to achieve enlightenment for the sake of all sentient beings. Ultimate mind generation is the wisdom realizing emptiness of true existence. These two represent method and wisdom that are necessary for the attainment of enlightenment.  
88. Jetsün Chökyi Gyältsän, Ocean of Sport , vol. 2, p. 259. 
89. See Meditation on Emptiness , pp. 118-19 for more information on the bodies of a 
buddha. 
90. Ocean of Sport , vol. 2, p. 263. 
91. The three pure grounds are the eighth, ninth, and tenth bodhisattva grounds. 
According to Svatantrika, they are called pure grounds because at this point the bodhisattva can no longer have a manifest conception of true existence.  
92. According to Svatantrika, for something to exist truly or ultimately, it would 
have to exist by way of its own objective mode of subsistence without being posited by the force of appearing to a non-defective awareness. (See the brief explanation of true existence in the section explaining the tenets of the Yogachara-Svatantrika school, pp. 259.) In order for something to exist conventionally, it must be renowned in the world and not be refuted by a valid cognizer. For more information about ultimate and conventional existence, see 
The Two Truths  by Guy Newland. 
93. In this context, a seed is the potential for the conception of true existence to 
arise. 
 
Notes  244 
  
94. There is a difference between complete  path of action and path of action. There 
are four factors that make an individual karma into a complete path of action: 
object, intention, commission, and completion. The first, object , is for example, 
the being to be killed. The second, intention,  has three parts: recognition, 
motivation, and affliction. Recognition means that one must identify properly the object of ones action. Motivation is, for example, wanting to kill. Affliction 
is any of the three poisons (desire, anger, ignorance). The third, commission , is 
actually committing the act, and the fourth, completion , means that the action is 
brought to its conclusion, e.g., the other being dies.  
95. The five certainties are as follows: (1)  abode   abiding only in the highest pure 
land, Akanishta; (2) body  adorned with thirty-two signs and eighty marks; (3) 
retinue   surrounded by only bodhisattva superiors; (4) doctrine   teaching only 
the Mahayana doctrine; and (5) time  abiding until the end of cyclic existence. 
96. Ocean of Sport , vol. 2, p. 267. 
97. The five obscurations are harmful intent, desirous aspiration, lethargy and 
sleep, excitement and regret, and doubt. 
98. An emanation body is a fully developed form body that does not possess the 
five certainties. 
99. Jetsün Chökyi Gyältsän, Ocean of Sport , vol. 2, p. 271. 
100. See the first topic of Chapter Four for explanation of these practices. 
101. These definitions are taken from Jetsün Chökyi Gyältsän, Ocean of Sport , vol. 2. 
102. Boundaries indicate the levels of the path upon which these topics can exist. 
For example, the boundaries of the hindrances to an application (topic 4) are 
from prior to entering a path through the seventh ground. This means that the 
hindrances can occur for a trainee any time prior to achieving the eighth ground. The hindrances can no longer arise once the trainee has reached the eighth ground. Therefore, the boundaries are inclusive of the outer limits. 
103. To be technically accurate, the definiendum for the definition given here 
should be the yoga of path perfection of wisdom. 
104. According to Prasangika-Madhyamaka, the four noble truths can be realized 
prior to becoming a superior; they can be directly realized before the Mahayana path of seeing.
